The Six States of Cognitions in Logic: Conceptual and Assent-Based
Author enumerates three states of conceptual and three states of assent-based cognitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and explain the three states of conceptual cognitions and the three states of assent-based cognitions that logic investigates.

Core Idea
Logic studies both concepts and assents in three modes: as tools for discovery, as immediate prerequisites, and as remote prerequisites.

This passage from al-Qutbi's logic text presents a comprehensive map of the topics of logic. It divides cognitions into conceptual (تصوری) and assent-based (تصدیقی), each with three states: the actual conveyance to unknown knowledge, the direct prerequisites, and the indirect prerequisites. Understanding this map helps you see how definitions, the five universals, propositions, and syllogisms fit together.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
The author first divides cognitions into conceptual and assent-based. Note that each has three states. Read the opening: 'أحوال المعلومات التصورية التي يبحث عنها في المنطق ثلاثة أقسام' and later 'وأما أحوال المعلومات التصديقية التي يبحث عنها في المنطق فثلاثة أيضا'.
Remember the parallel structure.
2
Analyze the three states of conceptual cognitions
First state: conveyance to an unknown concept via definitions (complete/incomplete, description). Second state: direct prerequisites like the five universals (genus, differentia, etc.). Third state: indirect prerequisites like subject and predicate in propositions.
Examples: genus 'animal' and differentia 'rational' define 'man'.
3
Analyze the three states of assent-based cognitions
First state: conveyance to an unknown assent via syllogism, induction, example. Second state: direct prerequisites (propositions). Third state: indirect prerequisites (premises and conclusions).
Syllogism example: 'The world is changing; every changing thing is originated; therefore the world is originated.'
4
Compare the structures
Notice the parallel: conveyance, direct prerequisites, indirect prerequisites for both types. The difference lies in the nature of the cognition (conceptual vs assent-based) and the specific topics (definitions vs syllogisms).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحوال المعلومات التصورية التي يبحث عنها في المنطق ثلاثة أقسام : أحدها الإيصال إلى مجهول تصوري : إما بالكنه كما في الحد التام، وإما بوجه ما ذاتي أو عرضي كما في الحد الناقص والرسم التام والرسم الناقص؛ وذلك في باب التعريفات. عن الجنس - كالحيوان - والفصل - كالناطق - وهما معلومان تصوریان من حيث أنهما كيف يركبان ليوصل المجموع إلى مجهول تصوّري كالإنسان، وكما يبحث عن القضايا المتعدّدة، كقولنا : «العالم متغير وكل متغير محدث وهما معلومان تصديقيان من حيث أنهما كيف يؤلفان، فيصير المجموع قياساً موصلاً إلى مجهول تصديقي، كقولنا : «العالم محدث» وثانيها ما يتوقف عليه الإيصال إلى المجهول التصوري، توقفاً قريباً، ككون المعلومات التصورية كليّة وجزئية، وذاتية ،وعرضيّة، وجنساً وفضلاً وخاصة فإن الموصل إلى التصور يتركب من هذه الأمور، فالإيصال يتوقف على هذه الأحوال بلاواسطة، فذكر الجزئية هاهنا على سبيل الاستطراد والبحث عن هذه الأحوال في باب الكليات الخمس. وثالثها ما يتوقف عليه الإيصال إلى المجهول التصديقي توقفاً بعيداً ، أي بواسطة، ككون المعلومات التصورية موضوعات ومحمولات، والبحث عنها في ضمن باب القضايا. وأما أحوال المعلومات التصديقية التي يبحث عنها في المنطق فثلاثة أيضا : أحدها الإيصال إلى المجهول التصديقي - يقينياً كان أو غير يقيني، جازماً أو غير جازم - وذلك مباحث القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل التي هي أنواع الحجة. وثانيها مايتوقف عليه الإيصال إلى المجهول التصديقي توقفا قريباً، وذلك مباحث القضايا. و ثالثها ما يتوقف عليه الإيصال إلى المجهول التصديقي توقفا بعيداً - أي بواسطة، ككون المعلومات التصديقية مقدمات وتوالي، فإن المقدم والتالي قضيتان بالقوة القريبة من الفعل ؛ فهما معدودان في المعلومات التصديقية دون التصورية، بخلاف الموضوع والمحمول فإنّهما من قبيل التصورات (شريف).
The states of conceptual cognitions that are investigated in logic are three divisions: First: conveyance to an unknown conceptual cognition—either in essence as in a complete definition, or through some essential or accidental aspect as in an incomplete definition, complete description, and incomplete description; that is in the chapter on definitions. Regarding genus—like animal—and differentia—like rational—and they are two conceptual cognitions insofar as how they are combined to convey the whole to an unknown conceptual cognition like man, and similarly investigating multiple propositions, like our saying: 'The world is changing and every changing thing is originated,' and they are two assent-based cognitions insofar as how they are composed, so that the whole becomes a syllogism conveying to an unknown assent, like our saying: 'The world is originated.' Second: what conveyance to the unknown conceptual cognition depends on directly, such as conceptual cognitions being universal and particular, essential and accidental, and genus, differentia, and property, for what conveys to a conceptual cognition is composed of these things; so conveyance depends on these states immediately. The mention of particularity here is by way of digression, and investigation of these states is in the chapter on the five universals. Third: what conveyance to the unknown assent depends on remotely, i.e., indirectly, such as conceptual cognitions being subjects and predicates, and their investigation is within the chapter on propositions. As for the states of assent-based cognitions investigated in logic, they are also three: First: conveyance to the unknown assent—whether certain or uncertain, decisive or indecisive—and that is the subject of syllogism, induction, and example, which are types of proof. Second: what conveyance to the unknown assent depends on directly, and that is the subject of propositions. Third: what conveyance to the unknown assent depends on remotely—i.e., indirectly—such as assent-based cognitions being premises and conclusions, for the premise and conclusion are two propositions in potentia near to actuality; so they are counted among assent-based cognitions, not conceptual cognitions, unlike subject and predicate which are of the kind of conceptual cognitions (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage classifies the states of cognitions that logic investigates, dividing them into two main categories: conceptual and assent-based, each with three subdivisions. For conceptual cognitions, the first state is the actual conveyance (إيصال) to an unknown concept. This can occur through a complete definition (الحد التام) that gives the essence, or through incomplete definition (الحد الناقص) or complete/incomplete description (الرسم التام والناقص) that give a non-essential characterization. Examples: genus (e.g., 'animal') and differentia (e.g., 'rational') combine to define 'man'. The author also mentions that multiple propositions, like 'the world is changing; every changing thing is originated', form a syllogism conveying to a new assent. The second state is what conveyance depends on directly (قريباً) such as the properties of universality, particularity, essential, accidental, genus, differentia, and property—these are the five universals. The third state is what depends indirectly (بعيداً), such as conceptual cognitions serving as subjects and predicates in propositions. For assent-based cognitions, the first state is conveyance to an unknown assent through syllogism (قياس), induction (استقراء), and example (تمثيل) which are forms of argument (حجة). The second state is direct dependence, which is the study of propositions themselves. The third state is indirect dependence, such as premises and conclusions, which are assent-based in potency. The commentator al-Sharif notes that premises and conclusions are considered assent-based cognitions, not conceptual, unlike subject and predicate which are conceptual. This careful division shows how logic systematically addresses every aspect of cognition relevant to reasoning, from simple concepts to complex arguments.

Key Terms
الحد التام
الرسم التام
القياس
الاستقراء
التمثيل
المقدمات والتوالي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many states of conceptual cognitions are investigated in logic?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Logic investigates both conceptual and assent-based cognitions in three parallel states: conveyance, direct prerequisites, and indirect prerequisites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Take the concept 'الصلاة' (prayer) from Islamic law. Using the first state of conceptual cognitions, construct a complete definition (genus + differentia) for it. Then identify which of the five universals (second state) are involved.
?
2. Consider the assent-based cognition: 'الوضوء واجب لكل صلاة' (ablution is obligatory for every prayer). Identify whether this falls under the first, second, or third state of assent-based cognitions, and explain why.

Non-Reciprocity of Mutabaqa with Tadammun and Iltizam
Author states that conformity signification does not entail inclusion or implication, and vice versa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why conformity signification (mutabaqa) does not entail inclusion (tadammun) or implication (iltizam), and why the converse is also not true.

Core Idea
Not every word that names an essence automatically includes its parts or implies its concomitants.

In the study of signification, a word can signify its meaning in three ways: by conformity (the whole meaning), by inclusion (a part of the meaning), or by implication (a concomitant of the meaning). This passage clarifies that these modes are not mutually entailing. Conformity does not guarantee inclusion or implication, and inclusion or implication do not guarantee conformity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise definition and argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of signification
Recall that al-dalala al-mutabaqa (conformity) is when a word signifies its whole meaning; al-dalala al-tadammun (inclusion) is when it signifies a part of that meaning; and al-dalala al-iltizam (implication) is when it signifies something necessarily accompanying that meaning in the mind.
For example, the word 'house' by conformity signifies the whole house, by inclusion signifies 'wall' or 'roof', and by implication signifies 'owner'.
2
Understand why mutabaqa does not entail tadammun
The author gives the example of simple terms (al-basa'it), which have no parts. For a term like 'wujud' (existence), there are no constituent parts to be signified by inclusion. Therefore, conformity does not imply inclusion for such terms.
Simple terms are those whose meaning is not composed of more basic concepts.
3
Understand why mutabaqa does not necessarily entail iltizam
The author argues that it is not certain that every quiddity (mahiyya) has a mental concomitant that necessarily follows from it. Even if a quiddity has accompaniments, it is not guaranteed that those accompaniments are signified by implication for every listener.
For instance, 'human' has the concomitant 'risible', but not everyone immediately thinks of 'risible' when hearing 'human'. Implication depends on the certainty and universality of the connection.
4
Recognize that the converse is also not true
Inclusion or implication do not guarantee conformity. For example, the word 'wall' signifies by inclusion a part of a house, but does not by conformity signify the whole house. Similarly, a word may imply something without being conformable to it.
Thus, the relationships between these types of signification are not reciprocal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطابقة لا تستلزم التضمن والالتزام، ولا عكس قال : المطابقة لا تستلزم التضمّن كما في البسائط، وأما استلزامها الالتزام فغير متيقن، لأن وجود لازم ذهني لكل ماهيّة يلزم من تصوّرها تصوّره غير متيقن
Conformity does not entail inclusion and implication, nor the converse. He said: Conformity does not entail inclusion, as in simple terms. As for its entailing implication, it is not certain, because the existence of a mental concomitant for every quiddity such that from its conceptualization its conceptualization follows is not certain.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a brief assertion about the non-reciprocal relationship among the three types of signification: conformity (mutabaqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam). The author states that conformity signification does not entail inclusion. The example given is simple terms (basa'it), which have no parts. For a simple term like 'existence' (wujud), there are no parts to be signified by inclusion. Therefore, mutabaqa does not imply tadammun. Similarly, the author claims that mutabaqa does not necessarily entail implication, because it is not certain that every quiddity (mahiyya) has a mental concomitant that necessarily follows from it. Even if something is a concomitant, it may not be certain or universally known. For example, the quiddity 'human' may have various mental concomitants, but those are not guaranteed to be implied by the word 'human'. The converse is also not implied: inclusion or implication do not entail conformity. This is a foundational principle in the theory of signification.

Key Terms
البسائط (al-basāʾiṭ)
الماهية (al-māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Conformity signification does not automatically entail inclusion or implication, and vice versa, so each type must be evaluated independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Classification of Compound Expressions: Complete and Incomplete
Author classifies compound expressions into complete (tam) and incomplete, then subdivides complete into declarative and non-declarative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any compound expression as complete (tām) or incomplete (ghayr tām) and further distinguish declarative (khabar) from non-declarative (inshā') based on the criteria given.

Core Idea
A complete compound is one that you can stop on—it need not be new information, just a finished utterance.

In logic, the first step in analyzing statements is to separate complete expressions (sentences) from incomplete ones. This lesson shows you the two-part division, then how complete expressions split into truth-apt propositions versus non-truth-apt constructions like commands and wishes. The commentator, al-Sharīf, adds a crucial refinement: a complete compound does not require novelty, only completeness of benefit.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish complete from incomplete by the 'stop test'
Can the listener naturally stop after hearing it? If yes, it's complete; if no, it's incomplete.
This is the primary division: tām vs ghayr tām.
2
For complete expressions, test if it can be true or false
If the expression admits truth and falsehood, it is a declarative (khabar/qadiyya). If not, it is non-declarative (inshā').
Declaratives are the building blocks of arguments; non-declaratives are not truth-apt.
3
Analyze non-declaratives indicating a request
If the inshā' indicates a request by conventional indication, determine the speaker-addressee relation: superiority → command, humility → request/supplication, equality → entreaty.
The three sub-types depend on social hierarchy.
4
Identify non-declaratives not indicating a request
These fall under 'notification' (tanbīh): wishing, hoping, wonder, oath, call. Recognize them by their function.
They do not seek an action but express emotion or emphasis.
5
Note the commentator's clarification on 'complete benefit'
Al-Sharīf corrects a possible misunderstanding: 'complete benefit' does not mean new information; it means the utterance is self-sufficient and the listener can stop on it, even if the content is already known.
This preserves the completeness of known facts like 'the sky is above us'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما المركب فهو إما «تام - وهو الذي يصح السكوت عليه - أو «غير تام»: والتام إن احتمل الصدق والكذب فهو «الخبر» و«القضية» وإن لم يحتمل فهو «الإنشاء». فإن دلّ على طلب الفعل دلالة أولية - أي وضعيّة – فهو مع الاستعلاء «أمر» كقولنا: اضرب أنت ومع الخضوع «سؤال» و «دعاء» ومع التساوي «التماس». وإن لم يدلّ فهو «تنبيه» يندرج فيه «التمني» و «الترجي» و «التعجب» و «القسم» و «النداء». وأما غير التام: فهو إما «تقييدي» كـ الحيوان الناطق» وإما «غير تقييدي كالمركب من اسم وأداة، أو كلمة وأداة. أقول : لما فرغ من المفرد وأقسامه شرع في المركب وأقسامه، وهو إما تام أو غير تام : لأنه إما أن يصح السكوت عليه - أي يفيد المخاطب فائدة تامة ) (۱) الأظهر أن يقال : «لأنه إما أن يفيد المخاطب فائدة تامة ، أي يصح السكوت عليه» فيجعل «صحة السكوت عليه تفسيراً للفائدة التامة، حتى لا يتوهم أن المراد بـ «الفائدة التامة» الفائدة الجديدة التي تحصل للمخاطب من المركب التام، فيلزم أن لا يكون مثل السماء فوقنا» وغيره من الأخبار المعلومة للمخاطب- مركباً تاماً، إذ لا يحصل منه للمخاطب فائدة جديدة (شريف).
He said: As for the compound, it is either 'complete' - and it is that on which it is correct to stop - or 'incomplete'. The complete, if it admits truth and falsehood, then it is the 'declarative' (al-khabar) and 'proposition' (al-qadiyya). If it does not admit [truth and falsehood], then it is the 'non-declarative' (al-inshā'). If it indicates a request for an action by primary indication - i.e., conventional - then with superiority it is 'command', like our saying 'strike you'; with humility it is 'request' and 'supplication'; with equality it is 'entreaty'. If it does not indicate [a request], then it is 'notification' which includes 'wishing', 'hoping', 'wonder', 'oath', and 'call'. As for the incomplete, it is either 'restrictive', like 'the rational animal', or 'non-restrictive', like a compound of a noun and a particle, or a verb and a particle. I say: When he finished with the single word and its divisions, he proceeded to the compound and its divisions, and it is either complete or incomplete: because either it is correct to stop on it - i.e., it gives the addressee a complete benefit (fa'ida tāmma). (1) The most apparent is to say: 'Because either it gives the addressee a complete benefit, i.e., it is correct to stop on it' - making 'correctness of stopping on it' an interpretation of 'complete benefit', so that it is not imagined that what is meant by 'complete benefit' is the new benefit (fa'ida jadīda) that occurs to the addressee from the complete compound, for then expressions like 'the sky is above us' and other known statements would not be complete compounds, since no new benefit occurs to the addressee from them (al-Sharīf).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage classifies compound expressions (al-murakkab) into two main types: complete (tām) and incomplete (ghayr tām). A complete compound is one on which it is correct to stop (i.e., it forms a meaningful utterance that satisfies the listener). The complete is further divided into: (1) declarative (khabar) or proposition (qadiyya), which admits truth and falsehood, and (2) non-declarative (inshā'), which does not admit truth and falsehood (e.g., commands, requests). The inshā' itself is subdivided according to whether it indicates a request for an action by primary (conventional) indication. If it indicates a request, then depending on the relative status of the speaker and addressee, it is either a 'command' (amr) if with superiority, 'request' or 'supplication' (su'āl/du'ā') if with humility, or 'entreaty' (iltimās) if with equality. If it does not indicate a request, it falls under 'notification' (tanbīh), which includes wishing (tamannī), hoping (tarajjī), wonder (ta'ajjub), oath (qasam), and call (nidā'). The incomplete compound is either 'restrictive' (taqyīdī), like 'the rational animal', or 'non-restrictive' (ghayr taqyīdī), like a combination of a noun and a particle or a verb and a particle. The commentator adds a clarification: the definition of 'complete' as 'correct to stop on it' should be understood as giving a complete benefit, but not necessarily new information. This is to avoid the misunderstanding that a known statement like 'the sky is above us' would not be considered complete because it offers no new benefit. In fact, it is complete because the listener can stop on it; the benefit is the completeness of the utterance, not novelty.

Key Terms
تام
خبر
إنشاء
تقييدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Entreaty
D) Wishing

Key Takeaway
Compound expressions are classified as complete (stop-worthy) or incomplete; among complete, only declaratives are truth-apt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Given the following expressions, classify each as complete or incomplete, and if complete, as declarative or non-declarative (specify the sub-type if applicable): (a) 'Zayd is standing' (زید قائم), (b) 'O Allah, forgive me' (اللهم اغفر لي), (c) 'rational animal' (الحيوان الناطق), (d) 'if Zayd comes' (إن جاء زید).
?
2. Why is the phrase 'the sky is above us' considered a complete compound according to al-Sharīf, even though it offers no new information? Explain in your own words.

Complete and Incomplete Compounds: The Foundations of Logical Statements
Author defines murakkab tamm and nāqiṣ, then divides tamm into khabar and inshā'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a complete compound (murakkab tamm) from an incomplete one (murakkab nāqiṣ) and classify complete compounds as khabar (statement) or inshā' (construction).

Core Idea
The complete compound is the basic unit of logical statements; knowing its types is essential for evaluating truth claims in Islamic sciences.

In this passage, the author defines the complete compound (murakkab tamm) by the criterion of 'being valid to be silent upon' (ṣiḥḥat al-sukūt). He then divides the complete compound into statement (khabar), which admits truth and falsehood, and construction (inshā'), which does not. This foundational classification is critical for understanding how propositions function in logic and Islamic disciplines like 'ilm al-ma'ānī and uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion of ṣiḥḥat al-sukūt
A compound is complete if it makes sense to stop speaking after uttering it without expecting another word. Example: 'Zayd is standing' vs. just 'Zayd'.
2
Identify the two types of compounds
Complete (tamm) and incomplete (nāqiṣ). The incomplete requires another word.
3
Distinguish khabar from inshā'
Among complete compounds, those that can be true or false are khabar (propositions); those that cannot are inshā' (e.g., commands, questions).
4
Apply the classification to Arabic sentences
Given a sentence like 'Allah is one' (khabar) or 'Do not be unjust' (inshā'), classify it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يكون حينئذ مستتبعاً للفظ آخر ينتظره المخاطب - كما إذا قيل : «زيد» فيبقى المخاطب منتظراً لأن يقال «قائم» أو «قاعد» مثلاً، بخلاف ما إذا قيل : «زيد قائم» - وإما أن لا يصح السكوت عليه : فإن صح السكوت عليه فهو المركب التام» وإلا فهو «المركب الناقص» و «غير التام». والمركب التام إما أن يحتمل الصدق والكذب و هو «الخبر» و «القضية» أولا يحتمل، وهو «الإنشاء». فإن قيل: الخبر إما أن يكون مطابقاً للواقع أو لا، فإن كان مطابقاً للواقع لم يحتمل الكذب، وإن لم يكن مطابقاً لم يحتمل الصدق، فلاخبر داخل في الحد. فقد يجاب عنه بأن المراد بالواو الواصلة أو الفاصلة بمعنى أن الخبر هو الذي يحتمل الصدق والكذب، فكل خبر صادق يحتمل الصدق، وكـلّ (1) هذا تفسير أيضاً لصحة السكوت، إذ فيه نوع إبهام أيضاً، كأنه قال : المراد بصحة سكوت المتكلّم على المركب أن لا يكون ذلك المركب مستدعياً للفظ آخر استدعاء المحكوم عليه للمحكوم به أو بالعكس، فلايكون المخاطب حينئذ منتظراً للفظ آخر كانتظاره للمحكوم به عند ذكر المحكوم عليه، وانتظار المحكوم عليه عند ذكر المحكوم به ; وقد أشار إلى أن المراد بـ «الاستتباع» أي الاستدعاء. وبـ«الانتظار» المنفيين ماذكره بقوله : كما إذا قيل : زيد... الخ. وحينئذ لا يتجه أن يقال : يلزم أن لا يكون مثل «ضرب زيد» مركباً تاماً، لأن المخاطب منتظر إلى أن يبين المضروب ويقال «عمراً» إلى غير ذلك من القيود - كالزمان والمكان (شريف). خبر كاذب يحتمل الكذب؛ فجميع الأخبار داخلة في الحد. وهذا الجواب غير مرضي، لأنّ الاحتمال لا معنى له حينئذ، بل يجب أن يقال إما صدق أو كذب». والحق في الجواب أن المراد احتمال الصدق والكذب بمجرد النظر إلى مفهوم الخبر، ولاشك أن قولنا «السماء (1) يعني إذا جرد النظر إلى مفهوم المركب ويقطع النظر عن خصوصية المتكلّم، بل عن خصوصيّة ذلك المفهوم، وينظر إلى محصل مفهومه وماهيته كان عند العقل محتملاً للصدق والكذب. فلايرد أن خبر الله تعالى وكذا خبر رسوله لا يحتمل الكذب، لأنا إذا قطعنا النظر عن خصوصية المتكلّم ولاحظنا محصل مفهوم ذلك الخبر وجدناه إما ثبوت شيء لشيء أو سلبه عنه، وذلك يحتمل الصدق والكذب عند العقل؛ وكذا لا يرد أن مثل قولنا : الكل أعظم من الجزء» وغيره من البديهيات التي يجزم العقل بها عند تصوّر طرفيها مع النسبة لا يحتمل عنده الكذب أصلاً، بل هو جازم بصدقه وحاكم بامتناع كذبه قطعاً، لأنا إذا قطعنا النظر عن خصوصية تلك البديهيات ونظرنا إلى محصول مفهوماتها وماهياتها وجدناه إما ثبوت شيء لشيء أوسلبه عنه ; وذلك يحتمل الصدق والكذب عند العقل بلااشتباه. والحاصل أن الخبر ما يحتمل الصدق والكذب عند العقل نظراً إلى ماهية مفهومه مع قطع النظر عما عداها ، حتى عن خصوصية مفهوم ذلك الخبر، وحينئذ فلا إشكال في أن الأخبار بأسرها محتملة للصدق والكذب.
And it (the compound) is not then requiring another word that the addressee awaits - as when it is said: 'Zayd', so the addressee remains waiting for it to be said 'standing' or 'sitting' for example, unlike when it is said: 'Zayd is standing'. And if it is not valid to be silent upon it: then if it is valid to be silent upon it, it is the complete compound, and if not, it is the incomplete compound and the non-complete. And the complete compound either admits truth and falsehood, and it is the statement and the proposition, or it does not admit them, and it is the construction. If it is said: the statement is either corresponding to reality or not, if it corresponds it does not admit falsehood, and if it does not correspond it does not admit truth, so no statement falls under the definition. It is answered that what is meant by the inclusive or exclusive disjunction is that the statement is that which admits truth and falsehood, so every true statement admits truth, and every false statement admits falsehood; so all statements are included in the definition. This answer is unsatisfactory, because possibility then has no meaning, but rather it must be said that it is either true or false. The correct answer is that what is meant is the possibility of truth and falsehood merely by looking at the concept of the statement, and there is no doubt that our saying 'The sky' (meaning if the concept of the compound is stripped and one cuts off consideration of the particularity of the speaker, rather the particularity of that concept, and one looks at the gist of its concept and its essence, it is in the intellect possibilizing truth and falsehood). So it is not objected that the statement of Allah and likewise the statement of His messenger do not admit falsehood, because when we cut off consideration of the particularity of the speaker and we consider the gist of the concept of that statement, we find it either an affirmation of something of something or a negation of it, and that admits truth and falsehood in the intellect. Likewise it is not objected that like our saying 'The whole is greater than the part' and other self-evident truths which the intellect judges with certainty when its two terms and the relation are conceptualized do not admit falsehood at all, rather it is certain of its truth and judges the impossibility of its falsehood absolutely, because when we cut off consideration of the particularity of those self-evident truths and we look at the gist of their concepts and their essences, we find it either an affirmation of something of something or a negation of it; and that admits truth and falsehood in the intellect without confusion. The conclusion is that the statement is that which admits truth and falsehood in the intellect by considering the essence of its concept while cutting off consideration from everything else, even from the particularity of the concept of that statement, and then there is no problem that all statements are possibilizing truth and falsehood.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the complete compound (murakkab tamm) and its division into statement (khabar) and construction (inshā'), and then defends the definition of khabar against objections. The author begins by explaining that a compound is complete if one can validly be silent upon it without expecting another word — e.g., 'Zayd is standing' vs. just 'Zayd'. A complete compound that admits truth and falsehood is a statement (khabar/proposition); one that does not is a construction (inshā'). An objection is raised: every actual statement is either true or false, so it cannot possibly be both. The author first gives an unsatisfactory answer (using inclusive/exclusive disjunction) and then provides the correct answer: possibility here refers to the conceptual essence of the statement, stripped of all particularities (of the speaker, the content, etc.). Thus even statements from Allah or self-evident truths like 'The whole is greater than the part' are, in their pure concept, neutral possibilities for truth or falsehood. The key is to consider the mahiyya (essence) of the proposition, not its actual instances.

Key Terms
المركب التام
المركب الناقص
الخبر
الإنشاء

B) 'Zayd is standing'
C) 'the book of'
D) 'if you study'

The classification of compounds into complete/incomplete and statement/construction is the first step in analyzing any utterance for logical content.

Refining the Definition of Khabar: Potential Truth and Falsehood in Conceptual Essence
Author defends the definition of khabar as admitting truth and falsehood by refining it to potentiality in the conceptual essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of khabar as 'admitting truth and falsehood' must be understood in terms of potentiality based on the conceptual essence, and apply this to problematic cases like divine statements and necessary truths.

Core Idea
The potential for truth and falsehood in a statement is grounded in its conceptual structure, not in its actual instances.

The simple definition of khabar as 'admitting truth and falsehood' faces an objection: every actual statement is either true or false, so it cannot admit both. The author presents a weak answer and then the correct one: the admission is based on the mere concept of the proposition, stripped of all particularities (speaker, content, etc.). This allows even divine statements and self-evident truths to fit the definition, because from the perspective of the conceptual essence, any affirmation or negation is potentially true or false.

Guided Path
1
State the objection to the definition of khabar
If a statement is true, it does not admit falsehood; if false, not truth. So no statement can be said to admit both.
2
Recognize the weakness of the first answer
The answer that a true statement admits truth and a false admits falsehood makes 'admit' trivial and is dismissed by the author.
3
Understand the correct answer
Possibility of truth/falsehood is considered at the level of the mere concept of the statement, disregarding all particularities.
4
Apply to two problematic cases
Divine statements and necessary truths: their conceptual essence is an attribution, so they admit potential truth/falsity.

Resolving the Circularity Objection in Defining Khabar
Author presents the famous circularity objection and answers by redefining truth/falsehood as correspondence of the assertive relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity objection in defining khabar by truth and falsehood and explain how redefining truth/falsehood as correspondence of the assertive and abstractive relations resolves it.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood can be defined without reference to khabar by focusing on the assertive relation.

In defining a statement (khabar) as that which is capable of truth and falsehood, a classic circularity objection arises because truth and falsehood themselves are defined by reference to the statement. The commentator al-Sharif al-Jurjani offers a precise solution by distinguishing between the assertive and abstractive relations. This lesson will walk through the objection and its resolution, sharpening your understanding of how definitions in logic must avoid circularity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the circular dependency
The definition of khabar uses truth/falsehood. But truth is defined as correspondence of khabar to reality. So truth depends on khabar, yet khabar is defined by truth. That is a vicious circle.
Circularity makes the definition useless.
2
Pinpoint the problematic assumption
The circularity assumes that truth/falsehood can only be defined via khabar. The commentator challenges this assumption by proposing an alternative definition of truth/falsehood independent of khabar.
If truth/falsehood can be defined differently, the circle breaks.
3
Introduce the two relations
The solution distinguishes the assertive relation (nisba iqāʿiyya) and the abstractive relation (nisba intizāʿiyya). Truth is correspondence of these relations to reality, not of the whole statement.
The assertive relation is the affirmation/negation link; the abstractive relation is the mind's abstraction of that link.
4
Verify the break in circularity
Now truth/falsehood are defined without mentioning khabar, only the relations. Thus the definition of khabar as capable of truth/falsehood is no longer circular.
The definition becomes: khabar is a statement such that its assertive/abstractive relation may correspond or not correspond to reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا سؤال مشهور : وهو أن تعريف الخبر باحتمال الصدق والكذب يستلزم الدور، لأنّ الصدق مطابقة الخبر للواقع، والكذب عدم مطابقة الخبر للواقع. والجواب أن ذلك إنما يرد على من فسر الصدق والكذب بماذكرتم، وأما إذا فسر «الصدق» بمطابقة النسبة الايقاعية والانتزاعية للواقع، و«الكذب» بعدم مطابقتهما للواقع فلاورود له أصلاً (شريف).
And here is a famous question: which is that defining the statement by possibility of truth and falsehood necessitates circularity, because truth is the correspondence of the statement to reality, and falsehood is the non-correspondence of the statement to reality. The answer is that this only applies to one who interprets truth and falsehood as you mentioned, but if 'truth' is interpreted as the correspondence of the assertive and abstractive relation to reality, and 'falsehood' as the non-correspondence of the two to reality, then there is no objection at all.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage addresses a famous circularity objection: if khabar is defined by possibility of truth/falsehood, and truth/falsehood are defined by khabar (truth is correspondence of khabar to reality), then the definition is circular. The author's answer is that this objection only applies if one defines truth and falsehood in terms of khabar. Instead, truth and falsehood can be defined as correspondence or non-correspondence of the 'assertive relation' (al-nisba al-īqāʿiyya) and 'abstractive relation' (al-nisba al-intizāʿiyya) to reality, independently of khabar. Thus the circularity is avoided.

Key Terms
الدور
النسبة الإيقاعية
النسبة الانتزاعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the circularity objection in defining khabar by truth and falsehood?
A) He redefines truth and falsehood as correspondence of the assertive and abstractive relation to reality
B) He asserts that the definition is not circular
C) He rejects the definition entirely
D) He adds a condition to the definition

Key Takeaway
Circularity in definition can be avoided by ensuring that the definiens (truth/falsehood) is grounded in concepts independent of the definiendum (khabar).

Defining Khabar and Insha' and Classifying Request Insha'
Author defines khabar and insha', then classifies insha' into request of action and tanbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between khabar and insha' and classify request insha' utterances as amr, iltimas, or su'al/du'a based on the speaker's status.

Core Idea
Insha' performs an action; its truth-value is irrelevant.

This lesson introduces the foundational division of complete utterances into informative (khabar) and performative (insha') in Islamic logic. Understanding this division is essential for analyzing the logical and legal status of statements in Islamic texts, especially commands and requests.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of complete utterances
Read the first sentence: 'The complete utterance, if it admits truth and falsehood according to its meaning, is khabar; otherwise it is insha'.' This is the basic dichotomy.
2
Understand the criterion for khabar
Khabar is defined by the possibility of being true or false by its meaning. Example: 'Zakat is obligatory' can be true or false.
3
Understand the criterion for insha'
Insha' does not admit truth or falsehood; it performs an action when uttered. Example: 'Give zakat' does not have a truth value.
4
Classify request insha'
If the insha' indicates a request by conventional indication, it is divided into three based on the speaker's status: superiority (amr), equality (iltimas), or submission (su'al/du'a).
Non-request insha' also exists, like tanbih (caution).
5
Apply the classification
Given a request utterance, determine the speaker's relative status to classify it correctly. For example, Allah's commands are amr, a peer's request is iltimas, a subject's request to a ruler is su'al.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمحصل التقسيم أنّ المركب التام إن احتمل الصدق و الكذب بحسب مفهومه فهو «الخبر» وإلا فهو «الإنشاء». وهو إما أن يدلّ على طلب الفعل دلالة أولية - أي وضعية - أو لا يدل، فإن دلّ على طلب الفعل دلالة وضعية : فإما أن يقارن الاستعلاء أو يقارن التساوي أو يقارن الخضوع : فإن قارن الاستعلاء فهو أمر». وإن قارن التساوي فهو «التماس». وإن قارن الخضوع فهو «سؤال» أو «دعاء». وإنما قيد «الدلالة بالوضع» احترازاً عن الأخبار الدالة على طلب الفعل لا بالوضع ، فإنّ قولنا : كتب عليكم الصلاة»، أو «أطلب منك الفعل» دال على طلب الفعل، لكنه ليس بموضوع لطلب الفعل، بل للإخبار عن طلب الفعل. (۱) اعترض عليه بأن الكلام في تقسيم الإنشاء، فلا تكون تلك الأخبار داخلة في مورد القسمة، فكيف يخرج بتقييد الدلالة بالوضع. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن المراد الاحتراز عن تلك الأخبار إذا استعملت في طلب الفعل بطريق الإنشاء على سبيل المجاز، فتكون داخلة في الإنشاء، لكن دلالتها على المعنى الإنشائي مجازية فلا تعد أمراً، لأن الفاظها في الأصل أخبار، وإن كان معانيها في هذا الاستعمال طلباً (شريف).
The summary of the division is that the complete utterance, if it admits truth and falsehood according to its meaning, is 'khabar'; otherwise it is 'insha'. And it either indicates a request for action by primary indication—i.e., by convention—or it does not. If it indicates a request for action by conventional indication, then either it is accompanied by superiority, or by equality, or by submission. If accompanied by superiority, it is 'amr'. If accompanied by equality, it is 'iltimas'. If accompanied by submission, it is 'su'al' or 'du'a'. He restricted 'indication by convention' as a precaution regarding reports that indicate a request for action not by convention, for our saying 'Prayer is prescribed for you' or 'I request the action from you' indicates a request for action, but it is not conventionally placed for requesting action, rather for informing about requesting action. (1) An objection was raised against this: The discussion is about the division of insha', so those reports are not included within the scope of the division; so how can they be excluded by restricting indication by convention? It can be answered that the intention is to guard against those reports when they are used for requesting action by way of insha' metaphorically, so they become included in insha', but their indication of the insha'i meaning is metaphorical, so they are not counted as amr, because their words are originally khabar, although their meanings in this usage are request (Sharif).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage begins by summarizing the division of complete utterances into two types: informative (khabar) and performative (insha'). Khabar is an utterance that admits truth or falsehood by its very meaning, while insha' does not. Then the author classifies insha' based on whether it indicates a request for action by primary conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya). If it does, it falls into three subcategories depending on the social relationship: if the speaker is superior, it is a command (amr); if equal, it is a plea (iltimas); if inferior, it is a request (su'al) or supplication (du'a'). The author then clarifies why he specified 'conventional indication': to exclude utterances that report a request (like 'Prayer is prescribed for you') which imply a request but are not conventionally used to make a request. An objection is raised: since the division is about insha', such reports are not even part of the division, so why mention them? The answer is that these reports, when used metaphorically to perform a request (e.g., saying 'I ask you to do it' as a direct request), become insha', but their indication is metaphorical, so they are not counted as amr. This shows the precision in distinguishing literal insha' from metaphorical uses.

The Role of Dalala Wad'iyya and the Objection Regarding Metaphorical Insha'
Author defines khabar and insha', then classifies insha' into request of action and tanbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author restricts 'indication by convention' (dalala wad'iyya) in defining request insha', and how the objection about metaphorical uses is resolved.

Core Idea
Only utterances that conventionally indicate a request are insha'; reports of requests remain khabar unless used metaphorically.

The passage includes a subtle refinement: the author adds 'by conventional indication' to exclude utterances that imply a request but are not conventionally used to make a request. An objection and answer clarify this further, showing the precision of Islamic logical analysis. This lesson focuses on that precision and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the restriction
The author says the insha' must indicate a request 'by primary indication - i.e., by convention (dalala wad'iyya)'. Locate this in the Arabic: 'دلالة أولية - أي وضعية'.
2
Understand why the restriction is needed
Without it, sentences like 'I ask you to do X' would be considered request insha', but they are actually khabar reporting a request. The restriction excludes such reports from the literal definition.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector says: Since the division is about insha', reports of requests are not part of the division, so why exclude them? The answer is that they could be used metaphorically as insha'.
The objection is a logical one: if the scope is already insha', there is no need to exclude khabar from it.
4
Evaluate the answer's logic
The answer shows that when a khabar sentence is used to perform a request, it becomes insha' but its indication is metaphorical, not conventional. Therefore, it does not fall under the literal classifications of amr etc. This preserves the precision of the classification.

Classification of Istifham as Tanbih: Definitions and Debate
Author addresses objection that istifham and nahy are not included in the division of insha'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a performative utterance like istifham should be classified under tanbih or amr by analyzing its conventional indication regarding action.

Core Idea
Classification hinges on what the utterance conventionally requests: action or mere notification.

In this passage, the author defends his classification of istifham (interrogation) under tanbih (notification) against objections. The debate turns on whether istifham conventionally indicates a request for action. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly categorizing performative utterances in logic. We will trace the dialectic and identify the key definitions at play.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection
The objector claims istifham and nahy are excluded from the division. For istifham, because it seeks information from the addressee (استعلام ما في ضمير المخاطب), not notifying something from the speaker (تنبيه على ما في ضمير المتكلم). Note the definitions used: istifham = seeking info; tanbih = notifying.
This sets up the classification problem.
2
Find the response to the main objection
The author responds that istifham, although it requests understanding (fahm), does not conventionally request action; so it falls under tanbih. The key is distinguishing request for understanding from request for action. The definition of tanbih is 'that which does not indicate a request for action by convention.'
3
Analyze the counter-objection about action
The objector then argues that understanding (fahm) is considered an action in linguistic custom (heart action), and that the real request is tafhīm (causing understanding), which is undeniably an action. This challenges the classification.
The debate shifts to the definition of fi'l (action).
4
Examine the final counter-response
The defender replies that tafhīm is not an action of the limbs, and the default meaning of 'action' when unqualified is limb action. So istifham does not request a limb action, preserving its status as tanbih.
Outcome depends on the scope of the term 'action' in conventional usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : الاستفهام والنهي خارجان عن القسمة : أما الاستفهام : فلأنه لا يليق جعله من التنبيه، لأنه استعلام ما في ضمير المخاطب، لاتنبيه على ما في ضمير المتكلم، وأما النهي : فلعدم دخوله تحت الأمر - لأنه دال على طلب الترك لاعلى طلب الفعل، لكن المصنف أدرج الاستفهام تحت التنبيه ولم يعتبر المناسبة (۱) قيل عليه : كيف يصح إدراجه في التنبيه مع أن الاستفهام دال على طلب الفعل - دلالة وضعية - والتنبيه ما لايدل على طلب الفعل - دلالة وضعيّة. وأجيب : بأن الاستفهام وإن دلّ بالوضع على طلب الفهم، لكنه لا يدل بالوضع على طلب الفعل، فلا يندرج في القسم الأول الذي هو الدال بالوضع على طلب الفعل، بل يندرج في التنبيه الذي هو ما لا يدل على طلب الفعل دلالة وضعية. ولقائل أن يقول : الفهم وإن لم يكن فعلاً بحسب الحقيقة – بل هو انفعال أو كيف - لكنه يعد في عرف اللغة من الأفعال الصادرة عن القلب، والمتبادر الألفاظ معانيها المفهومة عنها بحسب اللغة، فيصدق على الاستفهام أنه يدلّ بالوضع على طلب الفعل فلا يندرج في التنبيه ؛ وأيضاً : المطلوب بالاستفهام من المخاطب هو تفهيم المخاطب للمتكلم - لا الفهم الذي هو فعل المتكلّم - والتفهيم فعل بلا اشتباه (ن : لا اشتباه فيه فيلزم ماذكرناه. فإن قلت : التفهيم ليس فعلاً من أفعال الجوارح، والمتبادر من لفظ الفعل إذا أطلق هو الأفعال الصادرة عن الجوارح.
And one may say: Istifham (interrogation) and nahy (prohibition) are excluded from the division. As for istifham, because it is not appropriate to make it part of tanbih, since it is an inquiry into what is in the mind of the addressee, not a notification of what is in the mind of the speaker. As for nahy, because it does not fall under amr—since it indicates a request for omission, not a request for action. But the author included istifham under tanbih and did not consider the appropriateness. (1) It was said against this: How can it be validly included in tanbih when istifham indicates a request for action—by conventional indication—and tanbih is what does not indicate a request for action by conventional indication? And it was answered: Although istifham indicates by convention a request for understanding, it does not indicate by convention a request for action, so it does not fall under the first category which indicates a request for action by convention; rather it falls under tanbih, which is what does not indicate a request for action by conventional indication. And one may say: Understanding, even if not an action in reality—rather it is a passive state or quality—is considered in linguistic custom as an action emanating from the heart, and the primary understanding of words is their meanings understood from them according to language, so it is true of istifham that it indicates by convention a request for action, so it is not included in tanbih. Also, what is requested by istifham from the addressee is causing the addressee to understand the speaker—not the understanding which is the action of the speaker—and causing understanding is an action without ambiguity. If you say: Causing understanding is not an action of the limbs, and the primary meaning of the word 'action' when unqualified is actions emanating from the limbs.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that istifham (interrogation) and nahy (prohibition) are not properly included in the earlier division of insha'. The objector argues: Istifham is not fitting as tanbih because it seeks to know what is in the addressee's mind, not to notify what is in the speaker's mind. Nahy does not fall under amr because it requests omission, not action. However, the author (of the matn) had included istifham under tanbih without considering suitability. A further objection is raised: How can istifham be included in tanbih when istifham conventionally indicates a request for action (understanding), whereas tanbih is defined as not indicating a request for action? The answer is that istifham conventionally indicates a request for understanding (al-fahm), not a request for action. Understanding is not an action in reality (it is a passion or quality), but linguistically it is considered an act of the heart. So istifham does not indicate a request for action in the conventional sense, thus it falls under tanbih. But then another objection follows: Even if understanding is not an action in reality, it is considered an action in linguistic custom, and the primary import of words is their conventional meanings, so istifham does indicate a request for action, removing it from tanbih. Additionally, what is requested by istifham is the addressee's causing understanding (tafhīm), which is undoubtedly an action. The passage ends with a possible counter: tafhīm is not an action of the limbs, and the term 'action' unqualified primarily refers to limb actions. This dialectical exchange shows the complexity of classifying performatives and the importance of clear definition of terms like 'action' (fi'l).

Key Terms
استفهام
تنبيه
فعل

A) Whether it indicates a request for action by convention.
B) Whether it seeks information from the addressee.
C) Whether it is a command or prohibition.
D) Whether it involves understanding.

Key Takeaway
The classification of a performative as tanbih or talabi hinges on whether it conventionally indicates a request for action.

Defining the Proposition: Genus and Differentia
Author defines proposition with genus and differentia, and discusses tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia of a proposition (qadiyya), distinguish between spoken and mental propositions, and explain the two senses of tasdiq (assent).

Core Idea
A proposition is a statement that can be true or false – this differentia separates it from all other utterances.

In this passage, the author defines the proposition (qadiyya) by first giving its genus (statement, qawl) and then its differentia (the possibility of truth or falsehood). He then expands on the distinction between spoken and mental propositions, and clarifies the concept of tasdiq (assent). This definition is foundational for all subsequent logical reasoning about propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the Arabic term for proposition (القضية) and note that it is the subject of the entire definition.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is القول (statement). It is defined as a composite utterance (spoken) or mental concept. This genus includes both complete and incomplete statements.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is the phrase: 'it is correct to say of its speaker that he is truthful in it or lying'. This excludes incomplete statements (like phrases) and non-indicative utterances (commands, questions, etc.).
4
Distinguish spoken vs. mental proposition
Note that 'proposition' applies to both spoken (لفظية) and mental (معقولة) entities. The mental proposition is primary; the spoken is called a proposition because it signifies the mental one.
5
Understand the concept of tasdiq
Tasdiq (assent) is defined as knowledge of the proposition's truth-value. According to al-Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi), it is knowledge of the proposition itself; according to earlier philosophers, it is knowledge of the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقضية قول يصح أن يقال لقائله : «إنّه صادق فيه» أو «كاذب»، فالقول - وهو اللفظ المركب في القضية الملفوظة أو المفهوم العقلي المركب في القضيّة المعقولة - جنس يشمل الأقـول التامة والناقصة، وقوله : يصح أن يقال لقائله إنّه صادق فيه أو كاذب» فصل يخرج الأقوال الناقصة والإنشاءات كلّها - من الأمر والنهي والاستفهام وغيرها (۱) يعني أن القضية تطلق تارة على الملفوظة وتارة على المعقولة، إما بالاشتراك أو الحقيقة والمجاز. والثاني أولى ، لأنّ المعتبر هو القضية المعقولة، وأما الملفوظة فإنما اعتبرت لدلالتها على المعقولة، فسميت «قضيّة» تسمية الدال باسم المدلول. وكذلك لفظ «القول» يطلق على الملفوظ والمعقول، فالقول الملفوظ جنس للقضية الملفوظة، والقول المعقول جنس للقضية المعقولة. ثم القضية المعقولة هي المفهوم العقلي المركب من المحكوم عليـه وبـه والحكم - بمعنى وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها - فهذه المعلومات من حيث أنها حاصلة في الذهن تسمّى «قضيّة معقولة» والعلم بها يسمى «تصديقاً» عند الإمام. وأما عند الأوائل : فالتصديق هو العلم بالمعلوم الذي هو وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها كما عرفت - وقد يطلق التصديق بمعنى المصدق به على القضية لأن العلم التصديقي لا يتعلّق إلا بها، إما بجميع أجزائها أو ببعضها (شريف).
The proposition is a statement about which it is correct to say of its speaker that he is truthful in it or lying. So 'statement' – which is the composite utterance in the spoken proposition or the composite mental concept in the mental proposition – is a genus encompassing complete and incomplete statements. And his statement: 'it is correct to say of its speaker that he is truthful in it or lying' is a differentia that excludes all incomplete statements and non-indicative utterances – such as command, prohibition, interrogation, and others. (1) That is, 'proposition' is applied sometimes to the spoken and sometimes to the mental, either by homonymy or by reality and metaphor. The second is preferable, because what is considered is the mental proposition; the spoken is considered only because it indicates the mental, so it is called 'proposition' as a naming of the signifier after the signified. Similarly, the word 'statement' is applied to the spoken and the mental. So the spoken statement is a genus for the spoken proposition, and the mental statement is a genus for the mental proposition. Then the mental proposition is the mental concept composed of the subject, predicate, and judgment – meaning the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence. These pieces of knowledge, inasmuch as they are present in the mind, are called 'mental proposition', and the knowledge of it is called 'assent' (tasdiq) according to al-Imam. According to the earlier philosophers: assent is knowledge of the known thing which is the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, as you have learned – and 'tasdiq' may be used to mean the thing assented to, for the proposition, because assent knowledge does not relate except to it, either in all its parts or some of them (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition (qadiyya) by giving its genus (qawl, 'statement') and differentia (the possibility of truth or falsehood). The commentary then explains that 'proposition' applies to both spoken and mental entities, with the mental being primary. It distinguishes complete from incomplete statements and excludes non-indicative utterances (insha') like commands. The footnote clarifies terminology and introduces the concept of tasdiq (assent) as the knowledge of the proposition's truth-value, according to the Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) and earlier philosophers. The passage follows the order: definition, genus, differentia, then explanatory notes on the scope of 'proposition' and 'assent'.

Classifying Propositions: Categorical vs. Conditional
Primary division of propositions into categorical and conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as categorical or conditional by applying the resolution test to its terms.

Core Idea
A proposition is categorical if its terms are singular; conditional if they are propositions.

The first division in the study of propositions separates them into two fundamental types: categorical and conditional. This distinction is based on a simple mechanical test: remove the copula and check what remains. In this lesson, you will learn to apply this test to any proposition using examples from Islamic logic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula
Look for words indicating the relation between subject and predicate, such as 'is', 'is not', 'if...then', 'either...or'.
2
Delete the copula
Remove those connecting words from the proposition. What remains are the two terms.
3
Inspect the terms
Are the remaining terms singular (a single word or phrase that does not contain a proposition) or are they themselves full propositions?
4
Classify
If both terms are singular, the proposition is categorical. If at least one term is a proposition, it is conditional.
Subdivide categorical into affirmative and negative as in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي إما حمليّة أو شرطية، لأنها إما أن تنحل بطرفيها إلى مفردين أو لم تنحل، وطرفا القضية هما المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به، ومعنى انحلالها أن تحذف الأدوات الدالة على ارتباط أحدهما بالآخر، فإذا حذفنا من القضية مايدل على الارتباط الحكمي، فإن كان طرفاها مفردين، فهي حملية : إما موجبة إن حكم فيها بأن أحدهما هو الآخر، كقولنا : «زيـد هـو عالم، وإما سالبة إن حكم فيها بأن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، كقولنا : «زيد ليس هو بعالم»، فإنا إذا حذفنا لفظة «هو» الدالة على النسبة الإيجابية من القضية الأولى و ليس هو الدال على النسبة السلبية من القضية الثانية : بقي «زيد» و «عالم» وهما مفردان. (٢ وإن لم يكن طرفاها مفردين فهي «شرطيّة» كقولنا : «إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود» و «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً فإنه إذا حذفنا أدوات الاتصال - وهي كلمة «إن» و «الفاء»- بقي «الشمس طالعة، النهار موجود وهما ليسا بمفردين، وكذلك إذا حذفنا أدوات العناد - وهي «اما» و «أو» بقي «هذا العدد زوج وهذا العدد فرد» وهما أيضاً ليسا بمفردين. (1) القضيّة لابد فيها من الحكم، لأنه المحتمل للصدق والكذب، والحكم لابد له من المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به فهما أعني المحكوم عليه وبه - بمنزلة المادة للقضية والحكم الذي به يرتبط أحدهما بالآخر بمنزلة الصورة لها، وانحلال القضية هو بطلان صورتها وانفكاك أجزائها المادية بعضها عن بعض (شريف). (٢) كلمة «ليس» لرفع النسبة الإيجابية التي دلّ عليها لفظ «هو» ومجموعهما يدلّ على وضع النسبة السلبية، فيكون المجموع رابطاً للمحكوم به بالمحكوم عليه بالنسبة السلبية (شريف).
And it is either categorical or conditional, because either it resolves by its two terms into two singulars or it does not resolve. The two terms of the proposition are the subject and predicate. The meaning of its resolution is that we delete the particles indicating the connection of one to the other. So if we delete from the proposition what indicates the relational copula, then if its two terms are singular, it is categorical: either affirmative if it judges that one of them is the other, as in 'Zayd is a scholar', or negative if it judges that one of them is not the other, as in 'Zayd is not a scholar'. For if we delete the word 'is' indicating the affirmative relation from the first proposition, and 'is not' indicating the negative relation from the second proposition, there remains 'Zayd' and 'scholar', which are singular. (2) And if its two terms are not singular, then it is conditional, as in 'If the sun is risen, then daytime exists' and 'Either this number is even or odd'. For if we delete the tools of connection – namely the words 'if' and 'then' – there remains 'the sun is risen, daytime exists' which are not singular. Similarly, if we delete the tools of opposition – namely 'either' and 'or' – there remains 'this number is even and this number is odd', which are also not singular. (1) The proposition necessarily includes judgment, because it is what is liable to truth and falsehood. Judgment necessarily requires a subject and predicate; they – I mean subject and predicate – are like the matter of the proposition, and the judgment by which one is linked to the other is like its form. The resolution of the proposition is the nullification of its form and the separation of its material parts from each other (al-Sharif). (2) The word 'not' is for removing the affirmative relation indicated by the word 'is', and their combination indicates the establishment of the negative relation. So the composite is a copula linking the predicate to the subject in a negative relation (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage classifies propositions into two primary types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). The criterion is whether the two terms (subject and predicate) resolve into singular terms after removing the relational copula. If after deleting the copula ('is' or 'is not') only singular terms remain, it is categorical. The passage gives examples: 'Zayd is a scholar' (affirmative categorical) and 'Zayd is not a scholar' (negative categorical). If the terms are themselves propositions (e.g., 'the sun is risen' and 'daytime exists' after removing 'if...then'), it is conditional. Two subtypes of conditional are illustrated: conjunctive (ittisali, with 'if...then') and disjunctive (munfasil, with 'either...or'). Footnotes explain the necessity of judgment, the matter-form analogy, and the role of 'is' and 'not' in forming the copula.

Key Terms
الحملية (al-hamliyya): A proposition whose terms are singulars after removing the copula; e.g., 'Zayd is a scholar'. [Note: Standard term for categorical proposition across the tradition.]
الشرطية (al-shartiyya): A proposition whose terms are themselves propositions; includes conjunctive (ittisali) and disjunctive (munfasil). [Note: Stable term.]
الانحلال (al-inhilal): Resolution of a proposition into its material parts by deleting the relational copula. [Note: Technical term in logic; used consistently.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Every act of worship is good.
B) Some knowledge is beneficial.
C) If it rains, the prayer is in the mosque.
D) Zayd is not a traveler.

Key Takeaway
The primary division of propositions is into categorical and conditional, based on whether the terms resolve into singulars or propositions.

The Division of Propositions by Resolution into Simple Terms
Author defines categorical and conditional propositions based on resolution into simples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposition is categorical or conditional by applying the resolution test.

Core Idea
Whether a proposition resolves into simple terms determines its type.

The passage opens with the foundational division of propositions. Understanding this division is crucial because it shapes how we analyze arguments in Islamic logic. The key is the 'resolution' test: remove the copula or connecting particles and see what remains—if the two sides are simple words, it is categorical; if they are themselves propositions, it is conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition and its two sides
A proposition has a subject (mahkum 'alayhi) and a predicate (mahkum bihi). For example, in 'Zayd is a scholar', the subject is 'Zayd' and the predicate is 'scholar'. In 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists', the two sides are 'the sun rises' and 'daytime exists', each a proposition.
2
Remove the connecting particles
Remove words like 'is' (huwa), 'not' (laysa), 'if' (in), 'then' (fa), 'either' (imma), 'or' (aw). These indicate judgment or connection. For categoricals, remove the copula; for conditionals, remove the conditional or disjunctive markers.
3
Check if the remaining sides are simple (mufrad)
After removal, see if each side is a single word or can be expressed as a single word (potentially simple). In 'Zayd is a scholar', after removing 'is', we get 'Zayd' and 'scholar', both single words. In 'If the sun rises...', after removing 'if' and 'then', we get 'the sun rises' and 'daytime exists', which are full propositions, not single words.
The passage later clarifies that 'simple' includes potential simplicity, but for this step, stick to actual simplicity.
4
Classify the proposition
If the two sides are simple (actually or potentially), it is categorical (hamliyya). If they are not simple (i.e., they remain as propositions), it is conditional (shartiyya). The two types of conditionals are conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila).
Examples from the text: 'Zayd is a scholar' is categorical; 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists' is conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي إما حمليّة أو شرطية، لأنها إما أن تنحل بطرفيها إلى مفردين أو لم تنحل، وطرفا القضية هما المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به، ومعنى انحلالها أن تحذف الأدوات الدالة على ارتباط أحدهما بالآخر، فإذا حذفنا من القضية مايدل على الارتباط الحكمي، فإن كان طرفاها مفردين، فهي حملية : إما موجبة إن حكم فيها بأن أحدهما هو الآخر، كقولنا : «زيـد هـو عالم، وإما سالبة إن حكم فيها بأن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، كقولنا : «زيد ليس هو بعالم»، فإنا إذا حذفنا لفظة «هو» الدالة على النسبة الإيجابية من القضية الأولى و ليس هو الدال على النسبة السلبية من القضية الثانية : بقي «زيد» و «عالم» وهما مفردان. (1) القضيّة لابد فيها من الحكم، لأنه المحتمل للصدق والكذب، والحكم لابد له من المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به فهما أعني المحكوم عليه وبه - بمنزلة المادة للقضية والحكم الذي به يرتبط أحدهما بالآخر بمنزلة الصورة لها، وانحلال القضية هو بطلان صورتها وانفكاك أجزائها المادية بعضها عن بعض (شريف). (٢) كلمة «ليس» لرفع النسبة الإيجابية التي دلّ عليها لفظ «هو» ومجموعهما يدلّ على وضع النسبة السلبية، فيكون المجموع رابطاً للمحكوم به بالمحكوم عليه بالنسبة السلبية (شريف). وإن لم يكن طرفاها مفردين فهي «شرطيّة» كقولنا : «إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود» و «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً فإنه إذا حذفنا أدوات الاتصال - وهي كلمة «إن» و «الفاء»- بقي «الشمس طالعة، النهار موجود وهما ليسا بمفردين، وكذلك إذا حذفنا أدوات العناد - وهي «اما» و «أو» بقي «هذا العدد زوج وهذا العدد فرد» وهما أيضاً ليسا بمفردين. فإن قلت : قولنا «الحيوان الناطق ينتقل بنقل قدميه» وقولنا «زيد عالم نقيضه زيد ليس بعالم وقولنا الشمس طالعة يلزمها النهار موجود : حمليّات، مع أن أطرافها ليست بمفردات، فانتقض التعريفـان طرداً و عكساً. فنقول: المراد بـ«المفرد» إما المفرد بالفعل أو بالقوة، وهو الذي يمكن أن يعبر عنه بلفظ مفرد، والأطراف في القضايا المذكورة و إن لم تكن مفردات بالفعل إلا أنه يمكن أن يعبر عنها بألفاظ مفردة، وأقلها أن يقال : «هذا ذاك» أو «هو هو » أو «الموضوع محمول»- إلى غير ذلك - بخلاف الشرطيات، فإنّه لا يمكن أن يعبر عن أطرافها بألفاظ مفردة، فلايقال فيها هذه القضية تلك القضية؛ بل يقال : إن تحققت هذه القضية تتحقق تلك القضية» و «إما أن تتحقق هذه القضية أو تتحقق تلك القضية»، وهي ليست بألفاظ مفردة. نعم بقي هاهنا شيء، وهو أن الشرطية - كما فسرت – قضيّة إذا حللناها لا يكون طرفاها ،مفردين، ولاخفاء في إمكان أن يعبر عن طرفيها بعد التحليل بمفردين، وأقله أن يقال : «هذا ملزوم لذاك وذاك معاند لذاك»، فلو كان المراد بالمفرد إما المفرد بالفعل أو بالقوة، دخلت الشرطية تحت الحملية، فالأولى أن يحذف قيد الانحلال عن التعريف ويقال (1) فتعريف الشرطية غير مطّرد لدخول غير المحدود فيه. وتعريف الحملية غير منعكس الخروج بعض المحدود عنه (شريف). ٢) هذا القيد ذكره صاحب الكشف ومن تابعه والأولى تركه وحمل المفرد على ما يعم المفرد بالفعل وبالقوة - كما ذكره - ومن أنصف من نفسه عرف
And it (the proposition) is either categorical or conditional, because it either resolves into two simple terms with its two sides or does not resolve. The two sides of the proposition are the subject and the predicate. The meaning of its resolution is that we remove the particles indicating the connection of one to the other. So if we remove from the proposition what indicates the judgmental connection, then if its two sides are simple, it is categorical: either affirmative if it judges that one of them is the other, like our saying 'Zayd is a scholar', or negative if it judges that one of them is not the other, like our saying 'Zayd is not a scholar'. For if we remove the word 'is' indicating the affirmative relation from the first proposition, and 'is not' indicating the negative relation from the second proposition, there remains 'Zayd' and 'scholar', and they are simple. (1) The proposition necessarily requires judgment because it is what admits truth and falsehood. Judgment necessarily requires a subject and a predicate. They, i.e., the subject and predicate, are like the matter of the proposition, and the judgment by which one of them is connected to the other is like its form. The resolution of the proposition is the nullification of its form and the separation of its material parts from each other (Sharif). (2) The word 'not' is to remove the affirmative relation indicated by the word 'is', and their combination indicates the placement of a negative relation, so the combination is a connector of the predicate to the subject with a negative relation (Sharif). And if its two sides are not simple, then it is 'conditional', like our saying 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists', and 'Either this number is even or odd'. For if we remove the connective tools, which are the word 'if' and 'then', there remains 'the sun has risen, daytime exists', and they are not simple. Likewise if we remove the tools of opposition, which are 'either' and 'or', there remains 'this number is even' and 'this number is odd', and they are also not simple. If you say: Our saying 'The rational animal moves by moving his feet', and our saying 'Zayd is a scholar; its contrary is Zayd is not a scholar', and our saying 'The sun has risen; daytime exists necessarily' are categoricals, even though their sides are not simple, so the two definitions are refuted both by extension and exclusion. We say: What is meant by 'simple' is either actually simple or potentially simple, which is what can be expressed by a single word. The sides in the mentioned propositions, even if they are not actually simple, can nevertheless be expressed by single words—the least of which is to say 'this is that', or 'it is it', or 'the subject is the predicate', and so on—unlike conditionals, for their sides cannot be expressed by single words. So one does not say of them 'this proposition is that proposition'; rather one says 'If this proposition is realized, then that proposition is realized', and 'Either this proposition is realized or that proposition is realized', and these are not single words. Yes, there remains a point: the conditional, as defined, is a proposition such that when we analyze it, its two sides are not simple. But it is not hidden that it is possible to express its two sides after analysis by two simple terms, the least of which is to say 'this is a necessary condition for that' and 'that is an opposite of that'. So if what is meant by 'simple' is either actually or potentially simple, then the conditional would be included under the categorical. Therefore, it is better to omit the condition of resolution from the definition and say: (1) So the definition of the conditional is not universal because something not in the definiendum enters it, and the definition of the categorical is not exclusive because some of the definiendum is left out (Sharif). (2) This condition was mentioned by the author of Kashf and those who follow him; it is better to omit it and interpret 'simple' as including both actually simple and potentially simple, as he mentioned, and whoever is fair to himself will know.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the division of propositions into categorical and conditional. The author begins by stating the division: every proposition is either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). The criterion is whether the proposition 'resolves' into two simple terms (mufrad) when the copula (the particles indicating the judgmental connection) is removed. The two sides (tarafan) are the subject (mahkum 'alayhi) and predicate (mahkum bihi). If after removing the copula, the remaining terms are simple, the proposition is categorical. Examples: 'Zayd is a scholar' (affirmative) and 'Zayd is not a scholar' (negative). Removing 'is' and 'is not' leaves 'Zayd' and 'scholar', which are single words. If the sides are not simple, the proposition is conditional. Examples: 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists' (conjunctive conditional) and 'Either this number is even or odd' (disjunctive conditional). Removing the connecting particles leaves clauses that are not single words. The glosses by Sharif elaborate that judgment is essential, and the proposition's matter (subject and predicate) and form (judgment) are distinguished. An objection is then raised: some propositions like 'The rational animal moves by moving his feet' appear categorical, but their terms are not single words (e.g., 'rational animal' is a phrase). Similarly, 'Zayd is a scholar' and 'The sun has risen implies daytime exists' seem categorical, yet they contain complex terms. The objection claims the definitions of categorical and conditional are invalid both in inclusion (ghayr muttard) and exclusion (ghayr mun'akis). The answer clarifies that 'simple' includes both actually simple (bil-fi'l) and potentially simple (bil-quwwa). If a complex term can be expressed by a single word (like 'this is that'), it is potentially simple. In the categorical examples, we can replace the complex phrase with 'this is that' or 'the subject is the predicate', so they are potentially simple. In conditionals, however, such replacement is impossible because we must say 'If this proposition is realized, then that proposition is realized'—these are not single terms. Hence the conditional remains distinct. The passage then raises a further refinement: even conditionals can be expressed using simple terms after analysis, e.g., 'this is a necessary condition for that' or 'that is an opposite of that'. If 'simple' is taken as including potential simplicity, conditionals would wrongly be considered categorical. Therefore, the author suggests omitting the condition of 'resolution' (al-inhilal) from the definition of categorical and defining it directly as 'a proposition whose two sides are simple' in the sense that they cannot be expressed without referencing another proposition. This preserves the division.

حمليّة
شرطيّة

Key Takeaway
The resolution test—removing connecting particles—distinguishes categorical (simple sides) from conditional (propositional sides) propositions.

Refining the Definition: Potential Simplicity and the Objection Response
Author addresses objections to the definitions and refines them with potential simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the definitions of categorical and conditional propositions using the criteria of universality (tard) and exclusivity (aks), and apply the concept of potential simplicity.

Core Idea
Definitions must include all instances and exclude non-instances; the objection challenges both criteria.

After the definitions are presented, an objection is raised: the definitions appear invalid because some categoricals have complex terms, and some conditionals could be expressed simply. The response introduces 'potential simplicity' (al-mufrad bil-quwwa), refining the meaning of 'simple'. Then a further refinement suggests removing the resolution condition altogether. This dialectic illustrates how definitions are tested in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions under scrutiny
The definitions: a categorical proposition is one that resolves into two simple terms; a conditional does not resolve into simples. The objection claims these definitions fail the tests of tard (universality) and aks (exclusivity).
2
List the counterexamples
The objector gives examples: 'The rational animal moves by moving his feet' (seems categorical but 'rational animal' is not a single word), 'Zayd is a scholar; its contrary is Zayd is not a scholar' (contains a proposition? Actually it's a categorical with a complex predicate?), and 'The sun has risen; daytime exists necessarily' (a conditional? Actually it's a categorical with a complex subject?). The point: the simple condition fails both ways.
The text: 'قولنا الحيوان الناطق ينتقل بنقل قدميه وقولنا زيد عالم نقيضه زيد ليس بعالم وقولنا الشمس طالعة يلزمها النهار موجود : حمليّات، مع أن أطرافها ليست بمفردات'
3
Understand the response: potential simplicity
The author responds that 'simple' (mufrad) includes both actual (bil-fi'l) and potential (bil-quwwa) simplicity. If a complex term can be replaced by a single word (like 'this' or 'that'), it is potentially simple. In the examples, we can say 'this is that' or 'the subject is the predicate', so they are potentially simple. In conditionals, however, we cannot reduce the sides to single words; we must say 'If this proposition is realized, then that proposition is realized'. So the definitions hold.
4
Evaluate the further objection and solution
A further point: even conditionals can be expressed as 'this is a necessary condition for that' or 'that is an opposite of that', which are simple. So if potential simplicity is accepted, conditionals would be included in the categorical definition. The author suggests removing the condition of resolution from the definition entirely, and instead defining the categorical as a proposition whose two sides are simple in the sense that they are not themselves propositions (i.e., they cannot be expressed without reference to another proposition). This preserves the division.
The author says: 'فالأولى أن يحذف قيد الانحلال عن التعريف ويقال...'

وهي إما حمليّة أو شرطية، لأنها إما أن تنحل بطرفيها إلى مفردين أو لم تنحل، وطرفا القضية هما المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به، ومعنى انحلالها أن تحذف الأدوات الدالة على ارتباط أحدهما بالآخر، فإذا حذفنا من القضية مايدل على الارتباط الحكمي، فإن كان طرفاها مفردين، فهي حملية : إما موجبة إن حكم فيها بأن أحدهما هو الآخر، كقولنا : «زيـد هـو عالم، وإما سالبة إن حكم فيها بأن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، كقولنا : «زيد ليس هو بعالم»، فإنا إذا حذفنا لفظة «هو» الدالة على النسبة الإيجابية من القضية الأولى و ليس هو الدال على النسبة السلبية من القضية الثانية : بقي «زيد» و «عالم» وهما مفردان.
