And it (the proposition) is either categorical or conditional, because it either resolves into two simple terms with its two sides or does not resolve. The two sides of the proposition are the subject and the predicate. The meaning of its resolution is that we remove the particles indicating the connection of one to the other. So if we remove from the proposition what indicates the judgmental connection, then if its two sides are simple, it is categorical: either affirmative if it judges that one of them is the other, like our saying 'Zayd is a scholar', or negative if it judges that one of them is not the other, like our saying 'Zayd is not a scholar'. For if we remove the word 'is' indicating the affirmative relation from the first proposition, and 'is not' indicating the negative relation from the second proposition, there remains 'Zayd' and 'scholar', and they are simple. (1) The proposition necessarily requires judgment because it is what admits truth and falsehood. Judgment necessarily requires a subject and a predicate. They, i.e., the subject and predicate, are like the matter of the proposition, and the judgment by which one of them is connected to the other is like its form. The resolution of the proposition is the nullification of its form and the separation of its material parts from each other (Sharif). (2) The word 'not' is to remove the affirmative relation indicated by the word 'is', and their combination indicates the placement of a negative relation, so the combination is a connector of the predicate to the subject with a negative relation (Sharif). And if its two sides are not simple, then it is 'conditional', like our saying 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists', and 'Either this number is even or odd'. For if we remove the connective tools, which are the word 'if' and 'then', there remains 'the sun has risen, daytime exists', and they are not simple. Likewise if we remove the tools of opposition, which are 'either' and 'or', there remains 'this number is even' and 'this number is odd', and they are also not simple. If you say: Our saying 'The rational animal moves by moving his feet', and our saying 'Zayd is a scholar; its contrary is Zayd is not a scholar', and our saying 'The sun has risen; daytime exists necessarily' are categoricals, even though their sides are not simple, so the two definitions are refuted both by extension and exclusion. We say: What is meant by 'simple' is either actually simple or potentially simple, which is what can be expressed by a single word. The sides in the mentioned propositions, even if they are not actually simple, can nevertheless be expressed by single words—the least of which is to say 'this is that', or 'it is it', or 'the subject is the predicate', and so on—unlike conditionals, for their sides cannot be expressed by single words. So one does not say of them 'this proposition is that proposition'; rather one says 'If this proposition is realized, then that proposition is realized', and 'Either this proposition is realized or that proposition is realized', and these are not single words. Yes, there remains a point: the conditional, as defined, is a proposition such that when we analyze it, its two sides are not simple. But it is not hidden that it is possible to express its two sides after analysis by two simple terms, the least of which is to say 'this is a necessary condition for that' and 'that is an opposite of that'. So if what is meant by 'simple' is either actually or potentially simple, then the conditional would be included under the categorical. Therefore, it is better to omit the condition of resolution from the definition and say: (1) So the definition of the conditional is not universal because something not in the definiendum enters it, and the definition of the categorical is not exclusive because some of the definiendum is left out (Sharif). (2) This condition was mentioned by the author of Kashf and those who follow him; it is better to omit it and interpret 'simple' as including both actually simple and potentially simple, as he mentioned, and whoever is fair to himself will know.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory commentary on the division of propositions into categorical and conditional. The author begins by stating the division: every proposition is either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). The criterion is whether the proposition 'resolves' into two simple terms (mufrad) when the copula (the particles indicating the judgmental connection) is removed. The two sides (tarafan) are the subject (mahkum 'alayhi) and predicate (mahkum bihi). If after removing the copula, the remaining terms are simple, the proposition is categorical. Examples: 'Zayd is a scholar' (affirmative) and 'Zayd is not a scholar' (negative). Removing 'is' and 'is not' leaves 'Zayd' and 'scholar', which are single words. If the sides are not simple, the proposition is conditional. Examples: 'If the sun has risen, then daytime exists' (conjunctive conditional) and 'Either this number is even or odd' (disjunctive conditional). Removing the connecting particles leaves clauses that are not single words. The glosses by Sharif elaborate that judgment is essential, and the proposition's matter (subject and predicate) and form (judgment) are distinguished. An objection is then raised: some propositions like 'The rational animal moves by moving his feet' appear categorical, but their terms are not single words (e.g., 'rational animal' is a phrase). Similarly, 'Zayd is a scholar' and 'The sun has risen implies daytime exists' seem categorical, yet they contain complex terms. The objection claims the definitions of categorical and conditional are invalid both in inclusion (ghayr muttard) and exclusion (ghayr mun'akis). The answer clarifies that 'simple' includes both actually simple (bil-fi'l) and potentially simple (bil-quwwa). If a complex term can be expressed by a single word (like 'this is that'), it is potentially simple. In the categorical examples, we can replace the complex phrase with 'this is that' or 'the subject is the predicate', so they are potentially simple. In conditionals, however, such replacement is impossible because we must say 'If this proposition is realized, then that proposition is realized'—these are not single terms. Hence the conditional remains distinct. The passage then raises a further refinement: even conditionals can be expressed using simple terms after analysis, e.g., 'this is a necessary condition for that' or 'that is an opposite of that'. If 'simple' is taken as including potential simplicity, conditionals would wrongly be considered categorical. Therefore, the author suggests omitting the condition of 'resolution' (al-inhilal) from the definition of categorical and defining it directly as 'a proposition whose two sides are simple' in the sense that they cannot be expressed without referencing another proposition. This preserves the division.

مفرد بالقوة

Definitions must be tested for inclusion (tard) and exclusion (aks); the concept of potential simplicity resolves the objection but requires further refinement to avoid conflating conditionals with categoricals.

Defending Definitions: Actual vs Potential Singularity
Author rebuts an objection against definitions of categorical and conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the definitions of categorical and conditional propositions against objections by distinguishing actual and potential singularity.

Core Idea
A term is singular if it can be expressed in a single word, even if actually compound.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the standard definitions of categorical and conditional propositions. The objector claims that certain categorical propositions have compound terms, which would violate the definition that terms must be singular. The author's response introduces a critical distinction: singularity can be actual or potential. This defense preserves the integrity of the definitions and is essential for proper classification of propositions in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the objector's claim: he gives three examples of propositions that are clearly categorical but have terms that are not singular words. He says this violates the definitions 'in inclusion and exclusion' (ṭardan wa ʿaksan).
The objector uses 'ṭard wa ʿaks' to mean that the definitions fail both to include what they should and exclude what they should.
2
Examine the Examples
The examples are: (1) 'the rational animal moves by moving his feet' (subject and predicate are compound), (2) 'Zayd is knowledgeable, its contradictory is Zayd is not knowledgeable' (involves a proposition as term), (3) 'the sun is rising, its concomitant is daytime is existent'. Each has terms that are not single words.
These examples aim to show that the definitions of categorical propositions are too narrow or too broad.
3
Analyze the Response: Actual vs Potential Singular
The author explains that 'singular' (mufrad) can mean either actually singular (a single word) or potentially singular (what can be expressed by a single word). The terms in the examples, though actually compound, can be reduced to singular phrases like 'this is that' or 'he is he'.
This distinction salvages the definitions: the terms are potentially singular, so the examples still fit the definition of categoricals.
4
Contrast with Conditionals
The response then contrasts with conditional propositions. In conditionals, the terms are whole propositions linked by 'if...then' or 'either...or'. These cannot be expressed with singular words; one must say 'if this proposition is realized then that proposition is realized'. Hence conditionals truly have non-singular terms.
This shows that the definitions are not violated for conditionals—their terms are genuinely not singular, even potentially.
5
Evaluate the Defense
Consider whether the response successfully blocks the objection. Does potential singularity make the definitions too permissive? The author argues it does not, because conditionals still fail the test. The distinction preserves the boundary between categoricals and conditionals.
This step helps you appreciate how a well-crafted definition can withstand criticism through careful conceptual clarification.

Source Text

If you say: Our statement 'the rational animal moves by moving his feet' and our statement 'Zayd is knowledgeable, its contradictory is Zayd is not knowledgeable' and our statement 'the sun is rising, its concomitant is daytime is existent' are categoricals, even though their terms are not singulars, so the two definitions are violated in inclusion and exclusion.
So we say: What is meant by 'singular' is either actually singular or potentially singular, which is what can be expressed by a singular word. The terms in the mentioned propositions, although not actually singular, can be expressed by singular words—the least of which is to say 'this is that' or 'he is he' or 'the subject is predicate' and so on—unlike conditionals, for their terms cannot be expressed by singular words. It is not said in them 'this proposition is that proposition'; rather it is said 'if this proposition is realized then that proposition is realized' and 'either this proposition is realized or that proposition is realized', and these are not singular words.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response regarding the definitions of categorical (ḥamliyya) and conditional (sharṭiyya) propositions. The objection claims that certain propositions that have compound terms (e.g., 'the rational animal moves by moving his feet') are considered categorical, yet their terms are not singular, thereby violating the definitions both in inclusion (ṭard) and exclusion (ʿaks). The answer explains that 'singular' (mufrad) can be understood as actually or potentially singular. The terms in those examples, though actually compound, can be expressed with singular phrases like 'this is that' or 'he is he'. Hence they meet the definition potentially. In contrast, conditional propositions cannot be expressed with singular terms because their terms are whole propositions linked by conditional or disjunctive particles. This distinction preserves the definitions.

Refining the Definition of Conditional Proposition
Author resolves a remaining issue about conditionals being expressible as singular terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the original definition of conditional propositions and explain why the condition of analysis should be removed.

Core Idea
A definition must avoid allowing one type to collapse into another; the condition of analysis does just that.

The author addresses a subtle mistake in how conditional propositions are defined. If we define a conditional as a proposition whose terms, when analyzed, are not singular, then after analysis we can always express them as singulars (e.g., 'this is a concomitant of that'). If 'singular' includes 'potentially singular', then conditionals would wrongly be considered categorical. The fix is simple: drop the analysis condition entirely.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original definition
A conditional proposition was originally defined as a proposition whose terms, when analyzed, are not singular. This means if you break it down, you don't get singular terms.
The analysis (taḥlīl) refers to decomposing the proposition into its components.
2
Identify the problem
After analysis, it is possible to express the terms as singulars, e.g., 'this is a concomitant of that'. So if 'singular' includes potential singularity, then after analysis the conditional has singular terms, making it qualify as categorical.
The author points out this is 'not hidden' – it is obvious.
3
Recognize the consequence
If conditionals can be expressed as categoricals, the distinction between proposition types collapses. The definition must prevent such overlap.
4
Apply the author's solution
Remove the phrase 'when analyzed' (qayd al-inḥilāl) from the definition. The new definition: a proposition is categorical if its subject and predicate are singular; otherwise it is conditional. This matches Avicenna's definition in al-Shifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم بقي هاهنا شيء، وهو أن الشرطية - كما فسرت – قضيّة إذا حللناها لا يكون طرفاها مفردين، ولا خفاء في إمكان أن يعبر عن طرفيها بعد التحليل بمفردين، وأقله أن يقال : «هذا ملزوم لذاك وذاك معاند لذاك»، فلو كان المراد بالمفرد إما المفرد بالفعل أو بالقوة، دخلت الشرطية تحت الحملية، فالأولى أن يحذف قيد الانحلال عن التعريف ويقال: المحكوم عليه وبه في القضيّة إن كانا مفردين سميت حملية وإلا فشرطية» هذا هو المطابق لماذكره الشيخ في الشفاء )
Yes, something remains here. It is that the conditional—as interpreted—is a proposition which when analyzed its two terms are not singular, but it is not hidden that it is possible to express its two terms after analysis with singulars, the least of which is to say 'this is a concomitant of that' and 'that is an opposite of that'. So if what is meant by singular is either actually singular or potentially singular, the conditional would enter under the categorical. So it is better to delete the condition of analysis from the definition and to say: 'The subject and predicate in a proposition, if they are singular, it is called categorical; otherwise conditional.' This is what agrees with what the Shaykh mentioned in al-Shifa.

Commentary
This passage identifies a residual problem. The conditional proposition, as defined, has terms that are not singular when analyzed. However, after analysis (analysis into components), it is possible to express its terms with singular words like 'this is a concomitant of that'. If 'singular' includes potentiality, then the conditional might fall under the categorical definition, which is undesirable. The solution is to drop the phrase 'after analysis' (qayd al-inḥilāl) from the definition. The corrected definition simply states: if the subject and predicate are singular, it is categorical; otherwise conditional. This matches the definition in Avicenna's al-Shifa.

Critique of the Analysis-Based Definition of Conditional Propositions
Author presents an alternative definition and refutes it with two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proposed definition of conditional propositions based on analysis into two propositions and evaluate its two main criticisms.

Core Idea
Not every proposition that splits into two is conditional; analysis must preserve judgment.

In this passage, the author examines a proposed definition: a proposition is conditional if it can be analyzed into two complete propositions, and categorical otherwise. He rejects it with two arguments, using a counterexample involving a categorical proposition with a propositional predicate and a deeper point about the nature of analysis. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to correctly classify propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed definition
Locate the phrase 'وقيل: صوابه أن يقال: القضية إن انحلت إلى قضيتين فهي شرطية، وإلا فحمليّة'. This defines conditionals by their ability to decompose into two propositions.
This is an alternative definition; the author will reject it.
2
Recognize the counterexample
The example 'زيد أبوه قائم' is given. It is categorical (subject: Zayd, predicate: his father is standing) yet contains a proposition as predicate. Under the definition, it would be wrongly classified as conditional because it can be seen as containing two propositions: 'Zayd has a father' and 'that father is standing'? But note the author says it does not analyze into two singular terms because the predicate is a proposition.
The example shows the definition is too broad; it includes some categoricals.
3
Understand the first objection
The author says the definition is vulnerable to earlier objections (النقوض المذكورة). These likely include cases with contradictory or implicative relations that would also fit the analysis criterion incorrectly.
The passage references earlier objections; this lesson assumes you have seen them.
4
Understand the second objection
The author argues that analysis should break a compound into its actual parts. For conditionals, when you remove the conditional particles (لو، إن), the remaining parts are not full propositions because they lack the judgment that makes a proposition. What remains is merely material without the form of a proposition.
Thus, the analysis of a conditional does not yield two propositions, but two proposition-like fragments.
5
Grasp the implication
The definition fails because it mischaracterizes what analysis yields. It would also incorrectly classify some categoricals as conditionals (first objection) and misunderstands the nature of conditional parts (second objection).
The correct definition must account for the role of judgment and the conditional relation.

وقيل : صوابه أن يقال : «القضية إن انحلت إلى قضيتين فهي شرطية، وإلا فحمليّة لئلا يرد عليه مثل قولنا : «زيد أبوه قائم » فإنّه حمليّة مع أنه لم ينحل إلى مفردين، لأن المحكوم به فيه قضية.

1) راجع الشفاء : العبارة، ٣٢-٣٣، الفصل الخامس.

(٣) لأن المركب إنما ينحل إلى أجزائه الموجودة فيه لما عرفت من أن التحليل هو إبطال الصورة فلا يبقى إلا الأجزاء المادية، ثم إن أطراف الشرطية ليست قضايا، لأن القضية لاتتم إلا إذا اعتبر فيها الحكم إيقاعاً أو انتزاعاً، وما اعتبر فيه ذلك لا يرتبط بغيره ضرورة، فإنّك إذا قلت : «الشمس طالعة» وأوقعت النسبة بين طرفيه لم يتصور ربطه بشيء آخر بأن يصير محكوماً عليه أو به، فعالم تجرد القضية عن الحكم لم يمكن جعلها جزء قضية أخرى، فإذا حذفت أدوات الشرط والجزاء بقي «الشمس طالعة» و«النهار موجود بذلك المعنى الذي كان عليه حال الارتباط، فإنه بهذا المعنى كان موجوداً في الشرطية، فلا يكون قضية مالم يضم إليه الحكم، وحينئذ لا يكون ذلك تحليلاً فقط - بل تحليلاً إلى الأجزاء وضمّ شيء آخر إليها - ومن زعم أنه إذا حذفت
And it was said: The correct thing is to say: 'The proposition, if it can be analyzed into two propositions, is conditional; otherwise categorical' so that there does not enter into it something like our statement 'Zayd, his father is standing' for it is categorical even though it does not analyze into two singulars, because the predicate in it is a proposition. And this is not correct from two aspects: First, because some of the mentioned objections apply to it. Second, because the analysis of a proposition is into what it is composed of. [margin] That every categorical can be expressed by its two terms—with observation of the connection—by two singulars, and that the conditional cannot do that (al-Sharif). [footnote] Refer to al-Shifa: al-ʿIbāra, 32-33, chapter 5. (2) It is our statement: 'Zayd knows is contradicted by Zayd does not know' and our statement: 'The sun is rising entails daytime exists' (al-Sharif). (3) Because the compound only analyzes into its existing parts, since you know that analysis is the invalidation of the form so nothing remains except the material parts. Then, the terms of the conditional are not propositions, for a proposition is only complete when judgment is considered in it as positing or withdrawing. What is considered with that cannot be connected to something else necessarily, because if you say 'the sun is rising' and posit the relation between its two terms, it is inconceivable to connect it to something else to become a subject or predicate. So if the proposition is shorn of judgment, it cannot be made part of another proposition. If you drop the conditional and result particles, there remains 'the sun is rising' and 'daytime exists' in that meaning which they had in the state of connection, for in this meaning they existed in the conditional, so they are not a proposition unless judgment is attached to them. In that case, that is not only analysis, but analysis into parts and attachment of something else to them. And whoever claims that if he drops...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage discusses an alternative definition for conditional propositions: 'A proposition is conditional if it can be analyzed into two propositions; otherwise categorical.' The example 'Zayd, his father is standing' is categorical yet has a proposition as its predicate, so it would wrongly be considered conditional under this definition. The author rejects this definition for two reasons. First, it is vulnerable to the earlier objections (e.g., the examples with complex terms). Second, the analysis of a proposition should be into its actual constituent parts, but the terms of a conditional are not full propositions when considered in isolation from the conditional relation. The margin note from al-Sharif clarifies that every categorical can be expressed by two singulars considering the connection, unlike conditionals. The footnote elaborates that analysis of a conditional does not yield complete propositions because the judgment is removed when the conditional particle is dropped; what remains lacks proper propositional form. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is that the alternative definition is flawed.

قضية (qaḍiyya)

The proposed definition of conditionals by analysis into two propositions fails because it is too broad and misrepresents what analysis produces in conditionals: the parts are not full propositions due to the absence of independent judgment.

Refuting the Mistake about Conditional Tools
Author refutes a misconception about conditional tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that removing a conditional tool automatically reveals judgment in the extremes, using the logical principle that removal of a preventer does not guarantee existence without an efficient cause.

Core Idea
Removing a preventer requires an efficient cause; it does not necessitate existence.

You might think that stripping away the conditional tool 'if...then' leaves the extremes as independent propositions carrying truth or falsehood. The author warns against this error and demonstrates with a clear example that even when both extremes are false, the conditional can be true. Understanding this prevents a common misunderstanding in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mistaken claim
Some argue that the conditional tool prevents judgment in the extremes, so removing it restores judgment. Identify this claim: that the tool is a mere obstacle.
The tool is not just a preventer; it actively transforms the logical status.
2
Examine the counterexample
Consider the conditional: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray.' Both extremes ('Zayd is a donkey' and 'he would bray') are false, yet the conditional is true. This shows that removing the tool does not make the extremes true.
The truth of the conditional does not depend on the truth of extremes.
3
Analyze the logical error
The error is assuming that removing a preventer (the tool) suffices for judgment. In reality, judgment requires an efficient cause (mقتضي) to be present; absence of preventer is not enough.
The author cites the principle: 'زوال المانع لا يكفي في وجود الشيء' (removal of preventer is insufficient for existence).
4
Apply the principle to an Islamic science example
In Usul al-Fiqh, if a condition for a ruling is removed, the ruling does not automatically return. For instance, if travel ends, the concession to shorten prayer does not automatically cease unless the efficient cause (illah) of travel is absent.
This parallels the logical principle: removal of condition (preventer) does not guarantee return of judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدوات فقد وجد الحكم في الأطراف فقد أخطأ، وكيف يتوهم ذلك في مثل قولك : إن كان زيد حماراً كان ناهقاً مع العلم بكذب الطرفين وصدق الشرطية.
لا يقال : «الأدوات كانت مانعة عن الحكم ، فإذا زالت عاد الحكم»، لأنّ زوال المانع لا يكفي في وجود الشيء، بل لابد من وجود المقتضي، وزوال المانع لا يستلزمه كما في المثال المذكور.
وإن أردت تفصيلا يتضح به عليك الحال فاستمع لما نقول : القضية إن لم يوجد في شيء من طرفيها نسبة فهي حمليّة، كقولك : «الإنسان حيوان» وإن وجدت، فإن كانت مما لا يصح أن تكون تامه - بأن تكون نسبة تقييدية - فهي أيضاً حملية، كقولنا : الحيوان الناطق جسم ضاحك»، وإن كانت مما يصح أن تكون تامه، فإما أن توجد في أحد طرفيها، فتكون القضية أيضاً حملية، كقولك : «زيد أبوه قائم»، وإما أن توجد فيهما معاً، فإما أن تكون ملحوظة إجمالاً، فتكون أيضاً حملية، كقولك: «زيـد قـائـم ينافيـه زيـد لـيـس بقائم»، وإما أن تكون ملحوظة تفصيلاً، فتكون القضية شرطية، كقولنا : «إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود».
فظهر أن أطراف الحملية إما مفردة بالفعل أو بالقوة، فإن المشتمل على النسبة التقييدية مطلقاً أو الخبرية إذا كانت ملحوظة إجمالا ممايمكن أن يوضع موضعه مفرد، لأنّ دلالته إجمالية ؛ وأن أطراف الشرطية لا يمكن وضع المفردات في مواضعها إذ لا يمكن أن يستفاد من المفردات ملاحظة المحكوم عليـه وبـه والنسبة الحكمية على التفصيل ؛ فإن شئت قلت في تقسيم القضية : «طرفاها إما أن يكونا مفردين بالفعل أو بالقوة أو لا، وإن شئت قلت : «كلّ واحد من طرفيها إما أن يكون مشتملاً على نسبة تامة ملحوظة تفصيلاً أو لا ». وكان من قال : «القضيّة إن انحلت إلى قضيتين » أراد أن كل واحد من طرفيها قضية بالقوة ملحوظة تفصيلاً، فيكون قضية بالقوة القريبة من الفعل، فيصح التقسيم بهذا الوجه أيضاً.
The tools — if judgment is found in the extremes, he has erred. How could that be imagined in such as your saying: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' despite knowing the falsehood of the two extremes and the truth of the conditional? It is not said: 'The tools were preventing judgment, so when they are removed, judgment returns', because the removal of the preventer is not sufficient for the existence of the thing; rather, the existence of the efficient cause is necessary, and the removal of the preventer does not necessitate it, as in the mentioned example. And if you desire a detail by which the situation becomes clear to you, listen to what we say: A proposition — if there is no relation (nisba) in either of its extremes, it is categorical (hamliyya), like your saying: 'Man is an animal'. And if it exists, then if it is such that it cannot be complete — by being a restrictive relation (nisba taqyidiyya) — it is also categorical, like our saying: 'The rational animal is a laughing body'. And if it is such that it can be complete, then either it exists in one of its extremes, so the proposition is also categorical, like your saying: 'Zayd — his father is standing'. Or it exists in both together, then either it is considered summarily (ijmalan), so it is also categorical, like your saying: 'Zayd standing — contrary to it is Zayd not standing'. Or it is considered in detail (tafsilan), so the proposition is conditional (shartiyya), like our saying: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'. So it appears that the extremes of the categorical are either singular in actuality or in potentiality; for that which contains a restrictive relation absolutely, or a predicative relation when considered summarily, can be replaced by a singular term because its signification is summary. And the extremes of the conditional cannot have singular terms put in their places, because it is impossible to derive from singular terms the consideration of the subject, predicate, and the judgmental relation in detail. For if you wish, you may say in the division of the proposition: 'Its two extremes are either singular in actuality or potentiality, or not.' Or if you wish, you may say: 'Each of its two extremes either contains a complete relation considered in detail or not.' And whoever said: 'The proposition is analyzable into two propositions' meant that each of its two extremes is a proposition in potentiality considered in detail, so it is a proposition in potentiality close to actuality, thus the division by this aspect is also valid.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of propositions into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya) based on the presence and nature of internal relations (nisba) in the extremes. The author begins by rejecting a mistaken view: that removing the conditional tool reveals judgment in the extremes. He gives an example: 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray' — both extremes are false, yet the conditional is true. He then refutes the claim that the tool prevents judgment, explaining that removal of a preventer does not guarantee existence; the efficient cause is also needed. Then he offers a detailed classification. He presents a series of possibilities: (1) If there is no relation in either extreme, it is categorical (e.g., 'Man is an animal'). (2) If a relation exists but is not complete (i.e., restrictive, like 'rational animal' which is a qualification of 'animal'), the proposition is categorical. (3) If the relation can be complete and exists in one extreme only (e.g., 'Zayd — his father is standing'), it is still categorical because the incomplete extreme can be expressed as a singular term. (4) If the relation exists in both extremes, consider whether it is taken summarily (ijmalan) or in detail (tafsilan). If summarily, e.g., 'Zayd standing — contrary to it is Zayd not standing', it remains categorical. If in detail, e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists', it is conditional. The author then summarizes: categorical extremes are singular in actuality or potentiality (since a restriction or summary relation can be replaced by a singular term), whereas conditional extremes cannot be replaced by singular terms because they require detailed consideration of subject, predicate, and judgment. He offers alternative formulations: either the extremes are singular (actually or potentially) or not, or each extreme either contains a complete relation considered in detail or not. Finally, he clarifies that when logicians say a proposition is analyzable into two propositions, they mean each extreme is a proposition in potentiality considered in detail, which makes the division valid from that perspective.

شرطية
نسبة تقييدية

Key Takeaway
Removal of a preventer does not guarantee existence; an efficient cause is required.

Using the principle from this lesson, explain why the removal of the travel condition does not automatically negate the concession; the efficient cause (travel) is absent, so the ruling's basis is gone, not just the preventer.

Classifying Propositions as Categorical or Conditional
Author presents a detailed decision tree for classifying propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as categorical or conditional by analyzing whether its extremes contain a complete relation considered in detail.

Core Idea
A proposition is categorical if its extremes are singular in act or potency; conditional if each extreme contains a complete relation considered in detail.

Now we dive into the core classification. The author provides a decision tree that requires careful examination of the internal relations (nisba) within the extremes of a proposition. By systematically applying these criteria, you can determine whether a statement is categorical or conditional—a fundamental skill in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Check for any relation in the extremes
First, see if there is any relation (nisba) at all within the extremes. If none, the proposition is categorical (e.g., 'Man is an animal').
Relation here means a predicative or restrictive connection.
2
If a relation exists, determine if it can be complete
If the relation is not complete—i.e., it is a restrictive relation (nisba taqyidiyya) like 'rational animal'—the proposition remains categorical.
A restrictive relation merely qualifies a concept.
3
If complete, check if it exists in one or both extremes
If the complete relation exists in only one extreme (e.g., 'Zayd – his father is standing'), the proposition is still categorical because the other extreme can be replaced by a singular term.
The extreme with the relation is considered summarily.
4
If in both extremes, check the manner of consideration
If the complete relation exists in both extremes but is considered summarily (ijmalan), e.g., 'Zayd standing – contrary to it is Zayd not standing', it is still categorical. If considered in detail (tafsilan), it is conditional.
Summarily means the relation is taken as a whole; detail means the subject, predicate, and judgment are explicitly considered.
5
Apply the simplifying rule
Alternatively, remember: categorical extremes are singular in actuality or potentiality; conditional extremes cannot be replaced by singular terms because they require detailed expression.
Potential singularity arises from restrictive or summary relations.

Conditional propositions are not composed of two propositions
Author refutes the claim that conditional propositions are composed of two categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why conditional propositions are not composed of two independent propositions and identify the metaphorical usage of 'composition' in logical texts.

Core Idea
Conditional tools strip truth-value from their parts; conditionals are unities, not composites.

In Islamic logic, the nature of conditional propositions is often misunderstood. The author clarifies that a conditional is not simply two propositions joined by a connector. The conditional tool transforms its parts so that they no longer can be evaluated for truth or falsity independently. This distinction is crucial for accurate reasoning in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the author's claim
Understand that the author asserts conditionals are not composed of two propositions. This is a refutation of a common misconception.
2
Examine the effect of the conditional tool
Take a proposition that can be true or false, like 'The rainwater is pure.' Add the conditional word 'if' to get 'If the rainwater is pure.' Notice that now the statement cannot be evaluated for truth or falsity on its own; it is only part of a larger conditional.
3
Analyze the author's example
The author gives the example: 'The sun is rising' becomes non-propositional when prefixed with 'if'. Understand that the same applies to any proposition.
4
Understand the metaphorical concession
Logicians sometimes say conditionals are 'composed of two propositions' as a metaphor. This means that if we consider the parts with judgment, they would be propositions, but in reality they are not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرطية لا تتركب من قضيّتين، فإنّ أدوات الشرط والعناد أخرجت أطرافها عن أن تكون قضايا الا تري إذا قلنا : «الشمس طالعة» كانت قضية محتملة للصدق والكذب، ثم إذا أوردنا أداة الشرط عليه، وقلنا : إن كانت الشمس طالعة خرج عن أن يكون قضية تحتمل الصدق والكذب.
نعم ربما يقال في هذا الفنّ : «إنّ الشرطية مركبة من قضيتين» تجوزاً من حيث أن طرفيها إذا اعتبر فيها الحكم كانا قضيتين وإلا فهما ليستا قضيتين - لا عند التركيب ولا عند التحليل.
And the conditional is not composed of two propositions, for the tools of condition and opposition have removed its extremes from being propositions. Do you not see that when we say: 'The sun is rising', it is a proposition that can be true or false. Then when we bring the conditional tool upon it and say: 'If the sun is rising', it ceases to be a proposition that can be true or false. Yes, perhaps it is said in this art: 'The conditional is composed of two propositions' metaphorically, in the sense that its extremes, if judgment is considered in them, become two propositions; otherwise they are not propositions — neither in composition nor in analysis.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage clarifies that conditional propositions are not actually composed of two independent propositions. The author argues that the conditional or disjunctive tools (like 'if' or 'either-or') remove the extremes from being propositions that can be true or false. For example, 'The sun is rising' alone is a proposition, but when prefixed with 'If', it becomes part of the conditional and no longer carries independent truth-value. The author acknowledges that logicians sometimes say a conditional is 'composed of two propositions' as a (tajwuzan) metaphorical manner, meaning that if we consider the extremes with judgment assumed, they would be propositions; but in reality, they are not propositions either when composed or when analyzed. This refines the understanding: the extremes are not propositions in themselves; they are only potentially propositions if taken out of the conditional context.

A conditional proposition is a single unit; its parts are not truth-valuable on their own.

Connected vs. Disjunctive Conditionals
Qutbi divides conditionals into two types with definitions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disjunctive conditionals in Arabic logical texts and convert a disjunctive into its equivalent connected form.

Core Idea
Every disjunctive conditional reduces to two connected conditionals.

This passage from Qutbi's commentary introduces the two fundamental types of conditional propositions in Islamic logic: the connected (muttasila) and the disjunctive (munfasila). These propositions form the backbone of conditional syllogisms. Understanding their structure—especially how disjunctive conditionals can be transformed into connected ones—is crucial for analyzing arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Look for the key particles: 'in' (إن) for connected conditionals (if...then...) and 'imma' (إما) for disjunctive conditionals (either...or...). The passage provides clear examples of each.
2
Analyze the connected conditional
In a muttasila, the truth or falsehood of one proposition (the consequent) is judged conditionally on the truth of another (the antecedent). Example: 'If this is a human, it is an animal' — the truth of 'it is an animal' depends on the truth of 'this is a human'.
The antecedent is the condition, the consequent is the result.
3
Analyze the disjunctive conditional
In a munfasila, there is a judgment of incompatibility (tanafi) between the two propositions in truth and falsehood — either total (both cannot be true and both cannot be false) or partial. Example: 'Either this number is even or odd' — they are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
Total incompatibility is like even/odd; partial would be like 'either black or white' (can both be false if grey).
4
Convert disjunctive to connected conditionals
Qutbi explains that a disjunctive like 'Either even or odd' is equivalent to two connected conditionals: 'If it is even, it is not odd' and 'If it is odd, it is not even'. Practice this conversion to see the underlying implicative structure.
This equivalence reveals why disjunctives can be used in categorical syllogisms after transformation.
5
Recognize the absence of judgment in extremes
In both types, the judgment (hukm) is not in either extreme alone but in the relation. For the connected, this is obvious. For the disjunctive, it becomes clear only when we consider the implied connected conditionals.
This prevents misunderstanding: the conditional does not assert the truth of either part, only the relationship between them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والشرطية إما متصلة، وهي التي حكم بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على تقدير صدق قصية أخرى كقولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان» و ليس إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو جماد». و إما منفصلة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين القضيتين في الصدق والكذب - معا أو في أحدهما فقط - أو بنفيه كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فردا» و «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان حيواناً أو أسود». واعلم أن الشرطية لا يوجد في شيء من طرفيها الحكم، بل فرضه هذا في المتصلة ظاهر. وأما في المنفصلة فإنّما يظهر فرض الحكم إذا لوحظ فيها المتصلة اللازمة لها، فإن قولك : «هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد» في قوة قولك : «إن كان هذا العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً وإن كان فرداً لم يكن زوجاً وعلى هذا قياس ماعداه (شریف).
He said: And the conditional is either connected (muttasila), which is that in which the truth or falsehood of one proposition is judged upon the condition of the truth of another proposition, like our saying: 'If this is a human, it is an animal' and 'It is not that if this is a human, it is inanimate'. Or disjunctive (munfasila), which is that in which there is judgment of incompatibility (tanafi) between the two propositions in truth and falsehood — together or in one of them only — or of its negation, like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd' and 'It is not that either this human is an animal or black'. And know that the conditional has no judgment in either of its extremes; rather, this is obvious in the connected. As for the disjunctive, the supposition of judgment appears only when the connected necessary to it is considered. For your saying: 'This number is either even or odd' is equivalent to your saying: 'If this number is even, it is not odd, and if it is odd, it is not even.' And likewise for everything else.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the standard division of conditional propositions into two types: muttasila (connected/conditional) and munfasila (disjunctive). The connected conditional asserts that the truth (or falsehood) of one proposition follows from the truth of another — for example, 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' (implying that if the antecedent is true, the consequent is true) or the negative form like 'It is not that if this is a human, it is inanimate' (which denies the implication). The disjunctive conditional asserts incompatibility (tanafi) between the two propositions in truth and falsehood. This incompatibility can be total (both cannot be true and both cannot be false) or partial (only one aspect, e.g., they cannot both be true but can both be false, or they cannot both be false but can both be true). The author gives the example 'Either this number is even or odd' — which is a case of total incompatibility (they cannot both be true and cannot both be false). The negative disjunctive 'It is not that either this human is an animal or black' denies any incompatibility. The author then adds a technical note: in a conditional proposition, there is no judgment (hukm) in either extreme considered separately; this is clear for the connected conditional. For the disjunctive, the judgment appears only when we consider the implied connected conditionals — for instance, 'Either even or odd' is equivalent to two connected conditionals: 'If even, then not odd' and 'If odd, then not even'. This reveals the underlying logical structure.

Key Terms
متصلة
منفصلة
تنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) This is a human.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions split into connected (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or), and the disjunctive can be understood as a combination of two connected conditionals.

Identify whether each of the following is a connected (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila) conditional: (a) 'If someone performs wudu, then their prayer is valid.' (b) 'Either this action is obligatory or forbidden.' (c) 'It is not that if the sun rises, then it is night.'
?
Convert the disjunctive conditional 'Either this liquid is water or it is oil' into its equivalent pair of connected conditionals, using the pattern from the passage (if A then not B, if B then not A).

Muttasila: The Connected Conditional Proposition
Definition and subtypes of muttasila conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify muttasila propositions as positive or negative, and distinguish necessary (luzumiyya) from accidental (ittifaqiyya) connections.

Core Idea
A muttasila is an 'if-then' statement whose truth depends on the nature of the connection.

Conditional propositions are essential in Islamic logic for expressing dependencies, such as in usul al-fiqh when stating conditions for rulings. The muttasila, or connected conditional, is the basic 'if-then' form. In this lesson, we dissect its structure, its positive and negative forms, and how the strength of the connection determines its subtype—absolute, necessary, or accidental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In the example 'If this is a human, then it is an animal', find the condition (antecedent) and the result (consequent). The antecedent is 'this is a human' and the consequent is 'it is an animal'.
The antecedent is the condition upon which the consequent depends.
2
Determine the truth-claim
Is the proposition asserting that the consequent is true (positive) or false (negative) on the condition? In 'If this is a human, then it is an animal', the consequent is affirmed (positive). In 'It is not that if this is a human, then it is inanimate', the consequent is negated (negative).
The judgment is about the consequent given the antecedent.
3
Classify the connection
Is the connection necessary (always holds) or accidental (happens to be true in this case)? If the connection is necessary, it is luzumiyya; if accidental, ittifaqiyya. For example, 'If the fasting person eats intentionally, the fast is broken' is necessary (luzumiyya). 'If a person is in Mecca, he is in Saudi Arabia' is accidental (ittifaqiyya) because it is not logically necessary.
The text also mentions 'absolute muttasila' which is unspecified.
4
Apply the classification to an Islamic example
Take the statement from fiqh: 'If the prayer is invalid, then the wudu is not required.' Identify the antecedent, consequent, positive/negative, and type of connection. Is it luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya?
This helps connect theory to real Islamic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤١ - اقسام القضية الشرطية] قال : والشرطية إما متصلة، وهي التي حكم بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على تقدير صدق قصية أخرى كقولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان» وليس إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو جماد». وإما منفصلة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين القضيتين في الصدق والكذب - معا أو في أحدهما فقط - أو بنفيه كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فردا» و «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان حيواناً أو أسود». واعلم أن الشرطية لا يوجد في شيء من طرفيها الحكم، بل فرضه هذا في المتصلة ظاهر. وأما في المنفصلة فإنّما يظهر فرض الحكم إذا لوحظ فيها المتصلة اللازمة لها، فإن قولك : «هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد» في قوة قولك : «إن كان هذا العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً وإن كان فرداً لم يكن زوجاً وعلى هذا قياس ماعداه (شریف). القول : الشرطية قسمان متصلة ومنفصلة : فالمتصلة هي التي يحكم فيها بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى. فإن حكم فيها بصدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى : فهي متصلة موجبة» كقولنا : إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان» فإن الحكم فيها بصدق الحيوانية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية. وإن حكم فيها بسلب صدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى فهي متصلة سالبة كقولنا : ليس ألبتة إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو جماد» فإنّ الحكم فيها بسلب صدق الجمادية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية. فالمتصلة الموجبة هي التي يحكم فيها باتصال تحقق قضية بتحقق قضية أخرى، فإن اكتفي بمطلق هذا الاتصال سميت «متصلة مطلقة»، وإن قيد الاتصال بكونه لزومياً سميت متصلة لزومية أو بكونه اتفاقياً سميت «متصلة التفاقية». والمتصلة السالبة هي التي يحكم فيها بسلب ذلك الاتصال إما مطلقاً أو لزومياً أو اتفاقيا. والمنفصلة الموجبة هي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين قضيتين إما في التحقق والانتفاء معاً أو في أحدهما، فإن اكتفي بمطلق التنافي سميت «منفصلة مطلقة» وإن قيد التنافي بكونه ذاتياً سميت منفصلة عناديّة، وإن قيد بالاتفاق سميت «منفصلة اتفاقية». والمنفصلة السالبة هي التي يحكم فيها بسلب ذلك التنافي إما مطلقاً أو مقيداً بالعناد أو بالاتفاق. وسيرد عليك تفاصيل هذه المعانى في المتصلة والمنفصلة في مباحث الشرطيات (شريف).
Section on the divisions of conditional propositions. He said: The conditional is either muttasila (connected), which is that wherein judgment is made of the truth of a proposition or the lack of truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition, like our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' and 'It is not that if this is a human, then it is inanimate.' Or it is munfasila (disconnected), which is that wherein judgment is made of the mutual exclusion between two propositions in truth and falsehood—together or in only one of them—or their negation, like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd' and 'It is not that either this human is an animal or black.' And know that the conditional has no judgment in either of its extremes; rather, this is evident in the muttasila. As for the munfasila, the assumption of judgment appears only when the muttasila necessary for it is considered, for your saying: 'This number is either even or odd' is equivalent to your saying: 'If this number is even, it is not odd, and if it is odd, it is not even' and on this pattern is the rest (Sharif). The statement: The conditional is two types: muttasila and munfasila. The muttasila is that wherein judgment is made of the truth of a proposition or the lack of truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition. If judgment is made of the truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition, then it is a positive muttasila, like our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal,' for the judgment therein is of the truth of animality on the condition of the truth of humanity. And if judgment is made of the negation of the truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition, then it is a negative muttasila, like our saying: 'Absolutely not: if this is a human, then it is inanimate,' for the judgment therein is of the negation of the truth of inanimateness on the condition of the truth of humanity. So the positive muttasila is that wherein judgment is made of the connection of the realization of a proposition with the realization of another proposition. If one is satisfied with the absolute connection, it is called 'absolute muttasila'; if the connection is restricted to being necessary, it is called 'necessary muttasila'; or to being accidental, it is called 'accidental muttasila'. And the negative muttasila is that wherein judgment is made of the negation of that connection, whether absolutely, necessarily, or accidentally. The positive munfasila is that wherein judgment is made of the mutual exclusion between two propositions, either in realization and non-realization together or in one of them. If one is satisfied with the absolute exclusion, it is called 'absolute munfasila'; if the exclusion is restricted to being essential, it is called 'oppositional munfasila'; if restricted to being accidental, it is called 'accidental munfasila'. And the negative munfasila is that wherein judgment is made of the negation of that exclusion, either absolutely or restricted by opposition or accident. And details of these meanings will come to you regarding the muttasila and munfasila in the discussions of conditionals (Sharif).
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition and classification of conditional propositions, which are a key part of logic after categorical propositions. The author first defines the two main divisions: muttasila (connected conditional) is an 'if-then' statement where the truth of one proposition is judged on the condition of the truth of another; munfasila (disconnected conditional) is an 'either-or' statement where the two propositions are mutually exclusive. Examples are given: for muttasila, 'If this is a human, it is an animal' (positive), and 'It is not that if this is a human, it is inanimate' (negative); for munfasila, 'Either this number is even or odd' (positive) and 'It is not that either this human is an animal or black' (negative). The author also notes a subtlety: in a conditional, the judgment is not actually present in its parts; in muttasila this is clear, but in munfasila the judgment can be seen when it is reduced to a conjunct of muttasilas, as 'Either even or odd' is equivalent to 'If even then not odd, and if odd then not even'. The text then further subdivides each type by the nature of the connection or exclusion: muttasila can be absolute (muttasila mutlaqa), necessary (luzumiyya), or accidental (ittifaqiyya); similarly, munfasila can be absolute, essential/oppositional ('anadiyya), or accidental. Positive and negative versions exist for each. The author promises more details later. This classification is essential for understanding the truth-conditions of conditionals and for constructing valid syllogisms involving them.

متصلة
موجبة
سالبة
لزومية
اتفاقية
مطلقة

Key Takeaway
A muttasila proposition judges a connection between two propositions; the connection can be absolute, necessary, or accidental.

Munfasila: The Disconnected Conditional Proposition
Definition and subtypes of munfasila conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify munfasila propositions as positive or negative, and distinguish essential opposition ('anadiyya) from accidental (ittifaqiyya) exclusion.

Core Idea
A munfasila is an 'either-or' statement that asserts mutual exclusion between two propositions.

While muttasila expresses dependency, munfasila expresses disjunction and exclusion. In Islamic logic, munfasila is used in arguments of division (taqsim) and in establishing exhaustive options. This lesson unpacks the structure of munfasila, its positive and negative forms, and the nature of the exclusion—whether it is essential (by nature) or accidental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjuncts
In 'Either this number is even or odd', the two disjuncts are 'this number is even' and 'this number is odd'. Note that the munfasila asserts a relationship between these two propositions.
The disjuncts are the propositions being mutually excluded.
2
Determine the exclusion type
Is the exclusion in both truth and falsehood (mutual exclusivity in both) or only in one? The text says 'together or in only one of them'. In even/odd, the exclusion is in both truth and falsehood: if one is true, the other is false; if one is false, the other is true. In other cases, it might be only partial.
The text also mentions the possibility of negating the exclusion entirely (negative munfasila).
3
Classify the exclusion
Is the exclusion essential (by nature) or accidental? If it is essential, it is 'anadiyya (oppositional); if accidental, ittifaqiyya. For example, 'Either this number is even or odd' is essential ('anadiyya) because it is impossible for a number to be both or neither. 'Either this human is an animal or black' is accidental (ittifaqiyya) because the connection is not necessary.
The text also mentions 'absolute munfasila' which is unspecified.
4
Understand the reduction to muttasila
The author notes that a munfasila can be expressed as a conjunction of muttasilas: 'Either even or odd' is equivalent to 'If even, not odd; and if odd, not even'. This reduction is useful for logical analysis.
This shows the deep connection between the two types of conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤١ - اقسام القضية الشرطية] قال : والشرطية إما متصلة، وهي التي حكم بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على تقدير صدق قصية أخرى كقولنا: إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان» وليس إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو جماد». وإما منفصلة، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين القضيتين في الصدق والكذب - معا أو في أحدهما فقط - أو بنفيه كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فردا» و «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان حيواناً أو أسود». واعلم أن الشرطية لا يوجد في شيء من طرفيها الحكم، بل فرضه هذا في المتصلة ظاهر. وأما في المنفصلة فإنّما يظهر فرض الحكم إذا لوحظ فيها المتصلة اللازمة لها، فإن قولك : «هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد» في قوة قولك : «إن كان هذا العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً وإن كان فرداً لم يكن زوجاً وعلى هذا قياس ماعداه (شریف). القول : الشرطية قسمان متصلة ومنفصلة : فالمتصلة هي التي يحكم فيها بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى. فإن حكم فيها بصدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى : فهي متصلة موجبة» كقولنا : إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان» فإن الحكم فيها بصدق الحيوانية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية. وإن حكم فيها بسلب صدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى فهي متصلة سالبة كقولنا : ليس ألبتة إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو جماد» فإنّ الحكم فيها بسلب صدق الجمادية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية. فالمتصلة الموجبة هي التي يحكم فيها باتصال تحقق قضية بتحقق قضية أخرى، فإن اكتفي بمطلق هذا الاتصال سميت «متصلة مطلقة»، وإن قيد الاتصال بكونه لزومياً سميت متصلة لزومية أو بكونه اتفاقياً سميت «متصلة التفاقية». والمتصلة السالبة هي التي يحكم فيها بسلب ذلك الاتصال إما مطلقاً أو لزومياً أو اتفاقيا. والمنفصلة الموجبة هي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين قضيتين إما في التحقق والانتفاء معاً أو في أحدهما، فإن اكتفي بمطلق التنافي سميت «منفصلة مطلقة» وإن قيد التنافي بكونه ذاتياً سميت منفصلة عناديّة، وإن قيد بالاتفاق سميت «منفصلة اتفاقية». والمنفصلة السالبة هي التي يحكم فيها بسلب ذلك التنافي إما مطلقاً أو مقيداً بالعناد أو بالاتفاق. وسيرد عليك تفاصيل هذه المعانى في المتصلة والمنفصلة في مباحث الشرطيات (شريف).
Section on the divisions of conditional propositions. He said: The conditional is either muttasila (connected), which is that wherein judgment is made of the truth of a proposition or the lack of truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition, like our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' and 'It is not that if this is a human, then it is inanimate.' Or it is munfasila (disconnected), which is that wherein judgment is made of the mutual exclusion between two propositions in truth and falsehood—together or in only one of them—or their negation, like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd' and 'It is not that either this human is an animal or black.' And know that the conditional has no judgment in either of its extremes; rather, this is evident in the muttasila. As for the munfasila, the assumption of judgment appears only when the muttasila necessary for it is considered, for your saying: 'This number is either even or odd' is equivalent to your saying: 'If this number is even, it is not odd, and if it is odd, it is not even' and on this pattern is the rest (Sharif). The statement: The conditional is two types: muttasila and munfasila. The muttasila is that wherein judgment is made of the truth of a proposition or the lack of truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition. If judgment is made of the truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition, then it is a positive muttasila, like our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal,' for the judgment therein is of the truth of animality on the condition of the truth of humanity. And if judgment is made of the negation of the truth of a proposition on the condition of the truth of another proposition, then it is a negative muttasila, like our saying: 'Absolutely not: if this is a human, then it is inanimate,' for the judgment therein is of the negation of the truth of inanimateness on the condition of the truth of humanity. So the positive muttasila is that wherein judgment is made of the connection of the realization of a proposition with the realization of another proposition. If one is satisfied with the absolute connection, it is called 'absolute muttasila'; if the connection is restricted to being necessary, it is called 'necessary muttasila'; or to being accidental, it is called 'accidental muttasila'. And the negative muttasila is that wherein judgment is made of the negation of that connection, whether absolutely, necessarily, or accidentally. The positive munfasila is that wherein judgment is made of the mutual exclusion between two propositions, either in realization and non-realization together or in one of them. If one is satisfied with the absolute exclusion, it is called 'absolute munfasila'; if the exclusion is restricted to being essential, it is called 'oppositional munfasila'; if restricted to being accidental, it is called 'accidental munfasila'. And the negative munfasila is that wherein judgment is made of the negation of that exclusion, either absolutely or restricted by opposition or accident. And details of these meanings will come to you regarding the muttasila and munfasila in the discussions of conditionals (Sharif).
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition and classification of conditional propositions, which are a key part of logic after categorical propositions. The author first defines the two main divisions: muttasila (connected conditional) is an 'if-then' statement where the truth of one proposition is judged on the condition of the truth of another; munfasila (disconnected conditional) is an 'either-or' statement where the two propositions are mutually exclusive. Examples are given: for muttasila, 'If this is a human, it is an animal' (positive), and 'It is not that if this is a human, it is inanimate' (negative); for munfasila, 'Either this number is even or odd' (positive) and 'It is not that either this human is an animal or black' (negative). The author also notes a subtlety: in a conditional, the judgment is not actually present in its parts; in muttasila this is clear, but in munfasila the judgment can be seen when it is reduced to a conjunct of muttasilas, as 'Either even or odd' is equivalent to 'If even then not odd, and if odd then not even'. The text then further subdivides each type by the nature of the connection or exclusion: muttasila can be absolute (muttasila mutlaqa), necessary (luzumiyya), or accidental (ittifaqiyya); similarly, munfasila can be absolute, essential/oppositional ('anadiyya), or accidental. Positive and negative versions exist for each. The author promises more details later. This classification is essential for understanding the truth-conditions of conditionals and for constructing valid syllogisms involving them.

The Conjunctive Conditional: Muttasila
Author defines and classifies conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in a conjunctive conditional, classify it as affirmative or negative, and distinguish absolute, consequential, and accidental conjunctions.

Core Idea
The conjunctive conditional expresses a connection between antecedent and consequent; the type of connection determines its logical force.

We begin with the first kind of conditional proposition: the conjunctive (muttasila), the familiar 'if-then' statement. The author explains how to distinguish affirmative from negative forms, and further divides them based on whether the connection is absolute, consequential, or accidental. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating conditionals in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In a conjunctive conditional, locate the part after 'if' (the antecedent) and the part after 'then' (the consequent). For example, in 'If this is a human, then it is an animal', the antecedent is 'this is a human' and the consequent is 'it is an animal'.
2
Determine affirmation or negation
Check whether the consequent is affirmed or denied. If the consequent is affirmed (e.g., 'it is an animal'), it is an affirmative conjunctive. If the consequent is denied (e.g., 'it is not an inanimate'), it is a negative conjunctive. The author gives examples of both.
The negative conjunctive is indicated by 'not' or 'absolutely not'.
3
Classify the type of connection
If the connection between antecedent and consequent is unrestricted, it is 'absolute conjunctive'. If the connection is necessary (consequential), it is 'consequential conjunctive' (luzumiyya). If it is accidental, it is 'accidental conjunctive' (ittifaqiyya). The author mentions these three subtypes.
Look for keywords like 'luzumiyya' or 'ittifaqiyya' in the text.

القول : الشرطية قسمان متصلة ومنفصلة :
فالمتصلة هي التي يحكم فيها بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على صدق قضية أخرى.
فإن حكم فيها بصدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى : فهي متصلة موجبة» كقولنا : إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان» فإن الحكم فيها بصدق الحيوانية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية.
وإن حكم فيها بسلب صدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى متصلة سالبة كقولنا : ليس ألبتة إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو جماد» فإنّ الحكم فيها بسلب صدق الجمادية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية.
فالمتصلة الموجبة هي التي يحكم فيها باتصال تحقق قضية بتحقق قضية أخرى، فإن اكتفي بمطلق هذا الاتصال سميت «متصلة مطلقة»، وإن قيد الاتصال بكونه لزومياً سميت متصلة لزومية أو بكونه اتفاقياً سميت «متصلة التفاقية». والمتصلة السالبة هي التي يحكم فيها بسلب ذلك الاتصال إما مطلقاً أو لزومياً أو اتفاقيا.
والمنفصلة هي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين القضيتين : إما في الصدق والكذب معاً - أي بأنهما لا يصدقان ولا يكذبان- أو في الصدق فقط - أي بأنهما لا يصدقان ولكنهما قد يكذبان - أو في الكذب فقط - أي بأنهما لا يكذبان وربما يصدقان - أو بنفيه – أي بسلب ذلك التنافي.
فإن حكم فيها بالتنافي فهي «منفصلة موجبة».
أما إذا كان الحكم فيها بالتنافي في الصدق والكذب معاً سميت «منفصلة حقيقية» كقولنا : إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً» فإن قولنا : «هذا العدد زوج» و «هذا العدد «فرد» لا يصدقان معاً ولا يكذبان معاً.
وأما إذا كان الحكم فيها بالمنافاة في الصدق فقط، فهي مانعة الجمع» كقولنا : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء شجراً أو حجراً» فإن قولنا : «هذا الشيء شجر» أو «هذا الشيء حجر» لا يصدقان وقد يكذبان، بأن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً.
وأما إذا كان الحكم فيها بالمنافاة في الكذب فقط فهي «مانعة الخلو» كقولنا إما أن يكون هذا الشيء لا شجراً أو لا حجراً» فإن قولنا : «هذا الشيء لاشجر» أو «هذا الشيء لاحجر » لايكذبان، وإلا لكان الشيء شجراً وحجراً معاً - وهو محال - وقد يصدقان معاً بأن يكون حيواناً.
وإن حكم فيها بسلب التنافي فهي منفصلة سالبة»، فإن كان الحكم فيها بسلب المنافاة في الصدق والكذب معاً كانت «سالبة حقيقية» كقولنا : «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان أسود أو كاتباً» فإنّه يجوز اجتماعهما ويجوز ارتفاعهما.
Statement: The conditional proposition is two kinds: conjunctive and disjunctive.
The conjunctive is that in which one judges the truth or untruth of a proposition upon the truth of another proposition.
Thus, if it is judged the truth of a proposition upon the condition of the truth of another proposition, it is an affirmative conjunctive, like our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal,' for the judgment therein is the truth of animality upon the condition of the truth of humanity.
And if it is judged the negation of the truth of a proposition upon the condition of the truth of another proposition, it is a negative conjunctive, like our saying: 'Absolutely not: if this is a human, then it is an inanimate,' for the judgment therein is the negation of the truth of inanimateness upon the condition of the truth of humanity.
Thus, the affirmative conjunctive is that in which one judges the connection of the realization of a proposition with the realization of another proposition; if one is satisfied with merely this connection, it is called 'absolute conjunctive'; if the connection is restricted to being consequential, it is called 'consequential conjunctive'; or if it is accidental, it is called 'accidental conjunctive.' The negative conjunctive is that in which one judges the negation of that connection, whether absolutely, consequentially, or accidentally.
The disjunctive is that in which one judges the mutual exclusion between the two propositions—either in truth and falsity together, i.e., that they are neither both true nor both false; or in truth only, i.e., that they are not both true but they may be both false; or in falsity only, i.e., that they are not both false and perhaps both true; or its negation, i.e., the negation of that mutual exclusion.
Thus, if judgment therein is of mutual exclusion, it is an 'affirmative disjunctive.'
If judgment therein is of mutual exclusion in truth and falsity together, it is called 'true disjunctive,' like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd,' for our statements 'This number is even' and 'This number is odd' are not both true nor both false.
If judgment therein is of mutual exclusion in truth only, it is 'preventive of conjunction,' like our saying: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone,' for our statements 'This thing is a tree' and 'This thing is a stone' are not both true and may both be false, as when this thing is an animal.
If judgment therein is of mutual exclusion in falsity only, it is 'preventive of void,' like our saying: 'Either this thing is not a tree or not a stone,' for our statements 'This thing is not a tree' and 'This thing is not a stone' are not both false—otherwise the thing would be both tree and stone, which is impossible—and they may both be true, as when it is an animal.
If judgment therein is of the negation of mutual exclusion, it is a 'negative disjunctive'; if judgment therein is of the negation of mutual exclusion in truth and falsity together, it is 'true negative,' like our saying: 'It is not the case that either this human is black or a writer,' for they may both coexist and both be absent.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya), which are propositions that consist of two or more atomic propositions joined by a conditional connective. The author divides conditionals into two primary types: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila).

First, the conjunctive conditional (muttasila) is defined as a proposition in which one judges the truth or untruth of one proposition (called the consequent) based on the truth of another proposition (called the antecedent). The author gives two examples: an affirmative conjunctive ('If this is a human, then it is an animal') and a negative conjunctive ('Absolutely not: if this is a human, then it is an inanimate'). The negative form asserts that the consequent does not follow from the antecedent. The conjunctive is further subdivided based on the nature of the connection: absolute (mutlaqa) if the connection is unrestricted, consequential (luzumiyya) if the connection is necessary, and accidental (ittifaqiyya) if the connection is coincidental.

Second, the disjunctive conditional (munfasila) is defined as a proposition in which one judges mutual exclusion (tanafi) between two propositions. The mutual exclusion can be of four types: (1) in both truth and falsity (they cannot both be true nor both false), (2) in truth only (they cannot both be true but may both be false), (3) in falsity only (they cannot both be false but may both be true), or (4) the negation of mutual exclusion (i.e., they are not mutually exclusive).

If the judgment is of mutual exclusion, it is an affirmative disjunctive (munfasila mūjaba). This is then further divided into three kinds:
- True disjunctive (haqiqiyya): mutual exclusion in both truth and falsity. Example: 'Either this number is even or odd.' The two options are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
- Preventive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'): mutual exclusion in truth only. Example: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone.' They cannot both be true, but they may both be false (e.g., if it is an animal).
- Preventive of void (mani'at al-khuluw): mutual exclusion in falsity only. Example: 'Either this thing is not a tree or not a stone.' They cannot both be false, but they may both be true (e.g., if it is an animal, both 'not a tree' and 'not a stone' are true).

If the judgment is of the negation of mutual exclusion, it is a negative disjunctive (munfasila sāliba). If it negates mutual exclusion in both truth and falsity, it is a 'true negative' (sāliba haqiqiyya). Example: 'It is not the case that either this human is black or a writer'—they may both be true (e.g., a black writer) or both false (e.g., a white non-writer).

Throughout, the author uses clear examples from everyday reasoning, but all examples are rooted in general logical categories, not specifically Islamic sciences. However, the logical structure is applicable to Islamic jurisprudence where conditional reasoning is common.

The Disjunctive Conditional: Munfasila
Author defines and classifies conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify disjunctive conditionals as true (haqiqiyya), preventive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), or preventive of void (mani'at al-khuluw), and explain the logical relations they capture.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals express mutual exclusion; the three affirmative types differ in whether they exclude truth, falsity, or both.

The second kind of conditional is the disjunctive (munfasila), which asserts mutual exclusion between two propositions. The author describes four types of exclusion, leading to three affirmative subtypes and one negative form. Mastering these distinctions is crucial for analyzing either-or statements in Islamic legal debates and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjuncts
In a disjunctive conditional, there are two propositions joined by 'either...or' (إما...أو). Locate both parts. For example, in 'Either this number is even or odd', the disjuncts are 'this number is even' and 'this number is odd'.
2
Determine the type of exclusion
The author lists four possibilities: exclusion in both truth and falsity, truth only, falsity only, or negation of exclusion. For an affirmative disjunctive, decide which of the first three applies based on whether both disjuncts can be true, both false, or neither.
If they cannot be both true nor both false, it is haqiqiyya. If they cannot be both true but may be both false, it is mani'at al-jam'. If they cannot be both false but may be both true, it is mani'at al-khuluw.
3
Match to the correct subtype
Use the definitions: haqiqiyya (true disjunctive) excludes both truth and falsity; mani'at al-jam' excludes only truth; mani'at al-khuluw excludes only falsity. Practice with examples from the text.
The negative disjunctive (saliba) negates exclusion altogether.
