القول : الشرطية قسمان متصلة ومنفصلة :
فالمتصلة هي التي يحكم فيها بصدق قضية أو لاصدقها على صدق قضية أخرى.
فإن حكم فيها بصدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى : فهي متصلة موجبة» كقولنا : إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان» فإن الحكم فيها بصدق الحيوانية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية.
وإن حكم فيها بسلب صدق قضيّة على تقدير صدق قضية أخرى متصلة سالبة كقولنا : ليس ألبتة إن كان هذا إنساناً فهو جماد» فإنّ الحكم فيها بسلب صدق الجمادية على تقدير صدق الإنسانية.
فالمتصلة الموجبة هي التي يحكم فيها باتصال تحقق قضية بتحقق قضية أخرى، فإن اكتفي بمطلق هذا الاتصال سميت «متصلة مطلقة»، وإن قيد الاتصال بكونه لزومياً سميت متصلة لزومية أو بكونه اتفاقياً سميت «متصلة التفاقية». والمتصلة السالبة هي التي يحكم فيها بسلب ذلك الاتصال إما مطلقاً أو لزومياً أو اتفاقيا.
والمنفصلة هي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين القضيتين : إما في الصدق والكذب معاً - أي بأنهما لا يصدقان ولا يكذبان- أو في الصدق فقط - أي بأنهما لا يصدقان ولكنهما قد يكذبان - أو في الكذب فقط - أي بأنهما لا يكذبان وربما يصدقان - أو بنفيه – أي بسلب ذلك التنافي.
فإن حكم فيها بالتنافي فهي «منفصلة موجبة».
أما إذا كان الحكم فيها بالتنافي في الصدق والكذب معاً سميت «منفصلة حقيقية» كقولنا : إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً» فإن قولنا : «هذا العدد زوج» و «هذا العدد «فرد» لا يصدقان معاً ولا يكذبان معاً.
وأما إذا كان الحكم فيها بالمنافاة في الصدق فقط، فهي مانعة الجمع» كقولنا : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء شجراً أو حجراً» فإن قولنا : «هذا الشيء شجر» أو «هذا الشيء حجر» لا يصدقان وقد يكذبان، بأن يكون هذا الشيء حيواناً.
وأما إذا كان الحكم فيها بالمنافاة في الكذب فقط فهي «مانعة الخلو» كقولنا إما أن يكون هذا الشيء لا شجراً أو لا حجراً» فإن قولنا : «هذا الشيء لاشجر» أو «هذا الشيء لاحجر » لايكذبان، وإلا لكان الشيء شجراً وحجراً معاً - وهو محال - وقد يصدقان معاً بأن يكون حيواناً.
وإن حكم فيها بسلب التنافي فهي منفصلة سالبة»، فإن كان الحكم فيها بسلب المنافاة في الصدق والكذب معاً كانت «سالبة حقيقية» كقولنا : «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان أسود أو كاتباً» فإنّه يجوز اجتماعهما ويجوز ارتفاعهما.
Statement: The conditional proposition is two kinds: conjunctive and disjunctive.
The conjunctive is that in which one judges the truth or untruth of a proposition upon the truth of another proposition.
Thus, if it is judged the truth of a proposition upon the condition of the truth of another proposition, it is an affirmative conjunctive, like our saying: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal,' for the judgment therein is the truth of animality upon the condition of the truth of humanity.
And if it is judged the negation of the truth of a proposition upon the condition of the truth of another proposition, it is a negative conjunctive, like our saying: 'Absolutely not: if this is a human, then it is an inanimate,' for the judgment therein is the negation of the truth of inanimateness upon the condition of the truth of humanity.
Thus, the affirmative conjunctive is that in which one judges the connection of the realization of a proposition with the realization of another proposition; if one is satisfied with merely this connection, it is called 'absolute conjunctive'; if the connection is restricted to being consequential, it is called 'consequential conjunctive'; or if it is accidental, it is called 'accidental conjunctive.' The negative conjunctive is that in which one judges the negation of that connection, whether absolutely, consequentially, or accidentally.
The disjunctive is that in which one judges the mutual exclusion between the two propositions—either in truth and falsity together, i.e., that they are neither both true nor both false; or in truth only, i.e., that they are not both true but they may be both false; or in falsity only, i.e., that they are not both false and perhaps both true; or its negation, i.e., the negation of that mutual exclusion.
Thus, if judgment therein is of mutual exclusion, it is an 'affirmative disjunctive.'
If judgment therein is of mutual exclusion in truth and falsity together, it is called 'true disjunctive,' like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd,' for our statements 'This number is even' and 'This number is odd' are not both true nor both false.
If judgment therein is of mutual exclusion in truth only, it is 'preventive of conjunction,' like our saying: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone,' for our statements 'This thing is a tree' and 'This thing is a stone' are not both true and may both be false, as when this thing is an animal.
If judgment therein is of mutual exclusion in falsity only, it is 'preventive of void,' like our saying: 'Either this thing is not a tree or not a stone,' for our statements 'This thing is not a tree' and 'This thing is not a stone' are not both false—otherwise the thing would be both tree and stone, which is impossible—and they may both be true, as when it is an animal.
If judgment therein is of the negation of mutual exclusion, it is a 'negative disjunctive'; if judgment therein is of the negation of mutual exclusion in truth and falsity together, it is 'true negative,' like our saying: 'It is not the case that either this human is black or a writer,' for they may both coexist and both be absent.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya), which are propositions that consist of two or more atomic propositions joined by a conditional connective. The author divides conditionals into two primary types: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila).

First, the conjunctive conditional (muttasila) is defined as a proposition in which one judges the truth or untruth of one proposition (called the consequent) based on the truth of another proposition (called the antecedent). The author gives two examples: an affirmative conjunctive ('If this is a human, then it is an animal') and a negative conjunctive ('Absolutely not: if this is a human, then it is an inanimate'). The negative form asserts that the consequent does not follow from the antecedent. The conjunctive is further subdivided based on the nature of the connection: absolute (mutlaqa) if the connection is unrestricted, consequential (luzumiyya) if the connection is necessary, and accidental (ittifaqiyya) if the connection is coincidental.

Second, the disjunctive conditional (munfasila) is defined as a proposition in which one judges mutual exclusion (tanafi) between two propositions. The mutual exclusion can be of four types: (1) in both truth and falsity (they cannot both be true nor both false), (2) in truth only (they cannot both be true but may both be false), (3) in falsity only (they cannot both be false but may both be true), or (4) the negation of mutual exclusion (i.e., they are not mutually exclusive).

If the judgment is of mutual exclusion, it is an affirmative disjunctive (munfasila mūjaba). This is then further divided into three kinds:
- True disjunctive (haqiqiyya): mutual exclusion in both truth and falsity. Example: 'Either this number is even or odd.' The two options are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
- Preventive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'): mutual exclusion in truth only. Example: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone.' They cannot both be true, but they may both be false (e.g., if it is an animal).
- Preventive of void (mani'at al-khuluw): mutual exclusion in falsity only. Example: 'Either this thing is not a tree or not a stone.' They cannot both be false, but they may both be true (e.g., if it is an animal, both 'not a tree' and 'not a stone' are true).

If the judgment is of the negation of mutual exclusion, it is a negative disjunctive (munfasila sāliba). If it negates mutual exclusion in both truth and falsity, it is a 'true negative' (sāliba haqiqiyya). Example: 'It is not the case that either this human is black or a writer'—they may both be true (e.g., a black writer) or both false (e.g., a white non-writer).

Throughout, the author uses clear examples from everyday reasoning, but all examples are rooted in general logical categories, not specifically Islamic sciences. However, the logical structure is applicable to Islamic jurisprudence where conditional reasoning is common.

Key Terms
منفصلة
منفصلة حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
منفصلة سالبة

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive conditional captures mutual exclusion; the three affirmative types correspond to exclusion in truth, falsity, or both, each with distinct logical properties.

Defining Positive Disjunctive Propositions
Author defines positive disjunctives and classifies them into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of positive disjunctive propositions and distinguish them based on their mutual exclusion criteria.

Core Idea
Positive disjunctives judge mutual exclusion in truth, falsehood, or both — a precise tool for exhaustive options.

This passage introduces the core concept of disjunctive propositions in Islamic logic. The author explains that a disjunctive proposition involves a judgment of mutual exclusion (tanāfī) between two constituent propositions. Positive disjunctives are distinguished by affirming that mutual exclusion holds, and the specific type depends on whether the exclusion applies to truth, falsehood, or both. Understanding these types is essential for constructing and analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences like uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the global definition
Find the sentence where the author defines the disjunctive proposition (al-munfaṣila). It states that it is a judgment of mutual exclusion between two propositions. Note that the exclusion can be in truth, falsehood, or both, or their negation.
The definition is given at the beginning of the passage.
2
Extract the two dimensions of exclusion
The author mentions three possibilities: exclusion in truth and falsehood together, exclusion in truth only, and exclusion in falsehood only. Write these down as the two dimensions (truth and falsehood) and their combinations.
The negation (no exclusion) leads to negative disjunctives, which are not the focus here.
3
Understand positive disjunctives
When the judgment is that mutual exclusion exists, it is a positive disjunctive. The author then names three sub-types based on which dimension(s) the exclusion applies to. Recognize that the first type (haqiqiyya) is the strongest, while the other two are weaker.
The terms maniat al-jam‘ and maniat al-khuluw are used respectively.
4
Match each type to its condition
For each type, write the condition: (1) haqiqiyya: cannot be both true nor both false; (2) mani'at al-jam‘: cannot be both true, but may be both false; (3) mani'at al-khuluw: cannot be both false, but may be both true. Check that you understand the difference.
Use the examples from the passage to confirm understanding.

The disjunctive [proposition] is that in which there is a judgment of mutual exclusion between the two propositions: either in truth and falsehood together — i.e., that they are neither both true nor both false — or in truth only — i.e., that they are not both true but may be both false — or in falsehood only — i.e., that they are not both false and may be both true — or by its negation — i.e., by negating that mutual exclusion. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion, then it is a 'positive disjunctive'. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood together, it is called a 'true disjunctive' (haqiqiyya), like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd', for our saying 'this number is even' and 'this number is odd' are neither both true nor both false. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion in truth only, then it is a 'preventive of conjunction' (mani'at al-jam'), like our saying: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone', for our saying 'this thing is a tree' or 'this thing is a stone' are not both true and may be both false, if this thing is an animal. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion in falsehood only, then it is a 'preventive of emptiness' (mani'at al-khuluw), like our saying: 'Either this thing is not a tree or not a stone', for our saying 'this thing is not a tree' or 'this thing is not a stone' are not both false, otherwise the thing would be both a tree and a stone — which is impossible — and they may both be true, if it is an animal.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies positive disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila al-mujiba). The author begins by stating that a disjunctive proposition involves a judgment of mutual exclusion (tanafi) between two propositions. The mutual exclusion can be of four types: both in truth and falsehood (i.e., they cannot be both true or both false), only in truth (they cannot both be true, but may both be false), only in falsehood (they cannot both be false, but may both be true), or negation of that mutual exclusion (i.e., no mutual exclusion, leading to negative disjunctives). The focus here is on the first three which yield positive disjunctives. The first type, where mutual exclusion is in both truth and falsehood, is called 'true disjunctive' (munfasila haqiqiyya), exemplified by 'This number is either even or odd' — these two cannot be both true (a number cannot be both even and odd) and cannot be both false (a number must be either even or odd). The second type, mutual exclusion only in truth, is called 'preventive of conjunction' (mani'at al-jam'), as in 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' — they cannot both be true, but could both be false if the thing is an animal. The third type, mutual exclusion only in falsehood, is called 'preventive of emptiness' (mani'at al-khuluw), using the example 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone' — these cannot both be false (otherwise it would be both a tree and a stone, impossible), but may both be true if the thing is an animal. The passage uses clear examples from common sense and introduces the technical terms concisely.

Distinguishing the Three Types of Positive Disjunctives
Author gives examples for each type of positive disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given positive disjunctive proposition as haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', or mani'at al-khuluw by testing its truth conditions.

Core Idea
Test each type by checking whether both parts can be true or both false — the pattern reveals the type.

This lesson builds on the definitions by providing a practical method to distinguish the three types of positive disjunctives. Using the examples from the passage and new Islamic examples, you will learn to apply a simple test: check the possibilities of both truths and both falsehoods. Mastering this distinction is crucial for evaluating disjunctive arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Review the three conditions
Recall: Haqiqiyya: cannot be both true nor both false. Mani'at al-jam': cannot be both true, but can be both false. Mani'at al-khuluw: cannot be both false, but can be both true. Write these as a quick reference.
2
Analyze the passage examples
For each example given by the author (even/odd, tree/stone, not tree/not stone), confirm why it fits its type. For instance, 'even or odd' cannot be both true (a number cannot be both) and cannot be both false (a number must be one), so it is haqiqiyya.
3
Test new Islamic examples
Consider statements like 'This action is either obligatory (wājib) or forbidden (ḥarām).' Can it be both true? No, an action cannot be both wājib and ḥarām. Can it be both false? Yes, if it is permissible (mubāḥ). Therefore it is mani'at al-jam'.
Use other pairs: 'The world is either created or eternal' — can be both false? No, so it is probably haqiqiyya (if we accept only two options).
4
Create your own disjunctives
Think of a pair of propositions in an Islamic context (e.g., about the attributes of Allah, or rulings on food) and determine which type they form. Justify your answer by checking the truth possibilities.

The disjunctive [proposition] is that in which there is a judgment of mutual exclusion between the two propositions: either in truth and falsehood together — i.e., that they are neither both true nor both false — or in truth only — i.e., that they are not both true but may be both false — or in falsehood only — i.e., that they are not both false and may be both true — or by its negation — i.e., by negating that mutual exclusion. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion, then it is a 'positive disjunctive'. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood together, it is called a 'true disjunctive' (haqiqiyya), like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd', for our saying 'this number is even' and 'this number is odd' are neither both true nor both false. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion in truth only, then it is a 'preventive of conjunction' (mani'at al-jam'), like our saying: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone', for our saying 'this thing is a tree' or 'this thing is a stone' are not both true and may be both false, if this thing is an animal. If the judgment is of mutual exclusion in falsehood only, then it is a 'preventive of emptiness' (mani'at al-khuluw), like our saying: 'Either this thing is not a tree or not a stone', for our saying 'this thing is not a tree' or 'this thing is not a stone' are not both false, otherwise the thing would be both a tree and a stone — which is impossible — and they may both be true, if it is an animal.

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies positive disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila al-mujiba). The author begins by stating that a disjunctive proposition involves a judgment of mutual exclusion (tanafi) between two propositions. The mutual exclusion can be of four types: both in truth and falsehood (i.e., they cannot be both true or both false), only in truth (they cannot both be true, but may both be false), only in falsehood (they cannot both be false, but may both be true), or negation of that mutual exclusion (i.e., no mutual exclusion, leading to negative disjunctives). The focus here is on the first three which yield positive disjunctives. The first type, where mutual exclusion is in both truth and falsehood, is called 'true disjunctive' (munfasila haqiqiyya), exemplified by 'This number is either even or odd' — these two cannot be both true (a number cannot be both even and odd) and cannot be both false (a number must be either even or odd). The third type, mutual exclusion only in falsehood, is called 'preventive of emptiness' (mani'at al-khuluw), using the example 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone' — these cannot both be false (otherwise it would be both a tree and a stone, impossible), but may both be true if the thing is an animal. The passage uses clear examples from common sense and introduces the technical terms concisely.

Classifying Negative Disjunctives and the Naming Objection
Author defines negative disjunctive propositions and defends the conventional naming of all negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify negative disjunctive propositions into three types and defend the technical naming of negative propositions against linguistic objections.

Core Idea
The three types of negative disjunctives mirror the positives; their names are based on technical convention, not linguistic fit.

In this passage, the author classifies negative disjunctive propositions, showing that they mirror the three positive types. He then anticipates an objection: if negative propositions negate predication or connection, why call them categorical or conditional? His answer is rooted in the technical (istilahi) definition of these terms, which applies equally to affirmatives and negatives. This lesson will help you see how technical conventions in logic preserve consistency even when linguistic meanings seem to conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three negative disjunctive types
The author names three types: true negative (saliba haqiqiyya), negative preventive of conjunction (saliba mani'at al-jam'), and negative preventive of emptiness (saliba mani'at al-khuluw). Read the definitions and examples for each.
Each type corresponds to a negation of a specific aspect of mutual exclusion.
2
Map each negative to its positive counterpart
For each negative type, identify which positive type it negates. The true negative negates both jam' and khuluw; the second negates only jam'; the third negates only khuluw.
Understanding this mapping is key to seeing the symmetry between positive and negative disjunctives.
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues that negative propositions cannot be called categorical, conditional, or disjunctive because they negate the core relation (predication, connection, disconnection). This is a linguistic objection.
The objection hinges on the linguistic meaning of the terms.
4
Analyze the author's response
The author distinguishes linguistic meaning from technical convention (istilah). The technical definition of a categorical proposition is based on having two singular terms, not on affirmation. This definition applies equally to affirmatives and negatives, so negatives are properly called categorical by convention.
The author emphasizes that the same technical concepts apply to both positives and negatives.

وإن حكم فيها بسلب التنافي فهي منفصلة سالبة»، فإن كان الحكم فيها بسلب المنافاة في الصدق والكذب معاً كانت «سالبة حقيقية» كقولنا : «ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان أسود أو كاتباً» فإنّه يجوز اجتماعهما ويجوز ارتفاعهما. وإن كان الحكم فيها بسلب المنافاة في الصدق فقط كانت «سالبة مانعة الجمع» كقولنا : ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان حيواناً أو أسود» فإنّه يجوز اجتماعهما ولا يجوز ارتفاعهما . وإن كان الحكم فيها بسلب المنافاة في الكذب فقط كانت «سالبة مانعة الخلو» كقولنا : ليس إما أن يكون هذا الإنسان رومياً أو زنجياً» فإنّه يجوز ارتفاعهما دون الاجتماع. لايقال : السوالب الحمليّة والمتصلة والمنفصلة - على ماذكرتم - ما يرفع فيها الحمل والاتصال والانفصال فلا تكون حملية ومتصلة ومنفصلة، لأنّها ما يثبت فيها الحمل والاتصال والانفصال. لانا نقول : ليس إجراء هذه الأسامي على السوالب بحسب مفهوم اللغة، بل بحسب الاصطلاح، ومفهوماتها الاصطلاحية كما تصدق على الموجبات تصدق على السوالب. نعم المناسبة المتحققة للنقل أما في (۱) لأن مفهوم الحملية اصطلاحاً هو القضية التي يكون طرفاها مفردين إما بالفعل أو بالقوة، وهذا المفهوم كما يصدق على زيد قائم» يصدق على «زيد ليس بقائم» بلا تفاوت وكذلك الحال في مفهومي المتصلة والمنفصلة اصطلاحاً، بل نقول إطلاق الشرطية على المنفصلة أيضاً بحسب المفهوم الاصطلاحي كإطلاقها على المتصلة، وإن لم يكن معنى الشرطية بحسب اللغة في المنفصلة ظاهراً. وقد يتوهم من قوله ليس إجراء هذه الأسامي على السوالب بحسب مفهوم اللغة» أن إجراءها على الموجبات بحسب مفهوم اللغة، وليس كذلك ؛ بل إجراء هذه الأسامي عليهما معاً بحسب المفهوم الاصطلاحي قطعاً؛
If the judgment is of negating mutual exclusion, then it is a 'negative disjunctive'. If the judgment is of negating mutual exclusion in truth and falsehood together, it is a 'true negative' (saliba haqiqiyya), like our saying: 'It is not that either this man is black or a writer', for it is possible for both to be true and for both to be false. If the judgment is of negating mutual exclusion in truth only, it is a 'negative preventive of conjunction' (saliba mani'at al-jam'), like our saying: 'It is not that either this man is an animal or black', for it is possible for both to be true but not for both to be false. If the judgment is of negating mutual exclusion in falsehood only, it is a 'negative preventive of emptiness' (saliba mani'at al-khuluw), like our saying: 'It is not that either this man is Roman or Negro', for it is possible for both to be false but not for both to be true. It cannot be said: The negative categorical, conditional, and disjunctive [propositions] — according to what you have mentioned — are those in which the predication, connection, and disconnection are negated, so they are not categorical, conditional, and disjunctive, because they are those in which predication, connection, and disconnection are affirmed. For we say: The application of these names to negatives is not according to linguistic meaning, but according to technical convention, and their technical concepts apply to positives just as they apply to negatives. Indeed, the suitability that exists for the transfer [of meaning] is such that (1) because the technical concept of categorical is the proposition whose two terms are singular, either actually or potentially, and this concept applies to 'Zayd is standing' just as it applies to 'Zayd is not standing' without difference. Similarly is the case with the technical concepts of conditional and disjunctive. Rather, we say: The application of 'conditional' to the disjunctive is also according to technical convention, just as its application to the conditional, even if the linguistic meaning of 'conditional' in the disjunctive is not apparent. And one might misunderstand from his statement 'The application of these names to negatives is not according to linguistic meaning' that their application to positives is according to linguistic meaning, but that is not so; rather, the application of these names to both is according to technical convention, absolutely.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage first classifies negative disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila al-saliba) into three types corresponding to the positive types. A 'true negative' (saliba haqiqiyya) negates mutual exclusion in both truth and falsehood, e.g., 'It is not the case that either this man is black or a writer' — both can be true (if he is a black writer) and both false (if he is neither). A 'negative preventive of conjunction' (saliba mani'at al-jam') negates mutual exclusion only in truth, e.g., 'It is not that either this man is an animal or black' — both can be true (a black animal) but cannot both be false (since an animal is not black? Actually the example: a man is an animal by definition, so 'man is an animal' is always true, 'man is black' might be false, so they cannot both be false? Wait: the text says 'it is possible for both to be true but not for both to be false', meaning at least one is true. The example: 'This man is an animal' is always true, so at least one is true, so they cannot both be false. The negative of mutually exclusive in truth only means that in the original (positive) form, they cannot both be true; here we negate that, so we allow both true, but still at least one true? Actually careful: The passage is consistent: the negative form is the negation of the judgment of mutual exclusion. So for saliba mani'at al-jam', it negates the 'cannot both be true' part, meaning it asserts that they can both be true, but still may have some restriction on falsehood? The example: 'It is not that either this man is an animal or black'. Since 'this man is an animal' is always true, both can be true (if he is black) and they cannot both be false (because the animal proposition is always true). So it fits: they can both be true, but not both false. The third type, 'negative preventive of emptiness' (saliba mani'at al-khuluw) negates the 'cannot both be false' part, so they can both be false but not both true. Example: 'It is not that either this man is Roman or Negro' — both can be false (if he is neither), but cannot both be true (one person cannot be both). After this classification, the author addresses a potential objection: Some might argue that negative categorical, conditional, and disjunctive propositions are not properly called by those names because they negate predication (in categorical) or connection/disconnection (in conditional/disjunctive), whereas the names imply affirmation. The author responds that these names are applied according to technical convention (istilah), not according to linguistic meaning. The technical concepts themselves apply equally to positives and negatives. For example, the technical definition of a categorical proposition is one whose two terms are singular (actually or potentially) — this applies to 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing'. Similarly for conditionals and disjunctives. The passage ends by clarifying that the application to negatives is not according to linguistic meaning, but the same applies to positives; both are based on technical convention. This refutes a possible misinterpretation.

Negative Conditionals: Definitions and the Defense of Terminology
Defining two negative conditionals and defending their terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between negative exclusive conjunction (sāliba māni‘at al-jam‘) and negative exclusive disjunction (sāliba māni‘at al-khuluw), and explain why negative conditionals are still considered conditionals by conventional meaning.

Core Idea
Negative conditionals are conditionals by convention, not language.

In this passage, the author defines two key types of negative disjunctive conditionals: one that denies incompatibility in truth and another that denies incompatibility in falsehood. He then addresses a common objection: how can negative propositions, which remove connection, still be called 'conditional'? The answer lies in the crucial distinction between linguistic and conventional meaning—a fundamental principle in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Define 'negative exclusive conjunction'
Find the definition: it denies incompatibility in truth only. That means both parts can be true together, but they cannot both be false. The example given is: 'It is not the case that either this human is an animal or black'—both can be true (animal and black) but not both false.
2
Define 'negative exclusive disjunction'
Find the definition: it denies incompatibility in falsehood only. That means both parts can be false together, but they cannot both be true. The example is: 'It is not the case that either this human is Roman or Zanj'—both can be false (neither) but not both true.
3
Identify the objection
The objector says: negative categoricals, conjunctives, and disjunctives remove the very relation (predication, conjunction, disjunction) that defines those types. So how can they still be called by those names?
4
Analyze the author's response
The author replies that the names are applied by conventional meaning (mafhūm iṣṭilāḥī), not literal language. For example, 'categorical' conventionally means a proposition with two singular terms—this applies to both 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing'. Similarly, the conventional meaning of 'conditional' focuses on the form, not the presence of connection.
The footnote adds that the conventional meaning of categorical is 'a proposition whose two terms are singular, actually or potentially'—equally true for affirmative and negative.

If the judgment in it is the negation of incompatibility in truth only, it is a 'negative exclusive conjunction' as in our statement: 'It is not the case that either this human is an animal or black' — for it is possible for them to combine and not possible for both to be absent. If the judgment in it is the negation of incompatibility in falsehood only, it is a 'negative exclusive disjunction' as in our statement: 'It is not the case that either this human is Roman or Zanj' — for it is possible for both to be absent but not for them to combine. It cannot be said: The negative categoricals, conjunctives, and disjunctives — according to what you have mentioned — are those in which the predication, conjunction, and disjunction are removed, so they are not categorical, conjunctive, or disjunctive, because they are those in which predication, conjunction, and disjunction are affirmed. So we respond: The application of these names to negatives is not according to the linguistic meaning, but according to convention, and their conventional meanings apply to affirmatives just as they apply to negatives. Rather, we say that the application of 'conditional' to the disjunctive is also according to the conventional meaning, just as its application to the conjunctive, even if the linguistic meaning of 'conditional' is not apparent in the disjunctive.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of negative conditionals: the negative exclusive conjunction (saliba mani'at al-jam') and the negative exclusive disjunction (saliba mani'at al-khuluww). The first is when the judgment denies compatibility in truth only, meaning both cannot be false but can be true together; the example given is 'It is not the case that either this human is an animal or black' — this allows both to be true (the human is an animal and black) but not both false (since it must be at least one). The second is when the judgment denies compatibility in falsehood only, meaning both cannot be false but can be absent; the example is 'It is not the case that either this human is Roman or Zanj' — this allows both to be false (the human is neither) but not both true (cannot be both Roman and Zanj). An objection is then raised: negative categoricals, conjunctives, and disjunctives remove the very relation (predication, conjunction, disjunction) that defines those types, so how can they be called by those names? The author answers that the names are applied by convention (istilah), not by literal language. The conventional meaning of 'categorical' is simply 'a proposition with two terms', which applies equally to affirmative and negative. Similarly, the conventional meanings of 'conjunctive' and 'disjunctive' focus on the form, not the presence of connection. The footnote adds that the conventional meaning of categorical is a proposition with two singular terms (actual or potential), and this meaning holds for both affirmative and negative examples. This defense shows how the logical tradition uses technical terms consistently across affirmative and negative forms, based on shared structural features rather than literal meaning.

Key Terms
سالبة مانعة الجمع (sāliba māni‘at al-jam‘): A negative disjunctive proposition that denies incompatibility in truth only, allowing both conjuncts to be true but not both false.
سالبة مانعة الخلو (sāliba māni‘at al-khuluw): A negative disjunctive proposition that denies incompatibility in falsehood only, allowing both conjuncts to be false but not both true.
مفهوم اصطلاحي (mafhūm iṣṭilāḥī): Conventional meaning of a term as defined by logicians, as opposed to its literal linguistic meaning.

Key Takeaway
Negative conditionals are called conditionals by conventional meaning, not literal language, and they come in two types: those that deny incompatibility in truth (exclusive) and those that deny in falsehood (non-exclusive).

Correcting a Misunderstanding: Why Conditionals Are Discussed
Author corrects a misunderstanding and defends inclusion of conditionals in primary divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for including conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals as subdivisions under the primary division of propositions.

Core Idea
Primary divisions are categorical and conditional; subdivisions clarify the concept of each.

In the study of propositions, logicians first divide them into categorical and conditional. But then they immediately discuss affirmative/negative (in categoricals) and conjunctive/disjunctive (in conditionals). This might seem to add secondary divisions prematurely. In this passage, the author addresses a potential misunderstanding and explains that these subdivisions are not additional primary divisions but essential explanations to capture the full meaning of the primary types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the potential misunderstanding about naming
The passage begins by noting a possible error: someone might think that names like 'proposition' are applied to affirmatives based on linguistic meaning because they are not applied to negatives that way. The author corrects this: both are named conventionally.
2
Analyze the objection about primary divisions
An objector argues that conjunctive and disjunctive are not primary divisions since they fall under conditional. The author responds that the primary intention is to list primary divisions; mentioning subdivisions is incidental (bi al-'arad) and by way of digression (istitrad) to fully define the conditional.
3
Examine why subdivisions are necessary
The author explains that the concepts of categorical and conditional are not fully grasped without mentioning their subdivisions: affirmative/negative for categorical, and conjunctive/disjunctive (with types) for conditional. This is a rational exhaustive division (hasr 'aqli) that leaves no other possibility.
This shows that Islamic logicians prioritise conceptual completeness in classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بل إجراء هذه الأسامي عليهما معاً بحسب المفهوم الاصطلاحي قطعاً؛

(1) قد يتوهم من هذه العبارة أنهم أطلقوا هذه الأسامي على الموجبات أولاً لتحقق المعاني اللغوية فيها، ثم نقلوها منها إلى السوالب لمشابهتها للموجبات في الأطراف، والظاهر أنهم نقلوا هذه الأسامي من المعاني اللغوية إلى المفهومات الاصطلاحية بناء على وجود المناسبة في بعض أفراد هذه المفهومات - أعني الموجبات - فإن هذا القدر من المناسبة كاف في صحة النقل، فلاحاجة إلى التزام النقل مرتين (شريف). (2) الأقسام الأولية هي الحمليّة والشرطية، وإنما ذكر الموجبة والسالبة في الحملية على سبيل التبعية لأن مفهوم الحملية ينضبط بذكرهما، وكذا ذكر المتصلة والمنفصلة هاهنا، لأنهما حقيقتان مختلفتان مندرجتان تحت الشرطية، فلا يتحصل مفهومها إلا بهما.
واعتبر في المتصلة الإيجاب والسلب لما ذكرنا في الحملية، وذكر في المنفصلة أنواعها المختلفة لتنضبط وأشير إلى الإيجاب والسلب في جميعها لما ذكرنا. واعلم أن انقسام القضية إلى الحملية والشرطية حصر عقلي، وأما
It might be misunderstood from his statement 'The application of these names to negatives is not according to linguistic meaning' that their application to affirmatives is according to linguistic meaning, but it is not so; rather, the application of these names to both is definitely according to conventional meaning. It cannot be said: The premise was composed for mentioning the primary divisions of the proposition, and the conjunctive and disjunctive are not from the primary divisions, but from the divisions of their section, namely the conditional. So we respond: There is no doubt that the intended purpose of composing the premise is mentioning the primary divisions, and mentioning the divisions of the conditional in it is incidental and by way of digression. (2) So the more apparent in expression is to say: 'The application of these names to these propositions is not according to linguistic meaning' (Sharif). (1) It might be misunderstood from this statement that they first applied these names to affirmatives due to the realization of the linguistic meanings in them, then transferred them from there to negatives due to their resemblance to affirmatives in the terms; but the apparent view is that they transferred these names from linguistic meanings to conventional meanings based on the presence of appropriateness in some individuals of these meanings — namely affirmatives — for this amount of appropriateness suffices for the validity of the transfer, so there is no need to adhere to a double transfer (Sharif). (2) The primary divisions are the categorical and conditional, and the mention of affirmative and negative in the categorical is by way of subordination because the concept of categorical is defined by mentioning them; similarly, the mention of conjunctive and disjunctive here is because they are two different realities subsumed under the conditional, so their concept is not obtained except through them. Consider in the conjunctive affirmation and negation for what we mentioned in the categorical, and mention in the disjunctive its various types to be defined, and refer to affirmation and negation in all of them for what we mentioned. Know that the division of the proposition into categorical and conditional is a rational enumeration, and as for
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage begins by correcting a potential misunderstanding: someone might think that the earlier statement (names are not applied to negatives by linguistic meaning) implies that they are applied to affirmatives by linguistic meaning. The author clarifies that both affirmatives and negatives are named by conventional meaning. Then a new objection is raised: the premise (muqaddima) was intended to list the primary divisions of the proposition, but conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals are secondary divisions, not primary. The author responds that the primary intention is indeed the primary divisions; mentioning the subdivisions of conditionals is incidental and by way of digression. The Sharif (al-Jurjani) notes that a clearer expression would be 'the application of these names to these propositions is not according to linguistic meaning'. Another footnote from Sharif refines the understanding of the transfer: it is not a two-step transfer (first to affirmatives based on linguistic meaning, then to negatives by analogy), but rather a single transfer from linguistic meanings to conventional meanings, based on the appropriateness found in some individuals (the affirmatives), which is sufficient. The final paragraph explains why the subdivision is necessary: the primary divisions are categorical and conditional. In categoricals, affirmative and negative are mentioned because the concept of categorical is defined by them; similarly, conjunctive and disjunctive are mentioned as subdivisions because they are distinct realities under conditional, and the concept of conditional is only grasped through them. So the logical classification is exhaustive: the proposition is divided rationally into categorical and conditional, and each is further divided by quality (affirmation/negation) and type (conjunctive/disjunctive with their subforms). This shows the systematic nature of Islamic logic in organizing knowledge.

المناسبة
حصر عقلي

D) They are mentioned because the author forgot to list them separately.

Key Takeaway
The division of propositions into categorical and conditional is rationally exhaustive; their subdivisions are explained to clarify the concepts, not as additional primary divisions.

Defending the Inclusion of Conditional Subdivisions
Commentator defends the inclusion of conditional subdivisions in primary division discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection and the commentator's response in the debate over whether conjunctive and disjunctive propositions belong to primary divisions.

Core Idea
Primary divisions are only categorical and conditional; subdivisions are mentioned incidentally for definitional completeness.

In Islamic logic, the classification of propositions into primary divisions is foundational. However, a subtle objection arises: why are conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals discussed here if they are not primary? The commentator defends this inclusion as necessary for defining the conditional itself. This lesson guides you through the dialectical exchange and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector asserts that the introduction was meant for primary divisions only, so conjunctive and disjunctive should not be included.
Look for the phrase 'لا يقال' which introduces the objection.
2
Locate the commentator's response
The commentator admits the primary purpose is to list primary divisions, but subdivisions are mentioned incidentally (بالعرض) and by way of digression (على سبيل الاستطراد).
The response begins with 'لانا نقول'.
3
Analyze justification for inclusion
The concept of the conditional cannot be obtained without mentioning its two types (conjunctive and disjunctive), so they are included for conceptual necessity.
The commentator emphasizes that these are 'حقيقتان مختلفتان' under the conditional.
4
Understand the naming mechanism
Names are not applied based on linguistic meaning but are transferred from language to technical usage based on appropriateness.
This point addresses a potential misunderstanding about the naming of propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : المقدّمة كانت معقودة لذكر أقسام القضية الأولية، والمتصلة والمنفصلة ليست من الأقسام الأولية، بل من أقسام قسمها - أعني الشرطية. لانا نقول : لاشك أن المقصود بالذات من وضع المقدّمة ذكر الأقسام الأولية، وأما ذكر أقسام الشرطيّة فيها فبالعرض وعلى سبيل الاستطراد فالأظهر في العبارة أن يقال : ليس إطلاق هذه الأسامي على هذه القضايا بحسب مفهوم اللغة» (شريف). (۱) قد يتوهم من هذه العبارة أنهم أطلقوا هذه الأسامي على الموجبات أولاً لتحقق المعاني اللغوية فيها، ثم نقلوها منها إلى السوالب لمشابهتها للموجبات في الأطراف، والظاهر أنهم نقلوا هذه الأسامي من المعاني اللغوية إلى المفهومات الاصطلاحية بناء على وجود المناسبة في بعض أفراد هذه المفهومات - أعني الموجبات - فإن هذا القدر من المناسبة كاف في صحة النقل، فلاحاجة إلى التزام النقل مرتين (شريف). (۲) الأقسام الأولية هي الحمليّة والشرطية، وإنما ذكر الموجبة والسالبة في الحملية على سبيل التبعية لأن مفهوم الحملية ينضبط بذكرهما، وكذا ذكر المتصلة والمنفصلة هاهنا، لأنهما حقيقتان مختلفتان مندرجتان تحت الشرطية، فلا يتحصل مفهومها إلا بهما. واعتبر في المتصلة الإيجاب والسلب لما ذكرنا في الحملية، وذكر في المنفصلة أنواعها المختلفة لتنضبط وأشير إلى الإيجاب والسلب في جميعها لما ذكرنا.
It is not said: The introduction was devoted to mentioning the primary divisions of the proposition, and the conjunctive and disjunctive are not among the primary divisions, but rather among the divisions of its subdivision – namely the conditional. For we say: There is no doubt that the intended purpose of presenting the introduction is to mention the primary divisions, and as for mentioning the divisions of the conditional therein, it is incidental and by way of digression. So the most apparent in expression is to say: The application of these names to these propositions is not according to the linguistic meaning. (Sharif). (1) One might misunderstand from this expression that they first applied these names to affirmatives due to the realization of the linguistic meanings in them, then transferred them from there to negatives due to their similarity to affirmatives in the terms. But the apparent is that they transferred these names from the linguistic meanings to the technical concepts based on the existence of appropriateness in some individuals of these concepts – namely the affirmatives. For this amount of appropriateness suffices for the validity of the transfer, so there is no need to assume two transfers. (Sharif). (2) The primary divisions are the categorical and the conditional. The affirmative and negative were mentioned in the categorical only by way of subordination because the concept of the categorical is defined by mentioning them. Likewise, the conjunctive and disjunctive were mentioned here because they are two different realities subsumed under the conditional, so its concept is not obtained except through them. And in the conjunctive, positivity and negativity were considered for what we mentioned in the categorical, and in the disjunctive, its different types were mentioned to be defined, and positivity and negativity were indicated in all of them for what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange defending why conditional propositions (conjunctive and disjunctive) are discussed in the context of primary divisions of propositions. An objector claims that only categorical and conditional are primary divisions, so conjunctive/disjunctive should not be included. The commentator (possibly the author or Sharif) responds that the primary focus is indeed the two primary divisions (categorical and conditional), but the subdivisions of conditional are mentioned tangentially for completeness. The commentary then clarifies the method of naming: the terms like 'affirmative' and 'negative' are transferred from linguistic usage to technical terms based on appropriateness, not by multiple transfers. Finally, it explains that the primary divisions are categorical and conditional; within categorical, affirmative/negative are mentioned to define it, and within conditional, conjunctive/disjunctive are mentioned because they are distinct sub-types needed to define the conditional. The commentator emphasizes that the classification is not arbitrary but follows conceptual necessity.

Transfer of Logical Terms from Linguistic to Technical Usage
Commentator explains the theory of term transfer in logical classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process by which logical terms are transferred from linguistic meanings to technical concepts, and why a single transfer suffices.

Core Idea
Names are transferred to technical usage based on appropriateness in some individuals, not by multiple steps.

The commentator clarifies that the application of names like 'affirmative' and 'negative' to propositions is not based on their original linguistic meanings but on a transfer (naql) from language to logic. This lesson unpacks the logic of that transfer and its implications for how we understand logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception being corrected
One might think terms were first applied to affirmatives due to linguistic meaning, then transferred to negatives by similarity.
Look for the phrase 'قد يتوهم' which signals a potential misunderstanding.
2
Understand the correct view
The correct view is that terms are transferred directly from linguistic meanings to technical concepts based on appropriateness found in some individuals (the affirmatives).
The commentator says 'الظاهر أنهم نقلوا هذه الأسامي من المعاني اللغوية إلى المفهومات الاصطلاحية بناء على وجود المناسبة في بعض أفراد هذه المفهومات'.
3
Recognize why a single transfer suffices
The appropriateness in affirmatives is sufficient to justify the transfer; there is no need for a second transfer to negatives because the technical concept includes both.
The commentator explicitly says 'فلا حاجة إلى التزام النقل مرتين'.
4
Apply to other terms
This principle of transfer applies to many logical and fiqh terms, such as 'wajib' or 'haram' in fiqh.
Think of how linguistic meanings ground legal terminology.

5 min
Parts of a Categorical Proposition
Author defines the categorical proposition and lists its three essential parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential parts of a categorical proposition and distinguish between triple and double propositions.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition needs a subject, predicate, and copula, even if the copula is hidden.

This lesson introduces the foundational building block of categorical logic: the proposition itself. You will learn the three necessary components—subject, predicate, and copula—and see how they combine to form a complete categorical proposition. Understanding this structure is key to analyzing and constructing arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts
Find the three components in a categorical proposition: the subject (what is being talked about), the predicate (what is said about the subject), and the copula (the linking word like 'is' or 'is not').
2
Understand the copula's role
The copula expresses the relation (affirmation or negation) between subject and predicate. In Arabic, it is often the word 'huwa' (هو) but can be omitted in some languages.
In some languages, the copula is dropped because the mind infers the relation.
3
Distinguish triple vs double
A triple proposition explicitly includes the copula; a double proposition omits it. Both are valid; the difference is only linguistic explicitness.
4
Apply to an example
Take the proposition 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. Identify subject (Zayd), predicate (knowledgeable), copula (is). This is a triple proposition. Now consider 'Zayd knowledgeable'—the copula is omitted, making it a double proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية واقسامها [٤٢] قال : الفصل الأول في العملية وفيه أربعة مباحث: البحث الأول في أجزائها وأقسامها: الحملية إنما تتحقق بأجزاء ثلاثة محكوم عليه ويسمّى «موضوعاً»، و محکوم به ويسمى «محمولا»، و نسبة بينهما بها يرتبط المحمول بالموضوع واللفظ الدال عليها يسمّى «رابطة - كـ«هو» في قولنا «زيد هو عالم». وتسمى القضية حينئذ «ثلاثية»، وقد تحذف الرابطة في بعض اللغات لشعور الذهن بمعناها والقضيّة تسمى حينئذ «ثنائية».
The proposition and its sections [42] He said: The first chapter on the categorical proposition, and it contains four investigations: The first investigation on its parts and sections: The categorical proposition is realized by three parts: that which is judged upon and is called 'subject', that which is judged with and is called 'predicate', and a relation between them by which the predicate is connected to the subject, and the word indicating it is called 'copula' – like 'huwa' in our statement 'Zayd is knowledgeable'. Then the proposition is called 'triple', and the copula may be omitted in some languages due to the mind's awareness of its meaning, and then the proposition is called 'double'.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a definitional passage from the beginning of the first investigation on the categorical proposition. It provides a formal definition: a categorical proposition necessarily has three parts: the subject (that which is judged about), the predicate (that which is judged of the subject), and the copula (the relation linking them). The example 'Zayd is knowledgeable' illustrates these: 'Zayd' is the subject, 'knowledgeable' is the predicate, and 'is' (huwa) is the copula. A proposition explicitly containing all three is called 'triple'. However, in some languages, the copula may be dropped because the mind intuitively understands the relation, and such a proposition is called 'double' (only subject and predicate). This distinction is foundational for understanding the structure of categorical propositions in logic.

D) Both subject and predicate

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition always has three parts—subject, predicate, and copula—even if the copula is not explicitly stated.

The Inductive Basis of Conditional Division
Commentator argues that the division of conditional propositions is based on induction, not a priori reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the inductive basis for the division of conditional propositions and explain why the commentator considers it non-exhaustive a priori.

Core Idea
The division of conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive is based on observation, not logical necessity.

Logic is often thought to be purely a priori, but this passage reveals that even basic classifications in logic can rest on empirical observation. The commentator here challenges the assumption that the division of conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive is logically exhaustive. By arguing that this division is inductive, he opens the door for other possible relations and underscores the role of empirical input in logical classifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
The commentator is responding to a prior author who divided conditional propositions into conjunctive and disjunctive. Identify what is being critiqued: the claim that this division is exhaustive.
2
Extract the commentator's reasoning
The commentator argues that in a conditional, the relation between its two component propositions cannot be predication (as in categoricals). He then asserts that there is no necessary reason why this relation must be only conjunction or disjunction; other relations could exist. Trace this logical step.
3
Recognize the inductive claim
The commentator says the division is 'inductive' (istiqrāʾiyya) because it is based on observing that no other relation has been found in the sciences or common language. Understand that this means the division is empirical, not a priori.
Induction here is incomplete induction: because we haven't seen counterexamples, we assume the division holds.
4
Assess the epistemological implication
Consider why this matters: if logical divisions can be inductive, then logic is not entirely independent of empirical observation. This challenges a view of logic as pure reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : لما قسم القضية إلى الحمليّة والشرطية شرع الآن في انقسام الشرطية إلى المتصلة والمنفصلة فليس كذلك، لأن الشرطية طرفاها قضيتان بالقوة القريبة من الفعل، والنسبة بين القضيتين لا يمكن أن تكون يحمل إحداهما على الأخري، بل لابد أن تكون هناك نسبة غير الحمل، ولا يلزم أن تكون النسبة التي هي غير الحمل منحصرة في الاتصال والانفصال، لجواز أن تكون بوجه آخر، فهذه القسمة استقرائية إذ لم توجد في العلوم ومتعارف اللغة نسبة بوجه آخر معتبرة بين أطراف القضايا (شريف).
I say: When he divided the proposition into categorical and conditional, he then proceeded to divide the conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive, but it is not so. Because the two terms of the conditional are two propositions in potentiality close to actuality, and the relation between the two propositions cannot be by predicating one of them of the other. Rather, there must be a relation other than predication, and it is not necessary that the relation which is other than predication be restricted to conjunction and disjunction, for it is possible that it be in another way. So this division is inductive, since there has not been found in the sciences or in common linguistic usage another relation between the terms of propositions that is considered (Sharif).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological critique by the commentator (perhaps Sharif) regarding the division of conditional propositions. The author of the original text divided conditionals into conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The commentator argues that this division is not exhaustive a priori. His reasoning: a conditional proposition has two terms that are propositions themselves. The relation between them cannot be one of predication (as in categoricals); it must be a different kind of relation. There is no rational necessity that this relation be only of two types (conjunction or disjunction); other kinds of relations could exist. The reason logicians only mention these two is that they have not observed any other type of relation in actual sciences or common language. Therefore, the division is based on induction (istiqra'), not a logical necessity. This point highlights the empirical basis of logical classifications.

Key Terms
الشرطية
المتصلة
المنفصلة
الاستقرائية
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the division of conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive not exhaust all possibilities?
A) Because there are more than two types of conditionals in logic.
B) Because the relation between the two parts could be something other than conjunction or disjunction.
C) Because conditionals can be reduced to categoricals.
D) Because language is not a reliable guide.

Key Takeaway
Logical divisions can be based on induction from observed usage, not only on a priori reasoning, meaning they are open to revision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Find an example in Islamic jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh) where a classification (e.g., of qiyas or of causes) is based on inductive observation from the Quran and Sunnah rather than a priori reason. Write a short paragraph explaining the similarity to the commentator's point.

The Three Parts of a Categorical Proposition
Author defines subject, predicate, and copula and resolves objection about a fourth part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential parts of a categorical proposition (subject, predicate, copula) and explain why the copula suffices for both the relation and the judgment, thereby refuting the objection of a fourth part.

Core Idea
The copula carries both the relation and the judgment, making the proposition three-part.

This lesson unpacks the foundational structure of any categorical proposition. The author first names the three parts—subject, predicate, and the relation linking them—then anticipates a subtle objection: shouldn't the act of judging (affirmation or negation) be a separate part? The resolution hinges on what the copula actually expresses. By the end, you will see why the traditional three-part analysis is both elegant and sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three named parts
Locate the three parts the author explicitly lists: the subject (المحكوم عليه / الموضوع), the predicate (المحكوم به / المحمول), and the relation between them (النسبة الحكمية). Note that the relation is also called the copula when expressed by a word like 'huwa'.
The author says the proposition 'is composed of three parts' (تلتئم من أجزاء ثلاثة).
2
Understand the objection that introduces a fourth part
The objection says: if 'judgmental relation' means the bare relation, then the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation (the judgment itself) would be a fourth part. If 'judgmental relation' means the occurrence/non-occurrence, then the bare relation becomes a separate part. Either way, we seem to have four parts.
The objector is pushing for a precise distinction between the relation and the act of judging.
3
Follow the author's resolution
The author chooses the second interpretation: 'judgmental relation' here means the relation considered together with occurrence (or non-occurrence). He argues that the copula (e.g., 'huwa') simultaneously indicates both the relation and its occurrence. Therefore, one word does the work of two concepts, reducing the count back to three.
The phrase 'by which the predicate is connected to the subject' is taken as a clue that occurrence is already implied.
4
Check the copula's nature
The author notes the copula is a particle (أداة) because it depends on the subject and predicate, but it may appear as a noun like 'huwa'. Confirm that in Arabic, the copula is often implied or expressed, and 'huwa' serves as a copula in example 'Zayd is knowledgeable'.
In Islamic logic, the copula is the key to turning a mere concept-pair into a judgment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمليّات، وإنما قدمها على الشرطيات لبساطتها - والبسيط مقدم على المركب طبعاً. فالحملية إنما تلتئم من أجزاء ثلاثة : المحكوم عليه، ويسمّى «موضوعاً»، لأنه قد وضع ليحكم عليه والمحكوم به ويسمى «محمولاً» لحمله على شيء. ونسبة بينهما بها يرتبط المحمول بالموضوع، وتسمى «نسبة حكمية» وكما أن من حق الموضوع والمحمول أن يعبر عنهما بلفظين، كذلك من حق النسبة الحكميّة أن يُدلّ عليها بلفظ، واللفظ الدال عليها يسمّى رابطة» لدلالتها على النسبة الرابطة، تسمية للدال باسم المدلول، كـ«هو» في قولنا : «زيد هو عالم». فإن قلت : المراد بالنسبة الحكميّة إما النسبة التي هي مورد الإيجاب والسلب، وإما وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها الذي هو الإيجاب والسلب، فإن كان المراد بها الأول يكون للقضية جزء آخر، وهو وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها ، فلابد أن يُدلّ عليها بعبارة أخرى؛ وإن كان المراد الثاني كان النسبة التي هي مورد الإيجاب والسلب جزء آخر فليدلّ عليها أيضاً بلفظ ، والحاصل أن أجزاء الحمليّة أربعة ) ، فكان من حقها أن يُدلّ عليها بأربعة ألفاظ. فنقول : المراد الثاني، وكان قوله « بها يرتبط المحمول بالموضوع» إشارة إليه، فإن النسبة مالم يعتبر معها الوقوع واللاوقوع لم تكن رابطة ولاحاجة إلى الدلالة على النسبة التي هي مورد الإيجاب والسلب، فإن اللفظ الدال على وقوع النسبة دال على النسبة أيضاً، فالجزآن من القضية يتأديان بعبارة واحدة، لهذا أخذا جزء واحداً حتى انحصر الأجزاء في ثلاثة، ثمّ الرابطة أداة لأنّها تدلّ على النسبة الرابطة، وهي غير مستقلة لتوقفها على المحكوم عليه وبه، لكنها قد تكون في قالب الاسم - كهو في المثال
Categorical propositions: the author introduced them before conditional ones because of their simplicity—the simple is naturally prior to the compound. The categorical proposition is composed of three parts: that which is judged upon, called 'subject' because it is placed for judgment; that which is judged with, called 'predicate' because it is carried on something; and a relation between them by which the predicate is connected to the subject, called 'judgmental relation.' Just as it is proper for the subject and predicate to be expressed by two words, so it is proper for the judgmental relation to be indicated by a word, and the word indicating it is called 'copula' because it indicates the connecting relation—naming the signifier by the name of the signified—like 'huwa' in our statement 'Zayd is knowledgeable.' If you object: what is meant by the judgmental relation? Either the relation that is the locus of affirmation and negation, or the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation, which is affirmation and negation. If the first is meant, then the proposition has another part, namely the occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation, so it must be indicated by another expression. If the second is meant, then the relation that is the locus of affirmation and negation is another part, so it too must be indicated by a word. The conclusion would be that the parts of the categorical are four, so it would be proper for them to be indicated by four words. We say: the second is meant, and his statement 'by which the predicate is connected to the subject' indicates that. For the relation, as long as occurrence and non-occurrence are not considered with it, is not a copula, and there is no need to indicate the relation that is the locus of affirmation and negation. Indeed, the word indicating occurrence of the relation also indicates the relation itself. So the two parts of the proposition are discharged by one expression; therefore they are taken as one part until the parts are limited to three. Moreover, the copula is a particle because it indicates the connecting relation, and it is not independent due to its dependence on the subject and predicate, but it may be in the form of a noun—like 'huwa' in the example.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage defines the essential components of a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya) and addresses a potential objection about the number of parts. The author begins by stating that categorical propositions are simpler than conditional ones, hence they are discussed first. The categorical proposition is composed of three parts: the subject (al-mahkum 'alayhi, called mawdu'), the predicate (al-mahkum bihi, called mahmul), and a relation between them called the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya). Just as subject and predicate are expressed by separate words, the relation must also be indicated by a word, which is called the copula (rabita), e.g., 'huwa' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable.' The author then presents an objection: the term 'judgmental relation' could refer either to the bare relation that is the locus of affirmation/negation, or to the affirmation/negation itself (i.e., occurrence or non-occurrence of the relation). If the first, then the proposition would require a fourth part (the occurrence/non-occurrence), making four parts total. If the second, then the bare relation becomes a separate part, again four parts. The author resolves this by stating that the second meaning is intended. The phrase 'by which the predicate is connected to the subject' refers to the relation considered with occurrence. The copula indicates the occurrence of the relation, and in doing so it also indicates the relation itself. Thus, the relation and its occurrence are expressed together by one word (the copula), reducing the parts to three. The copula is a particle (ada) because it depends on the subject and predicate, though it may take the form of a noun like 'huwa'.

قَضِيَّة حَمْلِيَّة

Key Takeaway
The categorical proposition has three essential parts; the copula expresses both the relation and the judgment, eliminating the need for a fourth part.

Propositions

8 min
The Four Parts of a Categorical Proposition and the Copula's Role
Author defines the four parts and resolves them to three via the copula.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four proposed parts of a categorical proposition and explain why the preferred view reduces them to three by merging the copula with the relation-occurrence.

Core Idea
The copula combines relation and its assertion into one logical component.

Understanding the parts of a categorical proposition is foundational for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic. This passage unpacks a key debate: whether the copula is a separate part or unifies relation and occurrence. We will trace the reasoning that leads to the accepted three-part structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposed parts
From the passage, list the four parts: subject (mawdu'), predicate (mahmul), relation (nisba) between them, and the occurrence (wuqu') or non-occurrence (la wuqu') of that relation, which is affirmation (ijab) or negation (salb).
2
Understand the problem with four parts
If each part requires a distinct word, a proposition would need four words. The author questions whether the relation and its occurrence are separate or can be expressed together.
3
Learn the preferred solution
The author argues that the copula (rabita) inherently includes both the relation and its occurrence. Thus, the word indicating occurrence also indicates the relation, reducing distinct parts to three: subject, predicate, and copula.
This is the 'al-murad al-thani' (second intention) mentioned in the text.
4
Apply to an example
Take the proposition 'The world is created.' Subject: the world. Predicate: created. Copula: 'is' (implied or explicit). The copula conveys both the connection and the affirmation. Only three logical parts exist.
This matches the standard tripartite analysis in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية وأقسامها
والسلب، وإما وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها الذي هو الإيجاب والسلب، فإن كان المراد بها الأول يكون للقضية جزء آخر، وهو وقوع النسبة أو لا وقوعها ، فلابد أن يُدلّ عليها بعبارة أخرى؛ وإن كان المراد الثاني كان النسبة التي هي مورد الإيجاب والسلب جزء آخر فليدلّ عليها أيضاً بلفظ آخر، والحاصل أن أجزاء الحمليّة أربعة ) ، فكان من حقها أن يُدلّ عليها بأربعة ألفاظ.
فنقول : المراد الثاني، وكان قوله « بها يرتبط المحمول بالموضوع» إشارة إليه، فإن النسبة مالم يعتبر معها الوقوع واللاوقوع لم تكن رابطة ولاحاجة إلى الدلالة على النسبة التي هي مورد الإيجاب والسلب، فإن اللفظ الدال على وقوع النسبة دال على النسبة أيضاً، فالجزآن من القضية يتأديان بعبارة واحدة، لهذا أخذا جزء واحداً حتى انحصر الأجزاء في ثلاثة، ثمّ الرابطة أداة لأنّها تدلّ على النسبة الرابطة، وهي غير مستقلة لتوقفها على المحكوم عليه وبه، لكنها قد تكون في قالب الاسم - كهو في المثال المذكور) - وتسمّى غير زمانيّة، وقد تكون في قالب الكلمة - كـ«كان» في قولنا : «زيد كان قائماً» وتسمى زمانية.
والقضية الحملية باعتبار الرابطة إما ثنائية أو ثلاثية، لأنها إن ذكرت فيها الرابطة كانت ثلاثية لاشتمالها على ثلاثة ألفاظ لثلاثة معان، وإن حذفت - لشعور الذهن بمعناها - كانت ثنائية لعدم اشتمالها إلا على جزأين بإزاء معنيين.
وقوله : «وقد تحذف في بعض اللغات» إشارة إلى أن اللغات مختلفة في استعمال الرابطة ، فإن لغة العرب ربما تستعمل الرابطة وربما تحذفها بشهادة القرائن الدالّة عليها، ولغة اليونان توجب الرابطة الزمانية.
(شریف): حالة بينهما وآلة لتعرّف حالهما، فلاتكون معنى مستقلا يصلح لأن يكون محكوماً عليه أوبه، فاللفظ الدال عليها يكون أداة.
(شريف): قد يناقش في ذلك بأن لفظ «هو» في «زيد هو عالم» يدلّ على زيد لأنه ضمير راجع إليه فلايكون رابطة، ويقال الرابطة في هذه القضية هي حركة الرفع لأنها دالة على الارتباط والاستناد، والدليل عليه أن المفردات إذا ذكرت موقوفة الأواخر نحو «زيد» لم يحصل التركيب ولا يفيد الاسناد، وقد تكون في قالب الكلمة ككان الناقصة وما يتصرّف منها، وتسمّى زمانية لدلالتها على الزمان، بخلاف لفظ هو وأخواتها، إذ لا دلالة لها على الزمان أصلاً. وقد نوقش هاهنا أيضاً بأن مدلول «كان» زائد على مدلول الرابطة لدلالة «كان» على الزمان الذي لا مدخل له في الربط.
(شریف): قيل : وجه الضبط أن يقال : هاهنا ثلاثة أشياء : «الوجوب والامتناع والجواز » فتضربها في ثلاثة أخرى هي مجموع الرابطتين معاً والرابطة الزمانية وحدها، وغير الزمانية وحدها - وفيه بعد لا يخفى.
The proposition and its divisions. And negation, and either the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, which is affirmation and negation. If what is meant by it is the first, then the proposition has another part, namely the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, so it must be indicated by another expression; and if what is meant is the second, then the relation which is the subject of affirmation and negation is another part, so it too must be indicated by another word. The conclusion is that the parts of the categorical [proposition] are four, so it would be fitting that they be indicated by four words. We say: the second is meant, and his statement 'by which the predicate is connected to the subject' is an indication of it. For the relation as long as occurrence and non-occurrence are not considered with it is not a copula, and there is no need to indicate the relation which is the subject of affirmation and negation, because the word indicating the occurrence of the relation also indicates the relation. So two parts of the proposition are conveyed by one expression, for this reason they are taken as one part until the parts are confined to three. Then the copula is a particle because it indicates the relating relation, and it is not independent, depending on the subject and predicate. But it may be in the form of a noun—like 'huwa' in the mentioned example—and is called non-temporal; and it may be in the form of a verb—like 'kana' in our saying 'Zayd was standing'—and is called temporal. The categorical proposition with respect to the copula is either bipartite or tripartite, because if the copula is mentioned in it, it is tripartite, containing three words for three meanings; and if it is omitted—due to the mind's awareness of its meaning—it is bipartite, containing only two parts corresponding to two meanings. His statement 'and it may be omitted in some languages' is an indication that languages differ in the use of the copula. For the Arabic language sometimes uses the copula and sometimes omits it, as evidenced by the indicators that point to it, while the Greek language requires the temporal copula.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the parts of a categorical proposition and the nature of the copula. The author begins by examining the possible interpretations of the term 'proposition' (qadiyya) and its components. He notes that some consider the proposition to have four parts: the subject, the predicate, the relation between them, and the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation (which corresponds to affirmation or negation). However, he argues that if the occurrence or non-occurrence is taken as a separate part, then the relation itself becomes another part, requiring four distinct expressions. But the preferred view (al-murad al-thani) is that the relation without the occurrence/non-occurrence is not a copula; rather, the copula includes both the relation and its occurrence. Thus, the word indicating occurrence also indicates the relation, so two parts are expressed by one word, reducing the parts to three: subject, predicate, and copula/relation. The copula is further described as an instrument (ada) because it signifies the connecting relation and is not independent, needing the subject and predicate. The copula can appear in two forms: as a noun (like 'huwa' in 'Zayd is standing') — called non-temporal, or as a verb (like 'kana' in 'Zayd was standing') — called temporal, indicating time. Based on the copula's presence or absence, the categorical proposition is classified as tripartite (three words for three meanings) if the copula is stated, or bipartite (two words for two meanings) if the copula is omitted because the mind grasps its meaning. The author then notes that languages differ: Arabic occasionally omits the copula when context suffices, while Greek always requires the temporal copula. The commentary includes glosses (by Sharif) that discuss objections and refinements, such as whether 'huwa' serves as a copula or a pronoun, and whether 'kana' adds temporal meaning beyond the copula. These nuances highlight the precision required in logical analysis. The passage concludes by noting a method for classifying modalities (obligation, impossibility, contingency) with respect to different copular forms, though the author considers it far-fetched.

Key Terms
قضية حملية
رابطة
نسبة
إيجاب وسلب

B) Three: subject, predicate, and the copula which includes relation and occurrence.
C) Two: subject and predicate only.
D) Five: subject, predicate, relation, occurrence, and the copula.

Key Takeaway
The copula is not an extra connector; it inherently carries the assertion, reducing the proposition's essential parts to three.

Propositions

Types of Copula and Proposition Structure
Author categorizes copulas and propositions based on copula presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish temporal from non-temporal copulas and determine whether a categorical proposition is bipartite or tripartite based on copula mention.

Core Idea
Copular form (noun/verb) affects temporality and proposition length.

The copula can appear as a noun (like 'huwa') or a verb (like 'kana'), and its presence or absence splits propositions into two types. Moreover, languages differ in their use of the copula. We will examine these variations and the logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two copular forms
From the passage, note that the copula can be in the form of a noun (ism), e.g., 'huwa', or a verb (kalima), e.g., 'kana' or its derivatives.
2
Understand temporal vs non-temporal
The nominal copula (e.g., 'huwa') does not indicate time, so it is called non-temporal (ghair zamaniyya). The verbal copula (e.g., 'kana') indicates time, so it is called temporal (zamaniyya).
Al-Sharif later objects that 'huwa' may be a pronoun, and the real copula is the case ending.
3
Classify propositions by copula mention
If the copula is explicitly mentioned, the proposition is tripartite (three words: subject, predicate, copula). If omitted (e.g., in Arabic 'Zayd qaaim' without 'huwa'), it is bipartite (two words).
The mind supplies the copula from context.
4
Apply to language differences
The author notes that Arabic sometimes omits the copula, while Greek always requires the temporal copula. This shows that logical analysis must be sensitive to linguistic conventions.

(شریف): حالة بينهما وآلة لتعرّف حالهما، فلاتكون معنى مستقلا يصلح لأن يكون محكوماً عليه أوبه، فاللفظ الدال عليها يكون أداة.
