The proposition and its divisions. And negation, and either the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, which is affirmation and negation. If what is meant by it is the first, then the proposition has another part, namely the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, so it must be indicated by another expression; and if what is meant is the second, then the relation which is the subject of affirmation and negation is another part, so it too must be indicated by another word. The conclusion is that the parts of the categorical [proposition] are four, so it would be fitting that they be indicated by four words. We say: the second is meant, and his statement 'by which the predicate is connected to the subject' is an indication of it. For the relation as long as occurrence and non-occurrence are not considered with it is not a copula, and there is no need to indicate the relation which is the subject of affirmation and negation, because the word indicating the occurrence of the relation also indicates the relation. So two parts of the proposition are conveyed by one expression, for this reason they are taken as one part until the parts are confined to three. Then the copula is a particle because it indicates the relating relation, and it is not independent, depending on the subject and predicate. But it may be in the form of a noun—like 'huwa' in the mentioned example—and is called non-temporal; and it may be in the form of a verb—like 'kana' in our saying 'Zayd was standing'—and is called temporal. The categorical proposition with respect to the copula is either bipartite or tripartite, because if the copula is mentioned in it, it is tripartite, containing three words for three meanings; and if it is omitted—due to the mind's awareness of its meaning—it is bipartite, containing only two parts corresponding to two meanings. His statement 'and it may be omitted in some languages' is an indication that languages differ in the use of the copula. For the Arabic language sometimes uses the copula and sometimes omits it, as evidenced by the indicators that point to it, while the Greek language requires the temporal copula.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the parts of a categorical proposition and the nature of the copula. The author begins by examining the possible interpretations of the term 'proposition' (qadiyya) and its components. He notes that some consider the proposition to have four parts: the subject, the predicate, the relation between them, and the occurrence or non-occurrence of that relation (which corresponds to affirmation or negation). However, he argues that if the occurrence or non-occurrence is taken as a separate part, then the relation itself becomes another part, requiring four distinct expressions. But the preferred view (al-murad al-thani) is that the relation without the occurrence/non-occurrence is not a copula; rather, the copula includes both the relation and its occurrence. Thus, the word indicating occurrence also indicates the relation, so two parts are expressed by one word, reducing the parts to three: subject, predicate, and copula/relation. The copula is further described as an instrument (ada) because it signifies the connecting relation and is not independent, needing the subject and predicate. The copula can appear in two forms: as a noun (like 'huwa' in 'Zayd is standing') — called non-temporal, or as a verb (like 'kana' in 'Zayd was standing') — called temporal, indicating time. Based on the copula's presence or absence, the categorical proposition is classified as tripartite (three words for three meanings) if the copula is stated, or bipartite (two words for two meanings) if the copula is omitted because the mind grasps its meaning. The author then notes that languages differ: Arabic occasionally omits the copula when context suffices, while Greek always requires the temporal copula. The commentary includes glosses (by Sharif) that discuss objections and refinements, such as whether 'huwa' serves as a copula or a pronoun, and whether 'kana' adds temporal meaning beyond the copula. These nuances highlight the precision required in logical analysis. The passage concludes by noting a method for classifying modalities (obligation, impossibility, contingency) with respect to different copular forms, though the author considers it far-fetched.

رابطة غير زمانية

A) The word 'huwa'
B) The case ending (harakat al-raf') on 'Zayd' and 'qaaim'
C) The word 'qaaim'
D) There is no copula

Key Takeaway
The copula's lexical form (noun/verb) and presence affect proposition structure, but the true logical copula is the indicator of the relation-occurrence, which may be implicit.

Bipartite and Tripartite Categorical Propositions
Author defines bipartite and tripartite categorical propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given categorical proposition as bipartite or tripartite based on the presence or absence of the copula.

Core Idea
The copula can be hidden; the mind supplies it.

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental classification of categorical propositions: some are bipartite (without explicit copula) and some are tripartite (with explicit copula). This distinction is crucial because it reflects how Arabic and other languages handle the linking element that turns a simple juxtaposition into a meaningful assertion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
A categorical proposition consists of a subject (mubtada'), a predicate (khabar), and a copula (rabiṭa) that links them. The copula can be a word like kāna or implied.
2
Check for explicit copula
If the copula is explicitly mentioned (e.g., 'kāna' in 'Zayd kāna qā'iman'), the proposition is tripartite (thulāthiyya) because it has three distinct words for three meanings.
The author emphasizes that tripartite propositions contain three corresponding to three meanings.
3
Detect omitted copula
If the copula is omitted but understood by the mind (e.g., 'Zayd qā'im' meaning 'Zayd is standing'), the proposition is bipartite (thunā'iyya) as it only has two explicit parts.
The omission is possible due to contextual clues (qarā'in).
4
Apply to Arabic examples
In Arabic, many everyday propositions are bipartite. For instance, 'al-ʿālamu ḥādithun' is bipartite; 'al-ʿālamu kāna ḥādithan' is tripartite.
Practice identifying which is which.

دون غيرها - على ما نقله الشيخ - ولغة العجم لاتستعمل القضية خالية عنها - إما بلفظ كقولهم «هست» و «بود» - وإما بحركة - كقولهم «زيد دبير» بالكسر.
(۲) نقض ذلك بمثل قولهم : زيد دبير است و منجم» فإن قولهم «ومنجم» قضيّة خالية عن الرابطة (شريف).
The [mentioned] - and it is called non-temporal, and it may be in the form of a word - like 'kana' in our saying 'Zayd was standing' and it is called temporal. The categorical proposition, with respect to the copula, is either bipartite or tripartite, because if the copula is mentioned in it, it is tripartite due to its inclusion of three words for three meanings, and if it is omitted - due to the mind's awareness of its meaning - it is bipartite because it only includes two parts corresponding to two meanings. And his statement: 'And it is sometimes omitted in some languages' is an indication that languages differ in the use of the copula. For the Arabic language sometimes uses the copula and sometimes omits it, as evidenced by the indications pointing to it, and the Greek language requires the temporal copula as a state between them and an instrument for knowing their state, so it is not an independent meaning suitable to be judged upon or by it, so the word indicating it is a tool (Sharif). (1) It may be objected that the word 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa ʿalim' indicates Zayd because it is a pronoun referring back to him, so it is not a copula; and it is said the copula in this proposition is the vowel ending of the nominative case because it indicates connection and predication; the evidence is that if the individual words are mentioned with final vowels stopped, like 'Zayd', composition does not occur and predication is not useful. And it may be in the form of a word like the defective 'kana' and its derivatives, and it is called temporal because it indicates time, unlike the word 'huwa' and its sisters, since they have no indication of time at all. There is also discussion here that the meaning of 'kana' is additional to the meaning of the copula because 'kana' indicates time which has no entry in the connection (n: al-rabiṭ) (Sharif). (2) It is said: The way to adjust is to say: Here are three things: 'necessity, impossibility, and possibility'; then you multiply them by three others: the two copulas together, the temporal copula alone, and the non-temporal copula alone - and in it there is an apparent remoteness (Sharif). Without others - according to what the Shaykh transmitted - and the language of the non-Arabs does not use the proposition free from it - either with a word like their saying 'hast' and 'bud' - or with a vowel motion - like their saying 'Zayd dabīr' with a kasra. (2) That is refuted by example like their saying 'Zayd dabīr ast wa munajjim', for their saying 'wa munajjim' is a proposition free from the copula (Sharif).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of categorical propositions based on the copula (al-rabiṭa). The author first distinguishes between temporal and non-temporal copulas, giving 'kana' as an example of a temporal copula in 'Zayd was standing'. Then he explains that propositions are bipartite (thunā'iyya) when the copula is omitted and tripartite (thulāthiyya) when it is mentioned. The mind understands the copula even when omitted. He then discusses linguistic variation: Arabic often omits the copula due to contextual clues, while Greek requires a temporal copula. The marginal note from Sharif critiques the idea that 'huwa' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is a copula, arguing that it is a pronoun referring to Zayd, and the actual copula in Arabic is the vowel ending (ḥaraka al-rafʿ). Another note argues that 'kana' adds a temporal dimension not inherent in the pure copula. The commentator also discusses Persian, which uses particles like 'hast' and 'bud' or a vowel ending like kasra. Sharif counters that Persian can have propositions without a copula, like 'Zayd is an astronomer and an astrologer'. This passage clarifies the nature and linguistic expression of the copula, a crucial element in categorical propositions.

Temporal and Non-Temporal Copula Across Languages
Author discusses temporal/non-temporal copula and language differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish temporal from non-temporal copulas and describe how Arabic, Greek, and Persian handle the copula.

Core Idea
The copula can carry time or not; languages have different rules.

The passage explores the nature of the copula beyond the bipartite/tripartite division. It introduces temporal copulas like 'kāna' that add a time element, and non-temporal ones that are time-neutral. The author also notes cross-linguistic differences: Arabic allows omission, Greek mandates a temporal copula, and Persian uses specific words or vowel endings. A marginal note from Sharif counters the claim about Persian by providing a counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Understand temporal copula
A temporal copula (zamāniyya) indicates time, e.g., 'kāna' in 'Zayd kāna qā'iman' (Zayd was standing). It combines the linking function with a time reference.
The author says 'kāna' is called temporal because it indicates time.
2
Understand non-temporal copula
A non-temporal copula (ghayr zamāniyya) does not indicate time; it is a pure link. Examples include the implied copula in a bipartite proposition or the vowel ending (ḥaraka al-rafʿ).
The author mentions that the vowel ending can serve as a non-temporal copula.
3
Compare Arabic and Greek usage
Arabic sometimes uses the copula and sometimes omits it, relying on context. Greek, however, requires a temporal copula as a state between subject and predicate, making it an instrument rather than an independent meaning.
Sharif notes the contrast: in Greek the copula is a tool, not an independent term.
4
Examine Persian usage and counterexample
Persian typically uses copula words like 'hast' (present) or 'bud' (past), or a vowel ending like kasra. However, Sharif gives the example 'Zayd dabīr ast wa munajjim' where 'wa munajjim' is a proposition without copula, refuting the absolute claim.
This shows that even in Persian the copula can be omitted in certain constructions.

The Debate: Is 'Huwa' a Copula? The Role of Vowel Endings
Author discusses debate about 'huwa' and vowel ending as copula

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the arguments for and against considering 'huwa' as a copula, and identify the vowel ending as an alternative copula in Arabic.

Core Idea
The copula in Arabic may be the vowel ending, not the pronoun 'huwa'.

This passage includes a significant debate about what constitutes the copula in Arabic. Some suggest that words like 'huwa' (he) serve as copula, but the commentator Sharif objects, arguing that 'huwa' is a pronoun referring to the subject, and the true copula is the vowel ending (ḥaraka al-rafʿ). This discussion highlights the linguistic sophistication of Muslim logicians.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the common claim
Some logicians consider 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa ʿālim' (Zayd, he is knowledgeable) as the copula.
2
Understand Sharif's objection
Sharif objects: 'huwa' is a pronoun that refers to Zayd, so it is not a mere copula; it adds reference.
He says 'huwa' indicates Zayd, so it cannot be the copula.
3
Learn the alternative proposal
The copula is actually the vowel ending (ḥaraka al-rafʿ), which indicates connection and predication.
Evidence: if words are spoken with stopped vowels, no predication occurs.
4
Evaluate the evidence
The vowel ending is essential for composition; without it, the words remain unconnected.
Sharif argues that the vowel ending is the true copula.
5
Consider the case of 'kāna'
The discussion also notes that 'kāna' adds temporal meaning beyond the copula, so it is not a pure copula either.
This further refines the concept of copula.

والقضية الحملية باعتبار الرابطة إما ثنائية أو ثلاثية، لأنها إن ذكرت فيها الرابطة كانت ثلاثية لاشتمالها على ثلاثة ألفاظ لثلاثة معان، وإن حذفت - لشعور الذهن بمعناها - كانت ثنائية لعدم اشتمالها إلا على جزأين بإزاء معنيين.
وقوله : «وقد تحذف في بعض اللغات» إشارة إلى أن اللغات مختلفة في استعمال الرابطة ، فإن لغة العرب ربما تستعمل الرابطة وربما تحذفها بشهادة القرائن الدالّة عليها، ولغة اليونان توجب الرابطة الزمانية حالة بينهما وآلة لتعرّف حالهما، فلاتكون معنى مستقلا يصلح لأن يكون محكوماً عليه أوبه، فاللفظ الدال عليها يكون أداة (شريف). (۱) قد يناقش في ذلك بأن لفظ «هو» في «زيد هو عالم» يدلّ على زيد لأنه ضمير راجع إليه فلايكون رابطة، ويقال الرابطة في هذه القضية هي حركة الرفع لأنها دالة على الارتباط والاستناد، والدليل عليه أن المفردات إذا ذكرت موقوفة الأواخر نحو «زيد» لم يحصل التركيب ولا يفيد الاسناد، وقد تكون في قالب الكلمة ككان الناقصة وما يتصرّف منها، وتسمّى زمانية لدلالتها على الزمان، بخلاف لفظ هو وأخواتها، إذ لا دلالة لها على الزمان أصلاً. وقد نوقش هاهنا أيضاً بأن مدلول «كان» زائد على مدلول الرابطة لدلالة «كان» على الزمان الذي لا مدخل له في الربط (ن : الرابط) (شريف). ۲) قيل : وجه الضبط أن يقال : هاهنا ثلاثة أشياء : «الوجوب والامتناع والجواز » فتضربها في ثلاثة أخرى هي مجموع الرابطتين معاً والرابطة الزمانية وحدها، وغير الزمانية وحدها - وفيه بعد لا يخفى (شريف).
دون غيرها - على ما نقله الشيخ - ولغة العجم لاتستعمل القضية خالية عنها - إما بلفظ كقولهم «هست» و «بود» - وإما بحركة - كقولهم «زيد دبير» بالكسر.
(۲) نقض ذلك بمثل قولهم : زيد دبير است و منجم» فإن قولهم «ومنجم» قضيّة خالية عن الرابطة (شريف).
The [mentioned] - and it is called non-temporal, and it may be in the form of a word - like 'kana' in our saying 'Zayd was standing' and it is called temporal. The categorical proposition, with respect to the copula, is either bipartite or tripartite, because if the copula is mentioned in it, it is tripartite due to its inclusion of three words for three meanings, and if it is omitted - due to the mind's awareness of its meaning - it is bipartite because it only includes two parts corresponding to two meanings. And his statement: 'And it is sometimes omitted in some languages' is an indication that languages differ in the use of the copula. For the Arabic language sometimes uses the copula and sometimes omits it, as evidenced by the indications pointing to it, and the Greek language requires the temporal copula as a state between them and an instrument for knowing their state, so it is not an independent meaning suitable to be judged upon or by it, so the word indicating it is a tool (Sharif). (1) It may be objected that the word 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa ʿalim' indicates Zayd because it is a pronoun referring back to him, so it is not a copula; and it is said the copula in this proposition is the vowel ending of the nominative case because it indicates connection and predication; the evidence is that if the individual words are mentioned with final vowels stopped, like 'Zayd', composition does not occur and predication is not useful. And it may be in the form of a word like the defective 'kana' and its derivatives, and it is called temporal because it indicates time, unlike the word 'huwa' and its sisters, since they have no indication of time at all. There is also discussion here that the meaning of 'kana' is additional to the meaning of the copula because 'kana' indicates time which has no entry in the connection (n: al-rabiṭ) (Sharif). (2) It is said: The way to adjust is to say: Here are three things: 'necessity, impossibility, and possibility'; then you multiply them by three others: the two copulas together, the temporal copula alone, and the non-temporal copula alone - and in it there is an apparent remoteness (Sharif). Without others - according to what the Shaykh transmitted - and the language of the non-Arabs does not use the proposition free from it - either with a word like their saying 'hast' and 'bud' - or with a vowel motion - like their saying 'Zayd dabīr' with a kasra. (2) That is refuted by example like their saying 'Zayd dabīr ast wa munajjim', for their saying 'wa munajjim' is a proposition free from the copula (Sharif).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the division of categorical propositions based on the copula (al-rabiṭa). The author first distinguishes between temporal and non-temporal copulas, giving 'kana' as an example of a temporal copula in 'Zayd was standing'. Then he explains that propositions are bipartite (thunā'iyya) when the copula is omitted and tripartite (thulāthiyya) when it is mentioned. The mind understands the copula even when omitted. He then discusses linguistic variation: Arabic often omits the copula due to contextual clues, while Greek requires a temporal copula. The marginal note from Sharif critiques the idea that 'huwa' in 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is a copula, arguing that it is a pronoun referring to Zayd, and the actual copula in Arabic is the vowel ending (ḥaraka al-rafʿ). Another note argues that 'kana' adds a temporal dimension not inherent in the pure copula. The commentator also discusses Persian, which uses particles like 'hast' and 'bud' or a vowel ending like kasra. Sharif counters that Persian can have propositions without a copula, like 'Zayd is an astronomer and an astrologer'. This passage clarifies the nature and linguistic expression of the copula, a crucial element in categorical propositions.

Propositions

Defining Affirmative and Negative Propositions by the Judgmental Relation
Defines affirmative/negative by judgmental relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is affirmative or negative based on the nature of the judgmental relation between subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Affirmation and negation depend on whether the relation validates 'is' or 'is not'.

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental division of categorical propositions based on the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya). This relation is what the copula expresses, and it determines whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. You will learn to apply this criterion to any proposition from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Determine the two terms: the subject (mawḍūʿ) is the entity being spoken about, and the predicate (maḥmūl) is what is attributed to or denied of the subject.
2
Examine the judgmental relation
Consider the relation between subject and predicate. Is it a positive relation (nisba thubūtiyya) that affirms the predicate of the subject, or a negative relation (nisba salbiyya) that denies it? This is not about truth but about the form of the proposition.
3
Apply the rule from the passage
If the relation validates saying 'the subject is the predicate', the proposition is affirmative (mūjiba). If it validates saying 'the subject is not the predicate', it is negative (sāliba).
4
Test with Islamic science examples
For example: 'The Qurʼān is the word of Allah' – the relation between Qurʼān and 'word of Allah' is positive, so affirmative. 'The Qurʼān is not created' – the relation is negative, so negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٣ - القضية الموجبة والسالبة]
قال : وهذه النسبة إن كانت نسبة بها يصح أن يقال: «إنّ الموضوع محمول فالقضيّة موجبة كقولنا الإنسان حيوان»، وإن كانت نسبة بها صح أن يقال: «إنّ الموضوع ليس بمحمول» فالقضية سالبة، كقولنا: «الإنسان ليس بحجر».
أقول : هذا تقسيم ثانٍ للحملية باعتبار النسبة الحكمية، التي هي مدلول الرابطة.
فتلك النسبة إن كانت نسبة بها يصح أن يقال : «الموضوع محمول» كانت القضية موجبة كنسبة الحيوان إلى الإنسان، فإنها نسبة ثبوتية مصححة لأن يقال «الإنسان حيوان».
وإن كانت نسبة بها يصح أن يقال : «الموضوع ليس بمحمول فالقضية سالبة كنسبة الحجر إلى الإنسان فإنها نسبة سلبية بها يصح أن يقال :
(۱) راجع الشفاء : العبارة، ۳۷-۳۹، الفصل السادس من المقالة الأولى من الفن الثالث . و ۷۷ ، الفصل الأول من المقالة الثانية من الفن الثالث.
[43 - The affirmative and negative proposition] He said: This relation if it is a relation by which it is correct to say that 'the subject is the predicate', then the proposition is affirmative, like our saying 'Man is an animal'; and if it is a relation by which it is correct to say that 'the subject is not the predicate', then the proposition is negative, like our saying 'Man is not a stone'. I say: This is a second division of the categorical proposition with respect to the judgmental relation, which is the meaning of the copula. So that relation if it is a relation by which it is correct to say 'the subject is the predicate', then the proposition is affirmative, like the relation of animal to man, for it is a positive relation validating the saying 'Man is an animal'. And if it is a relation by which it is correct to say 'the subject is not the predicate', then the proposition is negative, like the relation of stone to man, for it is a negative relation by which it is correct to say 'Man is not a stone'. (1) Refer to al-Shifa': al-ʿIbara, 37-39, chapter six of the first article of the third art; and 77, chapter one of the second article of the third art.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines affirmative and negative categorical propositions. The author first quotes the matn: a proposition is affirmative if the relation between subject and predicate allows saying 'the subject is the predicate', and negative if it allows saying 'the subject is not the predicate'. Examples: 'Man is an animal' (affirmative) and 'Man is not a stone' (negative). Then the commentator ( 'أقول' ) explains that this is a second division of categorical propositions based on the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya), which is the meaning expressed by the copula. The judgmental relation is either positive (thubūtiyya), as between animal and man, or negative (salbiyya), as between stone and man. The positive relation makes the proposition affirmative, the negative makes it negative. The footnote references al-Shifa by Avicenna for further reading. This division is fundamental in logic because it determines the quality of a proposition, which is essential for syllogistic reasoning.

Key Takeaway

Propositions

Singular and Quantified Categorical Propositions
Author divides categorical propositions by subject type: singular vs. quantified, with quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between singular and quantified categorical propositions and identify the quantifiers for universal affirmative, universal negative, and particular affirmative propositions.

Core Idea
The quantifier (sawr) reveals the scope of the subject term in a categorical proposition.

Categorical propositions are the building blocks of logical reasoning. Before we can evaluate arguments, we must understand the different types of propositions. This lesson, based on the classical text of al-Qutbi, teaches you how to classify a proposition by its subject and quantifier. You will learn to recognize singular propositions (e.g., 'Zaid is standing') and quantified propositions with their specific quantifiers, which are essential for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject type
Look at the subject of the proposition. If it is a specific individual (e.g., 'Zaid', 'this fire'), it is a singular proposition (shakhsiyya). If it is a universal term (e.g., 'fire', 'human'), proceed to the next step.
2
Check for quantifier
For a universal subject, see if a word indicating quantity (quantifier) is present. The quantifier tells you how many individuals the judgment applies to. If absent, the proposition is indefinite (not discussed here).
Quantifiers are words like 'every', 'some', 'no'.
3
Classify the quantifier type
If the quantifier is 'every' (kull) or 'nothing/no one' (lā shay'/lā wāḥid), the proposition is universal. If it is 'some' (baʿḍ), it is particular. Determine whether it is affirmative or negative based on the copula.
Universal affirmative: 'Every fire is hot'; universal negative: 'No one of people is inanimate'; particular affirmative: 'Some humans are believers'.
4
Name the proposition
Combine the quantity and quality: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. The passage lists three examples; the fourth (particular negative) follows the pattern with quantifier 'some' and a negative copula, e.g., 'Some animals are not humans'.
The passage is incomplete but the fourth type is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وموضوع الحمليّة إن كان شخصاً معيناً سميت «مخصوصة» و «شخصية». وإن كان كليا: فإن بين فيها كمية أفراد ماصدق عليه الحكم – ويسمّى اللفظ الدال عليها «سورا» - سميت «محصورة» و «مسورة»؛ وهي أربع لاله إن بين فيها أن الحكم على كل الأفراد، فهي «الكلية» وهي إمـا موجبة وسورها «كل» كقولنا كلّ نار حارة» وإما سالبة وسورها لاشيء» و «لا واحد» كقولنا : «لاشيء - أو لاواحد - من الناس بجماد». وإن بين فيها أن الحكم على بعض الأفراد فهي «الجزئية» وهي إما موجبة
He said: The subject of the categorical proposition, if it is a specific individual, it is called 'particular' (makhsūsa) and 'singular' (shakhsiyya). And if it is universal: then if the quantity of the individuals to which the judgment applies is indicated in it – and the word indicating that is called a 'quantifier' (sawr) – it is called 'restricted' (mahṣūra) and 'quantified' (musawwara); and it is of four types: if it is indicated that the judgment applies to every individual, then it is 'universal' (kulliyya), and it is either affirmative, its quantifier being 'every' (kull) like our statement 'Every fire is hot', or negative, its quantifier being 'nothing' (lā shay') and 'no one' (lā wāḥid) like our statement 'Nothing – or no one – of people is inanimate'. And if it is indicated that the judgment applies to some individuals, then it is 'particular' (juz'iyya), and it is either affirmative...
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines two types of categorical propositions based on the nature of the subject term. If the subject is a specific individual (like 'Zaid'), the proposition is called 'makhsūsa' or 'shakhsiyya' (singular). If the subject is a universal term, the proposition may be quantified (musawwara) by indicating the quantity of individuals covered by the judgment. The word indicating this quantity is called a 'sawr' (quantifier). The quantified propositions are divided into four types: universal affirmative (with quantifier 'kull'), universal negative (with quantifiers 'lā shay' or 'lā wāḥid'), particular affirmative (quantifier 'some'), and particular negative. The passage gives examples for the first three and begins the fourth. The commentator emphasizes that the quantifier specifies the scope of the subject term relative to the judgment.

Personal vs Quantified Propositions
Defines personal and quantified propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a personal proposition from a quantified proposition and identify the quantifier in a quantified proposition.

Core Idea
A proposition is personal if its subject is a single individual; quantified if its universal subject is marked by a quantifier.

Categorical propositions can be divided based on their subject: if the subject is a specific individual like Zayd, the proposition is personal (shakhṣiyya). If the subject is a universal term and the quantity of individuals is specified by a quantifier (sūr), it is quantified (maḥṣūra). This distinction is foundational for understanding how judgments apply to individuals versus classes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject term
Look at the subject of the proposition. Is it a proper noun like 'Zayd' or a common noun like 'human'? If it's a proper noun, it's likely personal.
In Arabic, personal propositions often use names (Zayd, ʿAmr) or definite descriptions referring to a specific entity.
2
Check for a quantifier word
If the subject is a common noun, look for words like 'every' (kull), 'no' (lā shay'), 'some' (baʿḍ), etc. These are quantifiers that indicate the quantity of the subject.
The quantifier is called 'sūr' in Arabic, derived from 'city wall' because it encloses the scope of the judgment.
3
Determine if quantified or personal
If a quantifier is present, the proposition is quantified (maḥṣūra). If the subject is a specific individual (even without a quantifier), it is personal (shakhṣiyya).
A personal proposition like 'Zayd is standing' has no quantifier because the subject itself is unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : وموضوع الحمليّة إن كان شخصاً معيناً سميت «مخصوصة» و «شخصية».
وإن كان كليا: فإن بين فيها كمية أفراد ماصدق عليه الحكم – ويسمّى اللفظ الدال عليها «سورا» - سميت «محصورة» و «مسورة»؛ وهي أربع لاله إن بين فيها أن الحكم على كل الأفراد، فهي «الكلية» وهي إمـا موجبة وسورها «كل» كقولنا كلّ نار حارة» وإما سالبة وسورها لاشيء» و «لا واحد» كقولنا : «لاشيء - أو لاواحد - من الناس بجماد». وإن بين فيها أن الحكم على بعض الأفراد فهي «الجزئية» وهي إما موجبة
(۱) قيل عليه، إنما لا يشملها إذا حملت الصحة على ما هو في نفس الأمر، وأما إذا حملت على ما هو أعم من الصحة بحسب نفس الأمر، وتما هو بحسب زعم القائل، فيشملها قطعاً، وأنت تعلم أن المتبادر من عبارة المصنف هو الصحة في نفس الأمر، و التعريفات يجب حملها على معانيها المتبادرة منها (شريف).

وسورها «بعض» أو «واحد» كقولنا بعض الحيوان أو واحد من الحيوان - إنسان»، وإما سالبة وسورها ليس كلّ و ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس» كقولنا: ليس كلّ حيوان إنساناً» و «ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان» و «بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان».
القول : هذا تقسيم ثالث للحملية باعتبار الموضوع :
فموضوع الحملية إما أن يكون جزئياً أو كلياً :
فإن كان جزئيّاً : سميت القضية «شخصيّة» و «مخصوصة»، إما موجبة
كقولنا : «زيد إنسان وإما ،سالبة، كقولنا : «زيد ليس بحجر». أما تسميتها «شخصية» فلأن موضوعها شخص معين، وأما تسميتها «مخصوصة» فلخصوص موضوعها؛ ولما كان هذا التقسيم باعتبار
الموضوع لوحظ في أسامي الأقسام حال الموضوع.
وإن كان كلّيّاً : فإما أن يبين فيها كميّة أفراد الموضوع - من الكلية والبعضية - أو لا يبين.
واللفظ الدال عليها - أي على كمية الأفراد – يسمّى «سوراً» أخذاً من «سور البلد» كما أنه يحصر البلد ويحيط به، كذلك اللفظ الدال على كمية الأفراد يحصرها ويحيط بها.
فإن بين فيها كمية أفراد الموضوع سميت القضية «محصورة» و «مسورة»، أما أنها محصورة فلحصر أفراد موضوعها، وأما أنها مسوّرة
فلاشتمالها على السور.
وهي - أي المحصورة - أربعة أقسام : لأن الحكم فيها إما على كلّ
He said: The subject of a categorical proposition, if it is a specific individual, is called 'particular' (makhsūṣa) and 'personal' (shakhṣiyya). And if it is universal: then if the quantity of individuals to which the judgment applies is specified – and the word indicating it is called a 'quantifier' (sūr) – it is called 'restricted' (maḥṣūra) and 'quantified' (musawwara); and there are four [types]? If it is specified that the judgment applies to all individuals, then it is 'universal' (kulliyya), and it is either affirmative with its quantifier 'every' (kull) as in our saying 'Every fire is hot', or negative with its quantifier 'nothing' (lā shay') and 'no one' (lā wāḥid) as in our saying 'Nothing – or no one – among humans is an inanimate object'. And if it is specified that the judgment applies to some individuals, then it is 'particular' (juz’iyya), and it is either affirmative with its quantifier 'some' (ba‘ḍ) or 'one' (wāḥid) as in our saying 'Some animal – or one animal – is human', or negative with its quantifier 'not every' (laysa kull), 'not some' (laysa ba‘ḍ), and 'some not' (ba‘ḍ laysa) as in our saying 'Not every animal is human', 'Not some animal is human', and 'Some animal is not human'.
The commentary: This is a third division of the categorical [proposition] with respect to the subject: The subject of a categorical proposition is either particular or universal. If it is particular: the proposition is called 'personal' (shakhṣiyya) and 'particular' (makhṣūṣa), either affirmative as in our saying 'Zayd is human' or negative as in our saying 'Zayd is not a stone'. As for its being called 'personal', it is because its subject is a specific individual; as for its being called 'particular', it is due to the particularity of its subject. Since this division is with respect to the subject, the state of the subject is observed in the names of the divisions. And if it is universal: then either the quantity of individuals of the subject – universality or partiality – is specified in it, or it is not specified. And the word indicating it – i.e., the quantity of individuals – is called a 'quantifier' (sūr), derived from the 'wall of a city' (sūr al-balad), just as it confines and surrounds the city, likewise the word indicating the quantity of individuals confines and surrounds them. Then if the quantity of individuals of the subject is specified in it, the proposition is called 'restricted' (maḥṣūra) and 'quantified' (musawwara). As for its being restricted, it is due to the restriction of the individuals of its subject; as for its being quantified, it is due to its inclusion of the quantifier. And it – i.e., the restricted [proposition] – is four divisions: because the judgment in it is either about every...
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of categorical propositions based on the nature of their subject and the specification of quantity. The author first presents the primary division: if the subject is a specific individual (like Zayd or a particular person), the proposition is called 'personal' (shakhṣiyya) or 'particular' (makhṣūṣa). If the subject is a universal term (like 'human' or 'animal'), then we look at whether the quantity of individuals covered by the judgment is made explicit. The word that indicates this quantity is called a 'quantifier' (sūr), analogous to a city wall that encloses and delimits the city. When the quantity is stated, the proposition is termed 'restricted' (maḥṣūra) or 'quantified' (musawwara). The quantified propositions are of four types: universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every fire is hot'), universal negative (e.g., 'No human is inanimate'), particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some animal is human'), and particular negative (e.g., 'Not every animal is human' or 'Some animal is not human'). The commentary elaborates on the reasoning behind the names, emphasizing that the state of the subject determines the classification. It also clarifies that if the subject is universal but the quantity is not specified, the proposition is not quantified. The passage thus systematically organizes categorical propositions into personal and quantified, and within quantified into the four standard Aristotelian types. Note that the footnote (marked with (1)) is a gloss questioning the scope of a previous definition of 'sound judgment' and is not central to the present classification.

The Four Quantified Propositions
Lists and exemplifies the four quantified proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four quantified categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and provide examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Quantified propositions come in four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative.

Once a proposition is identified as quantified, we further classify it by quantity (universal or particular) and quality (affirmative or negative). This yields the four standard types: every S is P (universal affirmative), no S is P (universal negative), some S is P (particular affirmative), some S is not P (particular negative). Understanding these types is essential for syllogistic logic.

Guided Path
1
Determine the quantifier word
Identify the quantifier: 'every' (kull) for universal affirmative, 'no' (lā shay') for universal negative, 'some' (baʿḍ) for particular affirmative, 'not every' (laysa kull) or 'some not' (baʿḍ laysa) for particular negative.
Universal affirmative quantifiers: every, all, each. Universal negative: no, none. Particular affirmative: some, a certain. Particular negative: not every, some...not.
2
Check the quality
Is the judgment affirmative (the predicate belongs to the subject) or negative (the predicate does not belong)? Affirmative: 'every S is P'; negative: 'no S is P' or 'some S is not P'.
The quantifier often implies the quality: 'every' is affirmative, 'no' is negative, etc.
3
Categorize into one of the four types
Combine quantity and quality: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O).
This classification is standard in Aristotelian logic and is used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

واللفظ الدال عليها - أي على كمية الأفراد – يسمّى «سوراً» أخذاً من «سور البلد» كما أنه يحصر البلد ويحيط به، كذلك اللفظ الدال على كمية الأفراد يحصرها ويحيط بها.

The Concept of Quantifier (Sūr)
Etymology and function of the quantifier (sūr).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the function of a quantifier (sūr) in a categorical proposition and relate its etymology to its logical role.

Core Idea
The quantifier (sūr) confines the scope of a judgment just as a city wall confines a city.

The Arabic term 'sūr' (quantifier) is metaphorically derived from the wall that encloses a city (sūr al-balad). Just as the wall determines what is inside and outside, the quantifier specifies how many individuals of the subject are included in the judgment. Understanding this metaphor helps grasp the purpose of quantification: to delimit the range of the judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the literal meaning of 'sūr'
The word 'sūr' originally means 'city wall', a physical barrier that encloses and protects the city. It defines the boundary of the city.
In Arabic, the same word is used for a wall and for a logical quantifier.
2
Apply the metaphor to propositions
Just as a city wall confines the city, a logical quantifier confines the individuals of the subject that the judgment covers. It sets a boundary on which individuals are included (or excluded).
For example, 'every' includes all individuals; 'some' includes only a part.
3
Identify the quantifier in an Arabic proposition
Look for words like 'kull', 'baʿḍ', 'lā shay'' etc. These are the 'walls' that delimit the subject's scope.
In a personal proposition, there is no quantifier because the subject itself is already a single individual.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
Personal and Quantified Propositions
Author defines personal propositions and introduces quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a categorical proposition as personal (shakhsiyya/makhṣūṣa) or quantified (maḥṣūra) based on its subject and presence of a quantifier.

Core Idea
A proposition's subject determines whether it is personal (specific individual) or quantified (scope defined by quantifier).

In the third division of categorical propositions, we examine the subject: is it a specific individual or a general class? This distinction leads to personal propositions (about individuals) and quantified propositions (where a quantifier specifies the scope). Understanding this helps you read logical statements with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject type
Look at the subject of the proposition. Is it a particular individual (e.g., Zayd) or a universal term (e.g., animal)? If it is a specific person or thing, the proposition is personal (shakhsiyya/makhṣūṣa).
Example: 'Zayd is human' has a particular subject.
2
Check for a quantifier
If the subject is universal, see if the proposition includes a word indicating quantity (e.g., 'every', 'some', 'nothing'). This word is called the quantifier (sūr). If present, the proposition is quantified (maḥṣūra/musawwara).
Derived from 'sūr' meaning wall, as it encloses the scope.
3
Classify the proposition
Decide: if subject is particular -> personal; if subject is universal and quantifier present -> quantified; if universal but no quantifier -> non-quantified (muhmala).
This passage focuses on personal and quantified, not muhmala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : هذا تقسيم ثالث للحملية باعتبار الموضوع : فموضوع الحملية إما أن يكون جزئياً أو كلياً : فإن كان جزئيّاً : سميت القضية «شخصيّة» و «مخصوصة»، إما موجبة كقولنا : «زيد إنسان» وإما سالبة، كقولنا : «زيد ليس بحجر». أما تسميتها «شخصية» فلأن موضوعها شخص معين، وأما تسميتها «مخصوصة» فلخصوص موضوعها؛ ولما كان هذا التقسيم باعتبار الموضوع لوحظ في أسامي الأقسام حال الموضوع. وإن كان كلّيّاً : فإما أن يبين فيها كميّة أفراد الموضوع - من الكلية والبعضية - أو لا يبين. واللفظ الدال عليها - أي على كمية الأفراد – يسمّى «سوراً» أخذاً من «سور البلد» كما أنه يحصر البلد ويحيط به، كذلك اللفظ الدال على كمية الأفراد يحصرها ويحيط بها. فإن بين فيها كمية أفراد الموضوع سميت القضية «محصورة» و «مسورة»، أما أنها محصورة فلحصر أفراد موضوعها، وأما أنها مسوّرة فلاشتمالها على السور. وهي - أي المحصورة - أربعة أقسام : لأن الحكم فيها إما على كل الأفراد أو على بعضها، وأيا ما كان : فإما بالإيجاب أو بالسلب. فإن كان الحكم فيها على كل الأفراد فهي «كلية»: إما موجبة وسورها «كل» - أي كل واحد واحد - لا الكل المجموعي – كقولنا : «كلّ نار حارة» أي كل واحدة من أفراد النار حارة. وإما سالبة، وسورها «لاشيء» و«لا واحد» كقولنا : لاشيء - أولا واحد من الناس بجماد». وإن كان الحكم فيها على بعض الأفراد فهي «جزئية» : إما موجبة وسورها «بعض» و «واحد» كقولنا : بعض الحيوان - أو واحد من الحيوان - إنسان أي بعض أفراد الحيوان - أو واحد من أفراده - إنسان». وإما سالبة وسورها ليس كلّ و ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس» كقولنا : ليس كل حيوان إنساناً» و «ليس بعض الحيوان إنساناً» و بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان». والفرق بين الأسوار الثلاثة أن ليس كل دال على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالمطابقة وعلى السلب الجزئي بالالتزام، و«ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس بالعكس من ذلك. أما أن «ليس كل» دال على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالمطابقة : فلانا إذا قلنا : «كلّ حيوان إنسان يكون معناه ثبوت الإنسان لكل واحد واحد من أفراد الحيوان - وهو الإيجاب الكلّي - وإذا قلنا : «ليس كل حيوان إنساناً يكون مفهومه الصريح أنه ليس يثبت الإنسان لكل واحد واحد من أفراد الحيوان، وهو رفع الإيجاب الكلي.
Statement: This is a third division of the categorical [proposition] with respect to the subject. For the subject of the categorical is either particular or universal. If it is particular, the proposition is called 'personal' and 'particularized' — either affirmative, as in 'Zayd is human', or negative, as in 'Zayd is not a stone'. It is called 'personal' because its subject is a specific individual, and 'particularized' because of the particularity of its subject. Since this division is based on the subject, the state of the subject is observed in the names of the divisions. And if it is universal, then either the quantity of the individuals of the subject — from universality and partiality — is indicated in it or not. The word indicating them — i.e., the quantity of individuals — is called 'quantifier' (sūr), derived from 'the wall of a city', just as it encloses and surrounds the city, likewise the word indicating the quantity of individuals encloses and surrounds them. If the quantity of individuals of the subject is indicated in it, the proposition is called 'enclosed' (maḥṣūra) and 'quantified' (musawwara). It is called 'enclosed' because of the enclosure of the individuals of its subject, and 'quantified' because it includes the quantifier. And it — i.e., the enclosed — has four divisions, because the judgment in it is either upon all individuals or upon some of them, and whatever it is, it is either by affirmation or negation. If the judgment in it is upon all individuals, then it is 'universal': either affirmative, whose quantifier is 'every' — i.e., each and every one, not the collective whole — as in our saying 'Every fire is hot', i.e., every single individual of fire is hot; or negative, whose quantifier is 'nothing' and 'no one', as in our saying 'Nothing — or no one — from people is an inanimate object'. And if the judgment in it is upon some individuals, then it is 'particular': either affirmative, whose quantifier is 'some' and 'one', as in our saying 'Some animal — or one of the animals — is human', i.e., some individuals of animal — or one of its individuals — is human; or negative, whose quantifier is 'not every' and 'not some' and 'some not', as in our saying 'Not every animal is human', and 'Not some animal is human', and 'Some animal is not human'. And the difference between the three quantifiers is that 'not every' indicates the negation of the universal affirmative by direct implication (bi-l-muṭābaqa) and the particular negative by implicature (bi-l-iltizām), while 'not some' and 'some not' are the opposite of that. As for 'not every' indicating the negation of the universal affirmative by direct implication: because if we say 'Every animal is human', its meaning is the establishment of humanity for every single individual of animal — and that is the universal affirmative; and when we say 'Not every animal is human', its explicit concept is that humanity is not established for every single individual of animal, and that is the negation of the universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the third division of categorical propositions (al-ḥamliyya) based on the subject. The author explains that the subject of a categorical proposition is either particular (juz'ī) or universal (kullī). If the subject is particular, the proposition is called 'shakhṣiyya' (personal) because its subject is a specific individual, and 'makḥṣūṣa' (particularized) due to the specificity of its subject. The author gives examples: 'Zayd is human' (affirmative) and 'Zayd is not a stone' (negative). If the subject is universal, the proposition may or may not indicate the quantity of individuals (i.e., whether the judgment applies to all or some). The word that indicates the quantity of individuals is called 'sūr' (quantifier), metaphorically taken from the wall (sūr) of a city, as it encompasses and defines the scope of the subject. When the quantity is indicated, the proposition is called 'maḥṣūra' (enclosed) or 'musawwara' (quantified). The quantified propositions are four types: universal affirmative (quantifier 'kull' meaning each and every individual, not the collective whole), universal negative (quantifiers 'lā shay'' or 'lā wāḥid'), particular affirmative (quantifiers 'baʿḍ' or 'wāḥid'), and particular negative (quantifiers 'laysa kull', 'laysa baʿḍ', 'baʿḍ laysa'). The author then distinguishes between the three quantifiers of particular negative: 'laysa kull' directly negates the universal affirmative (i.e., denies that all individuals have the predicate) and implies the particular negative (i.e., that some do not have it), while 'laysa baʿḍ' and 'baʿḍ laysa' do the opposite—they directly affirm the particular negative and imply the negation of the universal affirmative. The author illustrates with an example: 'Every animal is human' is universal affirmative; 'Not every animal is human' directly denies that all animals are human, which is the negation of the universal affirmative.

Key Terms

مخصوصة
جزئي
كلي

What is the subject of a personal (shakhsiyya) proposition?
A) A universal term
B) A particular individual
C) A quantity of individuals
D) A quantifier

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition is personal if its subject is a particular individual; it is quantified if its subject is universal and includes a quantifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each proposition as personal, quantified, or neither: (a) Zayd is standing. (b) Every human is mortal. (c) A man entered the house.

Propositions
intermediate
7 min
The Four Quantified Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Author details the four quantified types and explains the three quantifiers for particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of quantified propositions and distinguish the logical roles of 'laysa kull' and 'laysa baḍ'.

Core Idea
Quantified propositions come in four types; 'laysa kull' directly negates universal affirmative, while 'laysa baḍ' directly negates particular affirmative.

Once a proposition is quantified, we classify it by quantity (all/some) and quality (affirmative/negative). The quantifiers for particular negative are especially nuanced: 'laysa kull', 'laysa baḍ', and 'baḍ laysa' differ in what they directly negate. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the four combinations
Quantified propositions have two quantities (universal, particular) and two qualities (affirmative, negative). This yields four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
The author lists quantifiers for each type.
2
Identify the quantifiers for each type
Universal affirmative uses 'kull' (each every); universal negative uses 'lā shay'' or 'lā wāḥid'; particular affirmative uses 'baḍ' or 'wāḥid'; particular negative uses 'laysa kull', 'laysa baḍ', or 'baḍ laysa'.
Note: 'kull' means distributive, not collective.
3
Distinguish the three particular negative quantifiers
'Laysa kull' directly negates the universal affirmative (i.e., not all S are P) and implies the particular negative (some S are not P). 'Laysa baḍ' and 'baḍ laysa' directly negate the particular affirmative (i.e., some S are P is false) and imply the universal negative.
Example: 'Laysa kull hayawān insān' denies 'every animal is human'; 'laysa baḍ hayawān insān' denies 'some animal is human'.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a quantified proposition, determine which quantifier is used and what it directly negates. This clarifies the precise logical force of the statement.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is called 'enclosed' because of the enclosure of the individuals of its subject, and 'quantified' because it includes the quantifier. Propositions
beginner
6 min
The Four Types of Categorical Propositions and Their Quantifiers
Definition of universal and particular propositions with quantifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any categorical proposition as universal or particular, and affirmative or negative, based on its quantifier.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition is either universal or particular, affirmative or negative — the quantifier makes it precise.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by the scope of their judgment. Knowing the quantifiers (sawr) is essential for analysing arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam. This lesson covers the four basic types and their quantifiers as defined in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject term
Find what the proposition is about. For example, in 'every fire is hot', the subject is 'fire'.
2
Determine the judgment scope
Does the judgment apply to all individuals of the subject (universal) or only some (particular)? The quantifier indicates this.
3
Identify the quantifier
Look for words like 'kull' (every), 'la shay' (nothing), 'ba'd' (some), 'laysa kull' (not every). These define the proposition type.
4
Classify as universal/particular and affirmative/negative
Combine scope and quality: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O).
The text gives examples for each type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الحكم فيها على كل الأفراد فهي «كلية»: إما موجبة وسورها «كل» - أي كلّ واحد واحد - لا الكل المجموعي – كقولنا : «كلّ نار حارة» أي كل واحدة من أفراد النار حارة. وإما سالبة، وسورها «لاشيء» و«لا واحد» كقولنا : لاشيء - أولا واحد من الناس بجماد». وإن كان الحكم فيها على بعض الأفراد فهي «جزئية» : إما موجبة وسورها «بعض» و «واحد» كقولنا : بعض الحيوان - أو واحد من الحيوان - إنسان أي بعض أفراد الحيوان - أو واحد من أفراده - إنسان». وإما سالبة وسورها ليس كلّ و ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس» كقولنا : ليس كل حيوان إنساناً» و «ليس بعض الحيوان إنساناً» و بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان». والفرق بين الأسوار الثلاثة أن ليس كل دال على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالمطابقة وعلى السلب الجزئي بالالتزام، و«ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس بالعكس من ذلك. أما أن ليس كل» دال على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالمطابقة : فلانا إذا قلنا : «كلّ حيوان إنسان يكون معناه ثبوت الإنسان لكل واحد واحد من أفراد الحيوان - وهو الإيجاب الكلّي - وإذا قلنا : «ليس كل حيوان إنساناً يكون مفهومه الصريح أنه ليس يثبت الإنسان لكل واحد واحد من أفراد الحيوان، وهو رفع الإيجاب الكلي. وأما أنه دالّ على السلب الجزئي بالالتزام : فلأنه إذا ارتفع الإيجاب الكلّيّ، فإما أن يكون المحمول مسلوباً عن كل واحد واحد وهو السلب الكلي، أو يكون مسلوباً عن البعض ثابتاً للبعض، وعلى كلا التقديرين يصدق السلب الجزئي جزماً؛ فالسلب الجزئي من ضروريات مفهوم «ليس كل» - أي رفع الإيجاب الكلّي - ومن لوازمه، فتكون دلالته عليه بالالتزام. لا يقال : مفهوم «ليس كل وهو رفع الإيجاب الكلي - أعم من السلب عن الكل - أي السلب الكلّي - والسلب عن البعض - أي السلب الجزئي - فلايكون دالا على السلب الجزئي بالالتزام، لأن العام لادلالة له على الخاص بإحدى الدلالات الثلاث. لانا نقول : رفع الإيجاب الكلّي ليس أعم من السلب الجزئي، بل أعم من السلب عن الكل والسلب عن البعض مع الإيجاب للبعض. والسلب الجزئي هو السلب عن البعض سواء كان مع الإيجاب للبعض الآخر أو لا يكون، فهو مشترك بين ذلك القسم وبين السلب الكلي، فيكون لازماً لهما، وإذا انحصر العام في القسمين وكل منهما يكون ملزوماً لأمر، كان ذلك الأمر اللازم لازماً للعام أيضاً، فيكون السلب الجزئي لازماً لمفهوم رفع الإيجاب الكلي. و بعبارة أخرى «ليس كلّ» يلزمه السلب الجزئي، فإنه متى ارتفع الإيجاب الكلّي صدق السلب عن البعض، لأنه لولم يكن المحمول مسلوباً عن شيء من الأفراد لكان ثابتاً للكلّ، والمقدر خلافه - هذا خلف.
If the judgment is upon all individuals, then it is 'universal': either affirmative with its quantifier 'every' (kull) — meaning each single individual, not the collective whole — as our saying: 'Every fire is hot', meaning each individual fire is hot. Or negative with its quantifier 'nothing' and 'no one' as our saying: 'Nothing or no one from humans is inanimate'. And if the judgment is upon some individuals, then it is 'particular': either affirmative with its quantifier 'some' and 'one' as our saying: 'Some animal — or one from the animals — is human', meaning some individuals of animal or one of its individuals is human. Or negative with its quantifiers 'not every', 'not some', and 'some...not' as our saying: 'Not every animal is human', 'Not some animal is human', and 'Some animal is not human'. The difference between the three quantifiers is that 'not every' indicates the negation of the universal affirmative by conformity (bi-l-mutabaqa) and indicates the particular negative by implication (bi-l-iltizam), while 'not some' and 'some...not' are the reverse. As for 'not every' indicating the negation of the universal affirmative by conformity: because when we say 'Every animal is human', its meaning is the affirmation of humanness for every single individual of animal — that is the universal affirmative — and when we say 'Not every animal is human', its explicit concept is that humanness is not affirmed for every single individual of animal, which is the negation of the universal affirmative. And as for it indicating the particular negative by implication: because when the universal affirmative is negated, either the predicate is negated from every single individual — that is the universal negative — or it is negated from some and affirmed for some, and in both cases the particular negative is necessarily true; thus the particular negative is among the necessities of the concept of 'not every' — that is, the negation of the universal affirmative — and among its concomitants, so its indication of it is by implication. It cannot be said: The concept of 'not every' — which is the negation of the universal affirmative — is more general than negation from all (universal negative) and negation from some (particular negative), so it does not indicate the particular negative by implication, because the general does not indicate the specific by any of the three indications. For we say: The negation of the universal affirmative is not more general than the particular negative; rather it is more general than negation from all and negation from some together with affirmation for some. And the particular negative is negation from some whether accompanied by affirmation for the rest or not, so it is common to that division and to the universal negative, thus it is necessary for both. And when the general is confined to the two divisions, and each of them is necessary for a matter, that matter becomes necessary for the general as well, so the particular negative is necessary for the concept of negation of the universal affirmative. In other words, 'not every' entails the particular negative; for whenever the universal affirmative is negated, negation from some is true, because if the predicate were not negated from any individual, it would be affirmed for all, and the contrary is assumed — this is a reductio ad absurdum.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines universal and particular categorical propositions and explains their quantifiers. The author begins by classifying propositions based on whether the judgment applies to all individuals (universal) or some individuals (particular). Each type is further divided into affirmative and negative, with specific quantifiers: for universal affirmative 'kull' (every), for universal negative 'la shay' (nothing) or 'la wahid' (no one); for particular affirmative 'ba'd' (some) or 'wahid' (one); for particular negative 'laysa kull' (not every), 'laysa ba'd' (not some), and 'ba'd laysa' (some...not). The author then clarifies a key difference among the three quantifiers for particular negative: 'laysa kull' primarily negates the universal affirmative (by conformity) and secondarily implies the particular negative (by implication), whereas the other two quantifiers directly express the particular negative. This distinction is important because 'laysa kull' is often misinterpreted. The author provides a detailed argument: when we say 'Not every animal is human', the explicit meaning is that it is not true that every animal is human — that is negation of the universal affirmative. From this, the particular negative necessarily follows: if not all are human, then at least one is not human. The author anticipates an objection: since the negation of the universal affirmative is more general than the particular negative (it could also be that none are human), it should not specifically imply the particular negative. The response clarifies that the particular negative is common to both possibilities (some not, or none), so it is a necessary concomitant of the negation of the universal affirmative. The passage concludes with a reductio argument: if the predicate were not negated from any individual, it would be affirmed for all, contradicting the negation of the universal affirmative.

Key Takeaway
Universal propositions use 'every' or 'no'; particular propositions use 'some' or 'not every'; and the quantifier (sawr) distinguishes the four types.

The Two Indications of 'Not Every': Conformity and Implication
Explanation of the two indications of 'not every'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the conformity (mutabaqa) and implication (iltizam) meanings of the quantifier 'not every', and explain why it necessarily implies the particular negative.

Core Idea
'Not every' directly negates the universal affirmative and indirectly implies the particular negative.

The Arabic quantifier 'laysa kull' (not every) is subtle: it primarily negates the claim that every individual has the predicate, but from that we necessarily infer that some individuals do not. This lesson unpacks the two types of indication and the logical argument that grounds the implication.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'laysa kull' as negating 'kull'
'Laysa kull' literally means 'it is not the case that every'. For example, 'Not every animal is human' denies the universal affirmative 'Every animal is human'.
2
Identify the explicit meaning (conformity)
The explicit meaning of 'laysa kull' is the negation of the universal affirmative. This is the direct, conforming indication (dalala bi-l-mutabaqa).
3
Recognize the implied meaning (implication)
From the negation of the universal affirmative, it necessarily follows that at least one individual does not have the predicate. This is the particular negative (salb juz'i). The indication of this meaning is by implication (dalala bi-l-iltizam).
4
Understand the logical necessity
The author argues that if the universal affirmative is false, then either all are not (universal negative) or some are not (particular negative). In both cases, the particular negative is true. Hence it is a necessary concomitant.
The passage includes a reductio ad absurdum to prove this necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

إنسان». وإما سالبة وسورها ليس كلّ و ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس» كقولنا : ليس كل حيوان إنساناً» و «ليس بعض الحيوان إنساناً» و بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان». والفرق بين الأسوار الثلاثة أن ليس كل دال على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالمطابقة وعلى السلب الجزئي بالالتزام، و«ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس بالعكس من ذلك. أما أن ليس كل» دال على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالمطابقة : فلانا إذا قلنا : «كلّ حيوان إنسان يكون معناه ثبوت الإنسان لكل واحد واحد من أفراد الحيوان - هذا خلف.

The difference between the three quantifiers is that 'not every' indicates the negation of the universal affirmative by conformity (bi-l-mutabaqa) and indicates the particular negative by implication (bi-l-iltizam), while 'not some' and 'some...not' are the reverse. As for 'not every' indicating the negation of the universal affirmative by conformity: because when we say 'Every animal is human', its meaning is the affirmation of humanness for every single individual of animal — that is the universal affirmative — and when we say 'Not every animal is human', its explicit concept is that humanness is not affirmed for every single individual of animal, which is the negation of the universal affirmative. Natural Propositions: Judging the Essence
Definition of natural proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a natural proposition and explain why its judgment is about the nature, not individuals.

Core Idea
Natural propositions judge the quiddity, not the individuals.

In this lesson, we explore the natural proposition (al-qadiyya al-tabi'iyya), where the predicate is attributed to the essence of the subject. This type contrasts with indefinite propositions and plays a role in logical discussions about universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Look at the proposition and note what is being said about what.
For example, in 'animal is a genus', subject is 'animal', predicate is 'genus'.
2
Determine suitability for universal/particular truth
Ask: Can this proposition be true as a universal (all individuals) or as a particular (some individuals)? If neither, it may be natural.
'Animal is a genus' cannot be true of all animals (since not every animal is a genus) nor of some animals (some animals are not genera).
3
Check if the judgment is about the nature
Natural propositions predicate something that belongs to the essence itself, not to individual instances.
Being a genus is a property of the concept 'animal' as a general term, not of individual animals.
4
Confirm with the definition
If the proposition is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, it is natural.
The author explicitly says: 'if it is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, it is called natural'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإن لم يبين فيها كمية الأفراد فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت القضية «طبيعية»؛ كقولنا: الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع - لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة - وإن صلحت لذلك سميت «مهملة» كقولنا : «الإنسان في «خسر و الإنسان ليس في خسر». القول : ما مرّ كان إذا بين في القضيّة كمية أفراد الموضوع، وأما إذا لم يبين : فلا يخلو إما أن تصلح القضيّة لأن تصدق كلية وجزئية - بأن يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع - أو لم تصلح - بأن يكون الحكم على طبيعة الموضوع نفسها لاعلى الأفراد - : فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت «طبيعية» لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة، كقولنا : الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع (۱) زعم بعضهم أن مثل هذه القضايا تسمّى «عامة» لأن الموضوع فيها هو الطبيعة بقيد العموم، فإن «الحيوان» من حيث أنه عام موصوف بالجنسية و«الإنسان» بقيد عمومه موصوف بالنوعية ؛ ومثلوا للطبيعية بنحو قولنا : «الإنسان حيوان ناطق»، فزادوا في القضايا قسماً خامساً.
He said: And if the quantity of individuals is not specified in it, then if it is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, the proposition is called 'natural' (tabi'iyya), like our saying 'animal is a genus' and 'man is a species'—because the judgment in it is upon the nature itself. And if it is suitable for that, it is called 'indefinite' (muhmala), like our saying 'man is in loss' and 'man is not in loss'. The discourse: What has passed was when the quantity of individuals of the subject is specified in the proposition. But if it is not specified: then it is either that the proposition is suitable to be true both universally and particularly—by the judgment being upon the individuals of the subject—or not suitable—by the judgment being upon the nature of the subject itself, not upon the individuals. Then if it is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, it is called 'natural' because the judgment in it is upon the nature itself, like our saying 'animal is a genus' and 'man is a species'. (1) Some claimed that such propositions are called 'universal' ('amma) because the subject in them is the nature with the condition of generality, for 'animal' insofar as it is general is described as genus, and 'man' with its generality described as species; and they gave as an example of a natural proposition something like 'man is a rational animal', thus adding a fifth type to the propositions.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines two types of categorical propositions when the quantifier (quantity of individuals) is not explicitly stated: natural (tabi'iyya) and indefinite (muhmala). First, the author explains that if the proposition is not suitable to be true as both universal and particular, it is called natural. For example, 'animal is a genus' — this statement is only true about the nature of animal as a genus, not about each individual animal (since not every animal is a genus). Similarly, 'man is a species' is true about the nature of man, not about each individual man. The judgment in such propositions is about the essence itself, not about the individuals. Second, if the proposition can be true either as universal or as particular (i.e., it is ambiguous), it is called indefinite. Example: 'man is in loss' (from Quran, Surah al-Asr) — this could be interpreted as all men or some men are in loss. The indefinite proposition does not specify quantity, so it may be applied universally or particularly. The footnote mentions that some scholars call natural propositions 'universal' ('amma) because the subject is the nature taken with generality, and they add a fifth type of proposition (like 'man is a rational animal') as an example of natural. However, the main text's distinction is clear: natural propositions predicate of the quiddity, while indefinite propositions predicate of individuals without specifying quantity.

قضية

Key Takeaway
A natural proposition makes a judgment about the essence itself and cannot be interpreted as referring to individuals.

Indefinite Propositions: Unspecified Quantity
Definition of indefinite proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an indefinite proposition and explain why it can be interpreted as universal or particular.

Core Idea
Indefinite propositions are ambiguous: they can be true as universal or particular.

This lesson covers the indefinite proposition (al-qadiyya al-muhmala), where the quantity of the subject is left unspecified. Such propositions can be true either as universal or particular statements, as illustrated by the Quranic verse 'man is in loss'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Look at the proposition and note what is being said.
In 'man is in loss', subject is 'man', predicate is 'in loss'.
2
Check if the proposition is suitable for both universal and particular truth
Ask: Can this proposition be true if we mean all men? Can it be true if we mean some men? If yes, it is indefinite.
'Man is in loss' can be true of all men (as a universal) or of some men (as a particular).
3
Confirm that the judgment is about individuals
Indefinite propositions predicate something about the individuals, not just the nature.
The judgment 'in loss' applies to individual humans, not to the essence 'humanity' itself.
4
Contrast with natural propositions
If the proposition cannot be true both ways, it is natural. Indefinite is the opposite condition.
The author explicitly says: if it is suitable for both, it is called indefinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإن لم يبين فيها كمية الأفراد فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت القضية «طبيعية»؛ كقولنا: الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع - لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة - وإن صلحت لذلك سميت «مهملة» كقولنا : «الإنسان في «خسر و الإنسان ليس في خسر». القول : ما مرّ كان إذا بين في القضيّة كمية أفراد الموضوع، وأما إذا لم يبين : فلا يخلو إما أن تصلح القضيّة لأن تصدق كلية وجزئية - بأن يكون الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع - أو لم تصلح - بأن يكون الحكم على طبيعة الموضوع نفسها لاعلى الأفراد - : فإن لم تصلح لأن تصدق كلّيّة وجزئية سميت «طبيعية» لأن الحكم فيها على نفس الطبيعة، كقولنا : الحيوان جنس» و «الإنسان نوع (۱) زعم بعضهم أن مثل هذه القضايا تسمّى «عامة» لأن الموضوع فيها هو الطبيعة بقيد العموم، فإن «الحيوان» من حيث أنه عام موصوف بالجنسية و«الإنسان» بقيد عمومه موصوف بالنوعية ؛ ومثلوا للطبيعية بنحو قولنا : «الإنسان حيوان ناطق»، فزادوا في القضايا قسماً خامساً.
He said: And if the quantity of individuals is not specified in it, then if it is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, the proposition is called 'natural' (tabi'iyya), like our saying 'animal is a genus' and 'man is a species'—because the judgment in it is upon the nature itself. And if it is suitable for that, it is called 'indefinite' (muhmala), like our saying 'man is in loss' and 'man is not in loss'. The discourse: What has passed was when the quantity of individuals of the subject is specified in the proposition. But if it is not specified: then it is either that the proposition is suitable to be true both universally and particularly—by the judgment being upon the individuals of the subject—or not suitable—by the judgment being upon the nature of the subject itself, not upon the individuals. Then if it is not suitable to be true both universally and particularly, it is called 'natural' because the judgment in it is upon the nature itself, like our saying 'animal is a genus' and 'man is a species'. (1) Some claimed that such propositions are called 'universal' ('amma) because the subject in them is the nature with the condition of generality, for 'animal' insofar as it is general is described as genus, and 'man' with its generality described as species; and they gave as an example of a natural proposition something like 'man is a rational animal', thus adding a fifth type to the propositions.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines two types of categorical propositions when the quantifier (quantity of individuals) is not explicitly stated: natural (tabi'iyya) and indefinite (muhmala). First, the author explains that if the proposition is not suitable to be true as both universal and particular, it is called natural. For example, 'animal is a genus' — this statement is only true about the nature of animal as a genus, not about each individual animal (since not every animal is a genus). Similarly, 'man is a species' is true about the nature of man, not about each individual man. The judgment in such propositions is about the essence itself, not about the individuals. Second, if the proposition can be true either as universal or as particular (i.e., it is ambiguous), it is called indefinite. Example: 'man is in loss' (from Quran, Surah al-Asr) — this could be interpreted as all men or some men are in loss. The indefinite proposition does not specify quantity, so it may be applied universally or particularly. The footnote mentions that some scholars call natural propositions 'universal' ('amma) because the subject is the nature taken with generality, and they add a fifth type of proposition (like 'man is a rational animal') as an example of natural. However, the main text's distinction is clear: natural propositions predicate of the quiddity, while indefinite propositions predicate of individuals without specifying quantity.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an indefinite proposition from the passage?
A) 'Animal is a genus'
B) 'Man is a species'
C) 'Man is in loss'
D) 'The human is rational'

Key Takeaway
An indefinite proposition leaves quantity unspecified and can be interpreted as universal or particular because the judgment is about individuals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'الصلاة خير من النوم' (prayer is better than sleep) — is it indefinite or natural? Explain using the passage's criteria.
